“Wandering Heath.”
That delectable writer, A. T. Quiller-
Couch, whom the reading public chiefly
know by his initial “Q.,” seems to have
definitely abandoned the struggle for fame.
Maybe it is marriage ; maybe century-end
weariness ; maybe his temperament indis-
poses him to
. . . crawl through life a paralytic
To win the praise of bard or critic.
But this incompetency for great efforts
seems to mark all the promising young men
of our time. Fancy a novelist nowa-
days undertaking the work which Thackeray
put into “ Esmond,” or George Eliot
into “ Romola ! ” Compare Kipling.
After one not too successful long
story, he has relapsed into literary
short-clothes —which are by no means as
gay as they used to be. “The Second
Jungle Book ” compares very unfavorably
with “ The Drums of the Fore and Aft,” or
“The Man Who Would be King.” And his
later stories —perhaps verses as -well—-seem
to lack the glow, the fervor, the inspiration
which we welcomed once. They are ingenious, *
admirably handled, interesting enough ; but
there is always lees story than workmanship.
This is true, also, of Gilbert Parker’s work.
His long stories are merely short stories spun
out ; and in spite of their delicacy and
beauty there is no power, no grip in them.
They want nothing but force in order to be
forcible—but this “ want ” is vital.
• * * *
There is J. M. Barrie, again. Have we
not had the cream of him in “My
Lady Nicotine ” and the Thrums sketches ?
“When a Man’s Single”—“The Little
Minister ” itself, with some of the most
delicious dialogue in English —these are not
the books we hoped for, the books he pro-
mised. In tiie opening chapters of his new
novel the eye catches the familiar traces of
weariness, of hard labor vainly trying to
reproduce the iridescent hues of vanished
fancy. The judgment is incautiously risked,
maybe, when the book is only just begun,
but ground for such judgment seems
already plain. And Stevenson —how keenly
he felt himself that all the art garnered and
lavished in his later years was unable to
match the bloom on his firstfruits ! And
Barry Pain, whose “ Story of a Beetle ”
struck a note akin to that of “African
Farm,” and nearly as strong —where is the
tale of triumphs which once there seemed
good reason to predict for him 1 And Olive
Schreiner herself—does not the long medita-
tion over her new book hint that she knows
it fails to reach the standard which her
beginnings set her %
**. * *
Half-a-dozen other names suggest them-
selves —brilliant in promise, woefully disap-
pointing in final performance. When one
counts-up in this fashion the permanent
results achieved by the fiction-writers at work
incur Time, one sees how great the early Vic-
torian giants really were. What vitality, what
depth, what fertility, had Scott, and Dickens,
and Thackeray, and George Eliot, to go
on producing book after book with fresh
plot, and fresh characters, and fresh scenery
which compelled admiration, often enthusiasm.
The generalisation must not be taken too
closely, but the idea is right. The authors
of to-day, like the racehorses, are sprinters,
who do their six-furlong short stories at
a marvellous pace, but never see the
end of a three-mile encounter. Meredith is
the last of the Titans. It seems the
same in France. How massive, how strong,
Hugo and Dumas look beside Maupassant
and Daudet ! Literature too may echo the
cry—Where are the builders of the Pyramids ?
* ♦ * *
This divagation seems not altogether out
of tune with “ Wandering Heath.” “ Q.’s ”
new book collects, as did “Noughts and
Crosses,” stories and sketches written for
the magazines—but there is a difference.
“ ‘ Noughts and Crosses ’ is the only book of
mine to which I attach any real value,” he is
reported to have said some time ago. Cer-
tainly, he has found nothing like those jewels
since ; and now it seems he never will. His
output is too quick and careless—though
probably meditation would not help
him. He has a book a year to his credit
since ’9l, and three or four over—readable
romances, readable sketches, readable
stories, piquant and pleasant to linger over,
good sometimes to return to, but always
io be kept a shelf below the Masters.
Perhaps another “Noughts and Crosses’
could be culled from the totality, but one is
doubtful. “The Bishop of Eucalyptus” in
ihe new volume (35., 45.) is praised by W.
L. Courtney, but it is merely an affectation
of Bret-Hartean pathos— dexterous and un-
impressive. Two really good things are
“ Wrestlers ” and “ Visitors at the Gunnel
Rock ’'—which have a touch of power to go
with “ Q.’s ” inevitable charm. The rest are
likeable and unsignificant.

Notes.
Louis Becke contributes some remini-
scences to Publisher Unwin’s gossiping
advertisement of the authors under his wing
(“Good Reading,” second series, Is. 3d.).
Of his “ stone-broke ” days Becke says :
Returning from the Islands, I could get nothing
to do for a long time, until a worthy German
gentleman at Manly Beach, near Sydney, gave me
employment at trenching and clearing scrub at
7s. 6d. per rood and <£3 per acre respectively. I
was very anxious to get back to the Islands again,
and whenever an Island trading vessel arrived in
Sydney, I would get away over to town in the
hope that I would meet some old comrade again.
(I may mention here that I did clear one acre of
scrub, and cost my employer about <£3 in tools.)
One day in Sydney I met an old friend—Ernest
Favenc, the explorer and author of “Tales of the
Austral Tropics.” He it was who first suggested
to me to write some tales of island life, and send
them to the Sydney Bulletin, and taking me
with him introduced me to the proprietors of that
paper. The result was the volume of stories
published under the title of “Byßeef and Palm.”
The stream of Stevenson small - talk,
nourished by the progress of “the Edinburgh
edition,” still murmurs in the English papers.
One learns without interest what the deceased
author ate and drank, and never ate or drank,
why he wore his hair and toes long, why he
cut his trousers short, and so on. Even
Stevenson’s admirers must weary of these
trivialities. No man should be asked to be a
valet to his hero.
“Clancy’s gone to Queensland droving
and we don’t know where he are.” Did
“ The Banjo’s ” line supply a refrain for the
song which a local music-hall manager calls
“Australia’s national anthem ” ? “ Clancy ”
was first published in The Bulletin Xmas
Number of ’B9, and a little later Francis
Adams quoted it in an English magazine
article. Hence, in all probability, fell the
germ which, flourishing in Cockney soil,
brought “ ’E dunno where ’e are ” to such
profitable fruitage.
When an Anglo-American war is talked of,
it is easy to see that the American proprietor
of an English newspaper may expect tribula-
tion. And millionaire Astor, who has
quarrelled a second time with Pall Mall
Gazette staff, insists strongly on the right
of every man to wallop his own editor.
Hence the second deluge which has swept
over Charing Cross road. Ex-editor Cook
founded the Westminster Gazette and pros-
pered ; but ex-editor Cust has no such open-
ing.
The most striking article in the New
Year’s number of the South African Review
(Gordon and Gotch, agents) deals with the
discrowned African dictator, Cecil Rhodes.
Here is a summation of Rhodes’s policy :
Mr. Rhodes’s chief present “ hobby ’’ —the word
is his own —is the political consolidation of South
Africa by the creation of a grand federated
dominion, under the British flag—somewhat on
the lines of the Canadian dependency. The Gape
Colony, lately enlarged by the annexation of
Pondoland and Southern Bechuanaland, is to form
the central as the oldest, best developed,
most thickly populated, and most accessible of the
combining territorial units. The Bechuanaland
Protectorate and Southern and Northern Rhodesia
are ripe for annexation, which may, in their case,
come about very shortly. Natal and Zululand
will inevitably find a common political denomi-
nator with the Cape in time to come, especially as
they have much to gain by combination with their
powerful neighbor. The Orange Free State
already tacitly recognises Her Majesty’s authority
in matters of external policy, and, closely con-
nected with the Cape by family and commercial
ties, should readily welcome a still closer partner-
ship arrangement. In the case of the Transvaal,
a very anomalous condition of things prevails.
The number of Boer males in the Republic
is probably about 15,000 ; the Uitlander
males may be computed at probably at
least 50,000. The machinery of the Govern-
ment is wholly in the hands of a Boer dictator in
the person of Paul Kruger, who is in turn directed
by a Hollander official named Dr. Leyds, the
Secretary of- State. Under this double regime
the Uitlander community—including practically
all the wealth and intelligence of the State—has
been misgoverned, over taxed, and generally
oppressed in the most outrageous manner. But
the handwriting is visible upon the wall. The
Transvaal may now be compared to a cone
balanced upon its apex, while the Uitlander base
is broadened by every passenger train that arrives
from the sea-boards. The cone must of necessity
topple over sooner or later, and a single word—
for which in time of crisis Johannesburg intently
listens-from Mr. Rhodes would subvert the
Transvaal constitution in 24 hours. Perhaps a
few shots might be fired, but the Boers would not
have the slightest chance against the Uitlanders
in their midst, to say nothing of the outside sup-
port—and what is important, the former are aware
of that fact. All of which is tantamount to say-
ing that the Transvaal’s future as an item in the
coining Federal Dominion is, humanly speaking,
fixed. i
The closing lines can be matched with others
in the English press to show that as early as
mid-December the Transvaal conspiracy was
whispered of in London. And this was a
friendly forecast of the issue ! Now —
Rhodes, discredited, has been thrust from
the Cape Premiership ; the Dutch party,
among his chief supporters, has turned
against him ; he has received a smart rap over
the knuckles from Salisbury ; his lieutenant
awaits a trial which ought to cost him his
life ; and he himself proposes to make amends
for the coup manque by the development of
Rhodesia on colossal lines. “ How are the
mighty fallen in the midst of tire battle ! ”
Bush Pictures.
(From Henry Lawson’s New Bool;.)
Hazell’s Annual for 1896 (4s 3d) is admir-
ably encyclopaedic concerning prominent
persons and tilings of the past year. Praise
for such a standard auxiliary to readers and
writers is superfluous. By way of criticism,
the editor may be urged to supply a crossed
and detailed index. The articles, he points
out with some air of feeling himself on his
defence, are alphabetically ranged, and carry
their index along with them.. But the pro-
portion of sub-heads per article, and sub-
references per subject, seems to grow con-
tinually larger, and through the wilderness
of these one wanders with much tribulation
in search of the promised landmark. A good
index would increase Hazell’s usefulness by
25 per cent.
Some of Percy Spence’s very best drawings
illustrate G. H. Gibson’s “ Ironbark Chips
and Stockwhip Cracks” (reduced to 3s.)—a
volume containing much excellent Gilbertian
verse with characteristic Australian color.
Ethel Turner’s ‘ ‘ Seven Little Australians, ”
and “ The Family at Misrule ” (like Louisa
Alcott’s stories), have been translated into
Dutch and published in Holland, but as
there is no copyright law between that
country and England she will benefit only
by increased fame. Miss Turner’s next book,
“ Shreds and Patches,” will collect her short
stories.
‘ ‘ Let us recognise the gifts and powers of
George Meredith, while we remain free to
acknowledge that still he is human : there-
fore, liable to have moments when his flight
is not so high, his meaning not so plain, his
message not so clear. It is, indeed, the
certain accompaniment of genius that there
are depressions as well as elevations. Homer
is not the only poet who sometimes nods.
Where the same level is maintained it must
be a low level. ” — National Observer.
The admission of arguments and articles
in favor of Protection, and of open and
covert sneers against Freetrade, into even
Tory British newspapers, shows significantly
whither public opinion is tending. Writes
G. R. Smalley in London Times :
“ The American . . . would say that
a wise legislator, while not assuming
that war is the normal condition of any
country in modern times, must nevertheless
regard''it as a condition which he is bound to
provide for, and that Protection is just as
legitimate as any other tax levied in time of
peace for maintaining an army or navy. He
would point to the United States as a country
where but for Protection there would be
manufactures of neither iron nor steel; where
but for Protection not a gun nor an armour-
clad ship could have been built. Such a
country, he would insist, would in time of
war be practically defenceless, or would be
dependent on foreign countries for the means
of defence, and would not be sure whether,
having no command of the seas, she could
import guns or ships or not.” Would the
Times ten years ago —five years ago —have
permitted its New York correspondent to
put the Protectionist case even thus cautiously
and hypothetically ?
THE DROVER’S SWEETHEART.
An hour before the sun goes down
Behind the ragged boughs,
I go across the little run
And bring the dusty cows;
And once I used to sit and rest
Beneath the fading dome,
For there was one that I loved best
Who’d bring the cattle home.
Our yard is fixed with double bails,
Round one the grass is green,
The bush is growing through the rails,
The spike is rusted in ;
And ’twas from there his freckled face
Would turn and smile at me —
He’d milk a dozen in the race
While I was milking three.
I milk eleven cows myself
Where once I milked but four,
I set the dishes on the shelf
And close the dairy door ;
And when the glaring sunlight fails
And the fire shines through the cracks,
I climb the broken stockyard rails
And watch the bridle-tracks.
He kissed me twice and once again
And rode across the hill —
The pint-pots and the hobble-chain
I hear them jingling still;
He’ll come at night or not at all,
He left in dust and heat,
And when the soft, cool shadows fall
It would be nice to meet.
And he is coming back again ;
He wrote to let me know ;
The floods were in the Darling then —
It seems so long ago ;
He’d come through miles of slush and mud,
And it was weary work ;
The creeks were bankers, and the flood
Was forty miles round Bourke.
He said the floods had formed a block,
The plains could not bo crossed,
And there was foot-rot in the flock
And hundreds had been lost;
The sheep were falling thick and fast,
A hundred miles from town,
And when he reached the line at last
He trucked the remnant down.
And so he’ll have to stand the cost,
His luck was always bad,
Instead of making more, he lost
The money that he had ;
And how he’ll manage, heaven knows !
(My eyes are getting dim),
He says—he says—he don’t—suppose
I’ll want —to —marry —him.
As if I wouldn’t take his hand
Without a golden glove ;
O ! Jack—you men won’t understand
How much a girl can love.
I long to see his face once more . . .
Jack’s dog ! thank God, it’s Jack !
(I never thought I’d faint before)
He’s coming—up—the track.
ANDY’S GONE WITH CATTLE.
WHEN THE CHILDREN COME HOME.
Our Andy’s gene to battle now
’Gainst Drought, the red marauder :
Our Andy’s gone with cattle now
Across the Queensland border.
He’s left us in dejection now ;
Our hearts with him are roving.
It’s dull on this selection now,
Since Andy went a-droving.
Who now will wear a cheerful face
In times when things are slackest?
And who will whistle round the place
When Fortune frowns her blackest?
Oh, who will cheek the squatter now
When he comes round us snarling ?
His tongue is growing hotter now
Since Andy crossed the Darling.
The gates are out of order now,
In storms the “ riders ” rattle ;
For far across the border now
Our Andy’s gone with cattle.
Poor Aunty’s looking thin and white ;
And Uncle’s cross with worry ;
And poor old Blucher howls all night
Since Andy left Macquarie.
Oh, may the showers in torrents fall,
And all the tanks run over ;
And may the grass grow green and tall
In pathways of the drover ;
And may good angels send the rain
On desert stretches sandy ;
And when the summer comes again
God grant ’twill bring us Andy.
On a lonely selection far out in the West

Bards of the Backblocks.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A BACKBLOCKS MONOLOGUE.
1 have come through the red of the sundown
To the township down here in the West;
My nerves and my system have run down—
I need a good tonic and rest.
When a man’s only comrades are cattle,
His brain seems to turn a bit queer,
So I’ve come from the station to battle
With the brandy they give you down here.
It reeks of tobacco, this brandy,
And sulphur, and similar stuff ;
But there’s no other beverage handy,
And my throat is—thank God —pretty tough.
Hi, Larry, you blessed emetic !
Why the deuce don’t you drink what you sell ?
Fill up two, you red-headed ascetic !
You’ve got to be poisoned as well.
My brain it comes back with the spirits—
By the Lord, I’ve been nearly insane !
This bush stuff has its own proper merits,
Since it gives me iny soul back again.
It’s a passport to Hell; but the Devil
Is bound to look after his own ;
And “ of goodness the soul in things evil ” —
Oh, hang it! let Shakespeare alone.
The lightning is chained in this liquor—
I can feel it in all of my veins ;
My speech grows perceptibly thicker,
And I keep my feet steady with pains.
How goes the old text ? “ He that arinketh
Consumed shall he be like the chaff ! ”
Behold my left eye, how it winketh !
Oh, noble bush whisky ! I laugh.
How the friends of my boyhood would sorrow
To see me out here on the tank !
One is living on what he can borrow,
And one drives a quill in a bank,
And one has been really successful—
He’s a grocer and mayor of his town—
Larry, darlin’, my stomach’s distressful:
Why doesn’t your liquor keep down ?
Just think what I’ve missed by my riot !
I, too, might have stopped in the shade,
Been respectable, sober, and quiet,
While I cheated my neighbors in trade,
And went into church with a “ topper ”
On my shiny, dishonest old brow.
“ Ah, my friends, what a fall, what a cropper ! ”
So they say when they talk of me now.
I’m blind-drunk, and I’m sick, and —let’s grant it—
A spectacle sad to behold;
But I haven’t sold sugar that’s sanded,
Or fattened on other men’s gold.
My sole “ trick of trade ” is a ruse to
Procure a stray drink when I’m broke;
But I’ll meet all the friends that I used to
When I’m sitting in state on the coke.
Here, you teetotal child of the prophet,
I want you to put me to sleep.
Not up there on the bench (I’d fall off it),
But down here where the carpet-bugs creep.
And my brave ginger-pated Mohammed
(The allusion is lost on the man),
When you’re shutting the door, please don’t slam
it;
Let my rest be as sweet as it can.
Let me sleep just as long as I’m able,
And, in case I should be in the way,
You may bundle me under the table
When you’re sweeping the bar for the day.
Take my cheque from my pocket, and splash it
With all of the boys that drop in :
It’s the handiest method to cash it,
And the very best use for the tin.
Vic.
w.
A VICARIOUS SACRIFICE.
The slow goods-train wound over the waste
With its freight of station stores,
The guard’s van jolted my weary bones
And rattled its crazy doors.
And far to the left, and far to the right,
And far as the eye could scan
There was never a hut to ease the sight,
And never a sign of man.
This side a mirage, and away to north
The guard espied a tree ;
From my lonely musing he drew me forth
To the platform end, to see.
“ MacDowell hung on that mulga bough ;
I cut him down,” he said ;
“ You’ve heard the yarn of his girl, and how
He brooded, and lost his head.”
“ I read of it, guard. Poor girl! her lot
Is her own with none to share ;
No flames of hell can be half as hot
As the shame she has to bear. ”
The guard laughed loud, with a glorious glee,
He laughed with a tickling charm—
“ MacDowell hangs on the mulga tree,
The girl on her husband’s arm.
“ She’s as fresh as paint, and she’s doing fine,
She’s gay as a colt in spring,
She married a fellow up the line
And they live in Linkeering.”
“ And what of the villain ? ” “ Going strong ;
And proud as a man should be ! ”
I said, “ How long, O Lord ! how long ! ”
Said the guard—“ About two foot three.
And it’s a beauty. There’s no flies
On it, as the papers show,
For yesterday week it took first prize
At the Mandoorie Baby Show. ”
The game’s as old as humanity,
It will cease when you stop the sun,
But MacDowell hangs on the mulga tree—
The others get all the fun.
Queensland.
G.

NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One 1 ound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province, you can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish to
interfere
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed'to William Macleod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. F.
Archibald.
Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘Correspondence” column all contributions submitted
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable).
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event, will
be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture containing
a good or adaptable idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion— i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.
Copies of The Bulletin can be obtained in London,
after the arrival of each mail, at the office of the British
Australasian Co., Limited, 31 Fleet Street, E.C.
Also in the Transvaal -.—The Standard and Diggers
News Co., Limited, Johannesburg, South Africa.
Also :— lt. A. Thompson and Co., 3 Church Street,
Capetown, South Africa.
Notice to Subscribers in Victoria, Queensland,
S, Australia, Westralia and Maoriland,
In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage on Intervrovincial Newspapers, the annual sub-
scription outside N.S. Wales is now £l, with 3s. 3d.
added for postage . England £l, with 6s. 6d. added for
postage.
No newspaper can be sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.

SATURDAY, FEBRUARY 22, 1896.
The Fatuity of Johnson, S.M.
The following is a report cut from Sydney
Daily Telegraph of a casetriedthe otherday,
in a Sydney police-court, before Mr. J ohn-
c! i\/r ti • 1 • i
son, S.M. It is interesting as showing either
the state of the law relating to attempted
suicide in New South Wales, or the state of
the said law as fixed up on the spur of the
moment by Johnson, S.M. It is also inter-
esting as showing the awful mental condition
t , .. . . t ,
of Johnson, S.M., and it is important as
casting a side light on the judicial system of
which Johnson, S.M., is a fine example—a
system which is all more or less Johnson,
and, therefore, all more or less unfeeling,
, ~ r, ,
illogical, stupid, and disgraceful
William Steel (30), bootmaker, and Robert Dixon (29),
printer, were charged at the Central Court yesterday
with frequenting Castlereagh-street with intent to com-
mit a felony early on Sunday morning. The magistrate
deemed the evidence for the prosecution weak and dis-
charged the accused. Steel was then charged with
attempting to commit suicide hy cutting his throat in the
ceils at No. 2 police-station early yesterday morning,
Constable Taplin stated that he found the accused lying
on the floor of the ceil bleeding from a wound in the
throat, evidently inflicted by a penknife. Accused
pleaded guilty, and urged that he had been driven to
attempt suicide by the fear that he might get six months
on a charge of which he was innocent. Mr. Johnson,
S.M., said that magistrates were usually inclined to be
very lenient with unfortunate persons whom troubles
tempted to take their own lives. Accused’s case however
was a very different one, he having attempted to commit
suicide in order to defeat the law, and he would have to
go to gaol for three months.
m, * AIT * , * *
Ihe N.S.W. law relating to attempted
suicide, if it can be judged by the adminis-
tration of it, is a loose, far-reaching affair
which leaves almost everything to the dis-
cretion of the Bench. The punishment
varies, according to the discretion of the
Bench, from a few fatherly observations up
to a long term of hard labor ; therefore,
when a case of this kind comes before a
Bench which hasn’t any -discretion, the
results are generally disastrous. The moral
law on the subject is about as indefinite as
the statute law, and public opinion is about
as indefinite as the law. The Scriptures, on
which the statutes are supposed to be more
or less based, leave the question severely
alone • thev nowhere distinctly condemn
suicide, though the Church, which blas-
phemously adds to and takes from its
written oracle to suit its own convenience,
nas chosen to say that they do so. The
Church commenced to do this princi-
pally because, in the old days of chattel
slavery, self-destruction was practically the
only means of escape from serfdom, and
the Church, as the friend and supporter of
the rights of Property, proceeded to block
that avenue of retreat with the material fires
of its alleged Gehenna, in order to prevent
valuable goods from getting away to the
detriment of their owners. The statute-law,
as represented by Johnson, S.M., and his
like, hover’s foolishly between two opinions.
If attempted suicide is a crime at all, then it
is simply attempted murder, and, when the
criminal can be got at, it should be treated
as such ; the only logical basis on which it
can be regarded as a crime is the basis that
the man who attempts to kill himself and the
man who attempts to kill someone else stand
on the same footing. If attempted suicide
is not attempted murder, then it is nothing,
and the law which gives the attempted suicide
seven days or three months, or discharges
him with a caution, or orders him to enter
into his own recognisances in the sum of £5O
to be of good behaviour for one year, practi-
cally acknowledges as much. And having
practically acknowledged it, the seven days
Or the three months are equally an outrage
on logic and morality.
* * * *
This is the situation as it stands without
the grotesqueness of Johnson, S.M., added
to the other complications. The world
is full of poverty and misery, and it con-
tains many people for whom it offers no pre-
sent prospect of an honest livelihood. The
law doesn’t profess to find employment for
any of these people, but it gaols them on the
charge of vagrancy for being found in a
world which doesn’t offer them a livelihood.
Also, after it has gaoled them for being in
that world it gaols them again for trying to
get out of it. In the present case, however,
it carried the matter to even more astounding
lengths. The prisoner, Steel, was arrested,
in the first place, for being up early and
being in a street. He was a shabbily-
dressed person, and perhaps because he was
about early, and was shabbily dressed for
want of better clothes, and was in a
street for want of any better place to go to,
and for no other reasons whatever, the law
assumed him to be a criminal and arrested
him. He had every prospect, under the
police-court variety of law, of getting a long
term of hard labor. And being innocent,
the present and prospective disgrace (the
assumption is a fair one) preyed on his mind,
and he endeavored, in a crude and primitive
fashion, to get away from a world which the
law has made unendurable for a sensitive
man whose clothes bring him under police
suspicion. Then the law, having driven him
to attempted suicide, gave him three months
for having been driven to it. And Johnson,
S.M., after having decided that the accused
wasn’t a criminal, and, therefore, that his
arrest was an injustice, intimated that he
awarded the three months because he had
endeavored to die in order to escape justice
—when the same Johnson had just decided
that it wasn’t justice he was trying to escape
from, but a wholly different article. There-
fore Steel received three months and
a mangled throat and a severe admonition
because the law made a gross mistake.
And Johnson, it would appear from the
daily paper report, went out of his way to
explain that he would have given much less
if the prisoner hadn’t been driven mad by
the prospective disgrace of conviction upon
a groundless charge. In other words, the
law visited Steel’s alleged offence more
heavily because the law’s mistake had driven
him to it in the first place. The law knocked
Steel down, and then kicked him, by the
large moral foot of Johnson, S.M., for not
standing up, and it explained at the same
time, through the beautifully logical John-
son, S.M., that it kicked him especially hard
because it had knocked him down in the
first place. If he had fallen on his own
account it would have kicked him much
more gently. Truly the ways of Johnson.
S.M., are past finding out.
* * * *
It is about time that attempted suicide
was definitely removed from the category of
crime. The law has given up attempting to
punish the suicide who succeeds, and it is a
mean, low-down proceeding to wreak ven-
geance on the one who fails. If there is
anything in those allegedly Scriptural doc-
trines on which the law professes to be
based, then the attempted suicide has done
his best to get into everlasting lire, and the
man who has just missed that destiny has
run enough risk to satisfy even the wrath of
Stipendiary Magistrate Johnson. If he
hasn’t just missed the alleged region of per-
petual horror, then the Scripture on which the
law professes to be based is a mistake, and the
law is a lie. The sentence passed on the
suicide who has failed— generally because he
was too ignorant or too much deranged to
understand the location of his own vital
parts, or too poor to buy a weapon that
could be trusted to go off—-is based on the
assumption that attempted suicide is at-
tempted murder ; that attempted murder is
equivalent to, say, 14 days’ imprisonment;
that, as in Steel s case, the attempt to get
away from the law’s brutal injustice is & so
much worse than attempted murder as to
merit 2| months extra; that the man who
suicides goes to perdition ; and that the
person who isn’t afraid of everlasting tor-
ment can be scared into a better way of
thinking by a short period of confinement.
The sentence passed on the suicide who
succeeds, because lie was learned enoug 1
or sane enough to know where his vitals
were located, or had obtained money
enough—perhaps by defrauding his creditors
—to buy a weapon that would go off, is almost
always that he was a perfectly innocent per-
son who was temporarily deranged, io put
the matter in another form, if a policeman
arrives in time to stop the suicide, the kit ei
is a sane person who is guilty of a great
crime, and Johnson sends him to gaol
accordingly. If the policeman doesn t amve
in time, then he is a deranged but guiltless
individual suffering from misfortune. There-
fore, sanity and guilt, or insanity and inno-
cence, depend on the nearness or the velocity
of a policeman, and the State, which fails to
supply enough policemen to keep one near
each possible suicide, is the party that is
really guilty.
* * * *
Further, if the suicide, like the ill-starred
Arthur Daore, had a medical training which
enabled him to find his jugular at one
attempt, and kill himself with certainty, then
he is an innocent but deranged person ; if,
like Steel, he had no medical training, and,
therefore, didn’t know the location of
his most convenient artery, he is fully re-
sponsible for his actions and has com-
mitted a truly dreadful offence. Consequently
insanity lies in the possession of a medical
training, and every doctor who commits
suicide is a lunatic, and every doctor who is
going to commit suicide is a lunatic, and, as
no one knows for certain which doctor may
not be going to commit suicide sooner or
later, the whole medical profession must be
viewed with grave suspicion. An insane
person may be also defined as a man who
steals or otherwise acquires a reliable re-
volver, or has access to a supply of prussic
acid, or who isn’t caught by a projecting tree
as he is taking a short-cut over a precipice ;
and a sane person is the same person, or any
other person, if the revolver misses fire, or if
he takes some slow and futile poison instead
of the reliable prussic acid—probably because
he hadn’t a match handy and seized the
wrong bottle in the dark, or if a tree
catches him by the clothes as he is going
down, and is strong enough to hold him.
And, as its strength may depend on
the presence or absence of dry-rot or
some other form of decay, a sane person may
be briefly defined as one who lives near a
sound tree, and a deranged person as one
who lives near a tree that isn’t sound. The
Bulletin didn’t make these definitions and
is in no way responsible for them; they
were made principally by the Johnsons of
this great country, and this paper invites
these dull and drivelling persons to consider
their handiwork.
* * * *
The great, dull, stupid public which in-
directly makes the laws has much to answer
for in this matter. It grovelled and maun-
dered and shed tears and blossoms over Dacre,
who killed his wife and then himself, and
important legal magnates sent flowers to his
pompous funeral—all because he wore good
clothes and was a man of some mark, and did
his alleged crime successfully and sensa-
tionally. If lie hadn’t killed his wife
and had failed to kill himself, and there-
foie had been incomparably less crimi-
nal, in the legal sense, than he was,
the same legal magnates who helped to
wail over his dust would probably have
given him three months—because he would
have, been so much less deserving of it.
Public opinion, or what passes for it, respects
the suicide, if he is sufficiently picturesque.
It has maundered for centuries over Richard
Grenville, who went to certain and unneces-
sary death in the Revenge. It respected
Balmaceda because he preferred to end his
career with dignity rather than be executed
as a public show. It has drivelled to utter
weariness over an alleged boy who committed
felo-de-se by needlessly standing on an alleged
burning deck It has dropped into poetry
over the suicides oi Sardanapalus, Brutus
Hannibal Cleopatra, Saul of Israel, Bem’
Arnold Winkelried, and any number of
other people who died voluntarily to avoid
what they regarded as a worse fate. The one
redeeming feature in the squalid tragedy of
apoleon the Little, according to public
opinion, is the picture of the dying man with
the painted face who sat o/hif Cslln
the face of a storm of bullets at Sedan
conm ng f ° r d6ath which wos£?

PLAIN ENGLISH.
A State Bank and a National Ass.
The first State Land Bank of Australia started
business last week in Adelaide, and the
Observer, which is against the Government
and the Democratic-party, and in favor of the
National Ass and all its Ass works, started out
in an elaborate article to prove that the said
Land Bank will be no assistance to the strugg-
ling agriculturist or pastoralist after all. This
is how the Observer does it :
As an illustration of the working of this clause we mav
take the case of a farmer with £lOOO worth of land and
£5OO worth of permanent improvements. Assuming that
he be allowed a mortgage up to the hilt on the valuation
he will be able to borrow £9OO from the Government ’
In the cases of the great majority of the Crown lessees' the
Act provides only trifling relief. A lessee on right of pur
chase of a property valued at £l5OO may have £lOOO
still to pay before he can complete his purchase That
£lOOO will be taken out of the £l5OO, and the loan -will be
based on the £5OO ; so that the maximum amount which
the lessee can borrow will be £3OO. He will not be able to
get as much as this in cases where the sum which he
agreed to pay for his property is in excess of present
market values. For illustration, a right of purchase mav
have been arranged at a total of £l5OO, of which £5OO
may have been paid; but the estate may be worth
now only £l2OO. In that event the utmost extent
of the advance, except in the cases of gardens, will be
three-fifths of £2OO, or £l2O. Other lessees may secure
advances merely on the improved value of their lands—a
lessee with £lOOO worth of land and £3OO of improve
ments thus borrowin''- on the £3OO only—a loan of £lBO
In all cases £5OOO is the maximum advance permissible to
any single borrower. Clearly, then, the most needy
landholders cannot be benefited directly under the Act *
The plaint of the Observer, consequently,
boils down as follows: If a man has £l5OO
worth of property, the Land Bank will only
lend £9OO on it. If he has £l5OO worth of
property on which he still owes the State
£lOOO, so that his own interest in it is only
£5OO, the Land Bank will only lend £3oo—it
actually won’t lend money on the £lOOO worth
of land which the mortgagor hasn’t paid for and
lias no title to. If he has a property that is
now worth only £l2OO, with a £lOOO liability
to the State upon it, so that his own interest is
only £2OO, the Land Bank will only lend on
the security of the £2oo—it flatly refuses to
lend on the security of the value that is
lost through depreciation, or on the property
which isn’t paid for, and which the borrower
consequently doesn’t own. And if a man holds
a lease from the State of land worth £lOOO, and
lias £5OO worth of improvements on it,’ the
State won’t lend money to its tenant on its
own land, because it isn’t his to mortgage, but
will only lend on the improvements which are
the tenant’s own property. Of course, no
private landlord would lend money to his own
tenant on the security of liis—the landlord’s—
own property; and no bank would lend anybody
money on land he had bought but hadn’t paid
for, or on the value which tiie land used to
have, but which it no longer possesses. But
the Ass-ridden Observer solemnly denounces
the State Land Bank because it won’t do these
tilings either, and will merely .lend on the same
security which is demanded by the private
lender—but at about half the private lender’s
rate of interest! Altogether, the Ass variety of
logic is a strange and floundering concern,
vaguely suggestive of a daddy-longlegs strug-
gling in a milk-jug.
The Troubles of the Teamster.
Not alone by constructing railways in order to
carry away the traffic which, geographically,
rightly belongs to a neighbor, nor by a system
of differential tariffs, nor by refusing to snag
rivers flowing into others’ territory, do Aus-
tralian provinces manifest their provincialism,
to the great loss in pounds, shillings, and pence
of the people. In'the administration of even the
N.S.W. Mines (Water Conservation) and Roads
and Bridges offices is the same thing illus-
trated. As The Bulletin said, the other day,
the natural outlet for Western and North-
western N.S.W. is Port Augusta or Port Pirie,
on Spencer’s Gulf (S.A.), from which a railway
mas inland 300 miles—owned by S.A. Govern-
ment as far as Cookburu, and by private com-
panies as far as Tarrawingee, 80 miles further.
Now, when the back-countryman realised that
this line was open, wool began to come down
from, and goods go up by, the new route to
most of the country west of the Darling, right up
to and beyond the Queensland border. There-
upon N.S.W. Government turned its attention
to the tracks towards Bourke (the terminus of
the N.S.W. Western-railway system, 503 miles
from Sydney), and absolutely neglected those
leading in the other direction. It has, in con-
formity with this policy, made costly' dams and
put down bores to ensure a good water-supply
on the way to or from Bourke, while the travel-
ler by the other route has for months past been
compelled to turn thief and steal water for his
horses or cattle, because the Government has
not provided tanks, and the stations (most of
them desperately short of water) have issued
notices that they will not supply the travelling
public. No end of trouble lias resulted from
this. Stations, finding it impossible to other-
wise preserve their scanty water, have closed
Lacks which have been open for many years,
and which teamsters believed were gazetted ;
and several unfortunates have been heavily
fined for refusing to turn back. One gives his
experiences of three days :
' ~ tank (a private one, five miles from the
tuesday morning. Got as far as the fence (seven

nr. s * x ni 'les next dav ; then took the horses
• tank (four miles). Did to the
tank next day, and camped Friday.
Seventeen miles with his “loading” in four
c ays; yet it will be observed his team had
ac ually to travel 39 miles. That was for water
a one; probably it was nearly as far to feed.
ven when the Government does make a tank
°r sink a bore (it is the same in this respect on
mos ot the tracks) it does not make a sufficient
reservation ot land in the neighborhood; and
the squatter-man wears the country all around
as >aie as a city-street. Further, at the bores

Those Japanese Boots.
A few days ago the New Soutli Wales Chamber
of Manufactures announced that the first ship-
ment of Japanese-made boots and shoes had
arrived in Sydney, and that the cheap little
brown man was beginning to compete in a
branch of industry which he hadn’t hitherto
interfered with, and to pour into New South
Wales’s open ports goods made by men working
in a cheap land at say 6d. a day to wipe out the
local white artisan. The news was received,
as usual, with a whoop of scorn by the Sydney
morning papers, and the one which perverts
the truth in King-street—as distinguished
from the one which doesn’t adhere to solid
fact at the corner of Hunter-street —started
out to show the utter absurdity of the allega-
tion. “ In Japan,” said this unreliable
authority, “ the common article of footwear
was a sandal which lasted only a few days, but
which was so inexpensive that a person’s total
expenditure in this direction amounted only
to about ss. annually. There was only one
small factory where leather boots were made in
the country . . . and the output was so
small that he had his whole work cut out to
supply orders from the Mikado and his suite
as well as the foreign residents.” This informa-
tion may have been reasonably accurate a dozen
or 20 years back, but it is lamentably out of
date now. At the Chicago Exhibition three
large Japanese boot-factories exhibited samples
of their goods, which were quite up to the
ordinary white man standard, and their agents
bought large quantities of labor - saving
machinery, with a view to enlarging their out-
put. Since then, five or six more large factories
have started. The straw sandal at ss. per
.annum is fast becoming extinct. During the
(late war, immense orders for boots for the
.-army were placed with the Jap. manufacturers,
who largely increased their plant to keep pace
with them, and, now that this demand
thas subsided, they are anxiously looking
{for new markets to keep their machinery
•employed, and New South Wales, under
•the present regime, is just the market that is
wanted. The large imports of hides and leather
into Japan are sufficient in themselves to show
that that country has got beyond the straw-
sandal stage of civilisation. Quite recently an
Australian order for the finer qualities of leather
.could not be filled in San Francisco without
several months’ delay, because all the available
supplies for a long time ahead were booked for
Japan. Under these circumstances, it is no good
fighting the inevitable of to-day with the alleged
facts of the decade before last. The small
brown men are hard facts who can’t be ex-
plained away, and they are in dead earnest.
London Referee, a very English paper indeed,
on the brotherhood of the alleged Anglo-Saxon
race :
It has often been alleged that the hostility of Americans
towards this country, as evidenced by the tall talk of
their newspapers, was more apparent than real. On this
subject let us no longer be under any delusion. The
Americans dislike us just as much—l don’t say more, but
just as much—and are just as ready to go to war with us
as any other nation, blood and speech notwithstanding.
On the question of blood, indeed, it is not a little re-
markable that the bitterest spirit towards England is
breathed by the representatives of the puritanical com-
munities who were directly founded by our own kith and
kin who went out in the Mayflower. Let those foolish
enthusiasts who prate about the essential fraternity of
kindred nations make note of that, and let us make note
of it, too, with regard to our own kith and kin in Africa,
Australia and Canada. The Canadians, to do them
justice, are, like ourselves, bridling up at Uncle Sam’s
pretensions. Doubtless they have no more love for their
kith and kin in the United States than ours in the United
States have for us. I was thinking for the moment of
the Imperial Federation idea. More than once in these
columns, on evidence which seemed to me convincing, I
have insisted that separation by thousands of miles
of sea checks, and in course of time kills, community
of sentiment, whether between two families or two
peoples ; and I have been reasoned with, and even blamed,
for so doing by patriotic correspondents with whom the
wish was father to the thought. The first generation of
colonists look back no doubt to the mother country with
affection. To them it is still the old home. Something
of this sentiment may cling to the second generation, but
the third and the fourth are strangers. Not only in-
different to England, which to them is a mere geographi-
cal expression, they have, if anything, a hostile bent,
because in their youthful self-sufficiency they resent the
supposed superiority of the old country, and chafe under
any burdens or sacrifices which the Imperial bond may
impose. I am as sorry as any of my correspondents that
this should be so. On the other hand, the fact is as I say;
* it is folly not to recognise it. ’Tis true, ’tis pity, and pity
■ ’tis, ’tis true. Considering what a hotch-potch of nation-
alities the people of the United States now are, the pre-
' dominance of English names among the governing classes
' is at first sight a little strange. It does not imply, how-
' ever, all that it seems to do. English being the
predominant language, there is a tendency for the
names brought by foreign immigrants to be Anglicised
Baumann becoming Bowman, Rosenfbld Rosefield, and
’ so on. Not long ago I had an interesting talk with a well-
iknow’n American dramatist, bearing an indubitably Eng-
lish name —I don’t see why I should not mention it,
IBronson Howard. I assumed that he was of English
descent. “ Not at all,” he replied; “ it may seem curious
to you, considering my name, but by descent I am some-
thing like three-quarters Dutch and one-quarter Scotch.
In my view, however, a pure English descent, if such a
thing can be said to exist in America, is not incompatible
with the most hostile sentiments towards the old country.
The great American Republic has acquired conceptions of
rights privileges, and duties wholly different from ours
as a nation. Hence there is no such community of feeling
between Great Britain and the U.S. A. as is theoretically
contended. Witness this war boom, which ,is as sudden,
as intense, and I fully believe as dangerous as any flare-up
of animosity that could take place between two Con-
tinental countries. I am afraid we are all too fond of
deluding ourselves with cant, of which there is, naturally,
a great deal about at the present season. There is the
cant of good wishes, of philanthropy, of friendship, and,
as I have been saying, of international concord and good-
will. I should have thought that this Anglo-American
crisis would have helped to brush the cobwebs away from
people’s minds, with regard to international relations,
anyhow. Nevertheless, in a great organ of public opinion
I find the following comment on the crisis : They (the
Americans) know how dear to us is the majestic march of
the Republic on the pathway of her grand destiny and
how we count upon her greatness and power for the future
progress and influence of our common race. This is the
very Pecksniftianism of journalism.
Purves, Q.C., has begun to regret his idiotic
attack on Champion. The accusation of
“ blackmailing ” was obviously barristerial
well, “ privilege,” and Champion’s remarks on
the subject were the more effective for coming a
few days after the accusation, when Melbourne
had had time to digest the browbeater’s in-
solence. “If Mr. Purves,” said Champion,
“ made any such statement outside a court of
law, I should have a remedy before the law.
Mr. Purves pointed out that the days of per-
sonal chastisement for unmerited insult are
past. That point I cannot argue with him. I
have not his wide experience of such
matters, and have never been thrashed in
the public street, or concerned in a race-
course brawl, owing to cowardly abuse
of a privileged position. His opening in
this case, with its wild and totally unfounded
charges against me and my paper, is a disgrace
to the Bar, a menace to justice, and a proof of
unscrupulous malignity. With Mr. Purves’s
coarser, viler, and more general abusive epithets
I will not trouble the court. I have no need to
defend myself from them, coming, as they do,
from a man whose recent address to the youths
entering his own profession was unprintable on
the score of its obscenity.” That knocks h 1
out of Purves, Q.C., and should be useful here-
after to any litigant who may defend his own
case against a hired vituperator. It is notable,
by the w r ay, that the “ great ” dailies of Mel-
bourne, which printed in full Purves’s imputa-
tion of blackmailing against Champion, cordially
ignored Champion’s stinging and well-formu-
lated reply to the lawyer’s reckless abuse.
If the Victorian Crown Law Department be
anxious to arrive at the truth of Lormer’s
charges against certain brother magistrates, it
is strange that the department should have
appointed an Enquiry Board which can’t possi-
bly dispose of the scandal promptly. A few
days since the Board threatened to close its
sittings altogether, unless the Crown could pro-
ceed at once with its cases. The inevitably in-
dignant lawyer for the defence snorted forth
his inevitable request for the curtain of silence
to be dropped on the enquiry, and it really
seemed as though Lormer was to be the victim
of a fiasco. However, he managed to bring the
Crown up to the scratch again, and for
the next three or four days the Board was busily
engaged in taking “revelations.” Then, just
as the fun was fastest and most furious, the
chairman (Judge Williams) intimated that he
would be engaged on circuit for the rest of the
month, after which another member of the
board (Prof. Kernot) would have private
matters to attend to. The board therefore
adjourned to a date unfixed. It may possibly
meet again towards the end of March, unless
the third member shall happen to be overtaken
by an important engagement. Also, there is a
reasonable chance of somebody being too ill to
proceed when further proceedings fall due.
Meantime, the shower of revelations has been
sufficiently heavy to soak into the public mind
and nourish the roots of curiosity.
The Argus in condemnation of that clause in
the Vic. Factories’ Bill forbidding the letting-
out of work, excised by the House of Misrepre-
sentatives :
The right of the citizen to work where he pleases is
obvious, and the proposed interference with this liberty
was so gross that the strongest reasons would be required
to justify its adoption.
The right of the citizen to work where he pleases
is not obvious, and is not admitted. For in-
stance, he may not work in an unsafe house or
one condemned as insanitary ; and, as for the
gross interference with his liberty, all law is an
interference with somebody’s liberty, in one
way or another, and it is only when the sub-
ject’s liberty to sweat for the fat middleman is
interfered with that respectable papers like the
Argus think it worth while to protest. This
sudden anxiety about the liberty of the woman
who earns 7s. 6d. a week from the sweater, by
working all day and half the night, is truly
touching. It would, indeed, be a lamentable
thing if the law interfered with her beatific
freedom.
Extracts from one leader in the S.M. Herald
of Friday, 14th Feb. :
Competent authorities do not attribute the dulness of
their trade to the importation of Japanese boots, but to
“ the heavy stocks held by many of the manufacturing
firms.” In other words, it was the effect of our short-
lived Protection. The protection given to the manufac-
ture had its usual effect of unduly stimulating internal
competition, and causing the production of more goods
than the restricted market could absorb The
principle underlying the application of Freetrade to the
industrial conditions of this colony is the necessity of
cheapening production to enable us to hold our own in
competition with other countries. To cheapen production
we must cheapen the necessaries of life. We must do this
by removing all trammels imposed by a mistaken theory
which aimed, not at cheapening cost, but at artificially
enhancing it. The new system requires time to effect the
required adjustments, but these being duly made there is
no reason to doubt that the bracing air .of freedom will
vivify all our genuine industries and augment the
volume of the output with which we have to balance our
accounts with the world.
It appears from these half-dozen sentences : (1)
That Protection makes trade dull; (2) that
Protection unduly stimulates trade; (3) that
Protection gluts the market with goods, and, as
everyone knows, a glutted market means low
prices ; (4) that Protection is a mistaken theory
which “ artificially enhances ” prices ; (5) that
Protection is bad because it “ unduly stimu-
lates internal competition and causes the pro-
duction of more goods than the restricted
market can absorb ”; (6) that Freetrade is
good because its “bracing air” will “vivify”
our industries and “augment our output”—
in other words, Freetrade is going to make this
objectionable production still greater ; (7) that
Protection has caused the dullness in the boot-
trade and the consequent low wages, and there-
fore should be put down ; (8) that Freetrade is
going to further “ cheapen production ” —which
necessarily includes cheapening the producer,
otherwise reducing wages—therefore, it should
be boosted up. Which, all put together, is a
good deal of information to find in one article.
“We want to avoid being placed in the hands
of the class of ‘ professional Governors ’ who
take the position to make money out of it,”
says Sydney Daily Telegraph of Bth instant.
How, why should not a Governor, as well as
others, make money by his work? According to
all accounts, Queen Victoria lays by something
regularly. The Telegraph does not admit
that a Governor should lose money by his office,
and it consequently adopts towards him the
Henry Parkes phraseology in the Payment of
Members Act—the payment of an annual
amount to reimburse him for expenses incurred.
This is sad shambling on the part of a paper
professing to wear a democratic eye. The
phraseology of the Payment of Members Act is
fraudulent English; any phraseology which
offers a man a salary, but tells him lie must not
regard it as a salary, merely beats about the
bush of principle,
Sydney Morning Herald starts, in a curious
obfuscation of ideas, to prove that the proposed
Government Tobacco Monopoly in Victoria
can’t possibly work. “ Either the State,” it
says, “to benefit the local growers must re-
strict, perhaps cease, the importation of Ameri-
can leaf, and so sacrifice <£250,000 a year of
revenue, or else it is not apparent where the
farmer who grows the Victorian leaf or the
workmen who manufacture it are to find their
advantage.” Well, suppose a degraded country
imports lib. of American tobacco, value Is., and
tlie State charges a Customs duty of 35., and
further suppose it grows lib. of Victorian
tobacco at Is., and the State charges an excise
duty of 35., then the State will have got 6s.
revenue, and both tobaccos will sell at, say, 4s.
per lb. And if a State Tobacco Monopoly shuts
out the American product, and buys
21b. of local leaf at Is. per lb., and sells it at 4s.
per lb., it will still have got 6s. profit; it won’t
have sacrificed £250,000 a year or any other
sum ; it won’t have raised prices ; and the local
grower will come in simply because he will sell
twice as much of his product. The Herald’s
incapacity to do a small sum like that is painful
to any well-regulated mind.
An obsolete tyrant has just been deposed in
Victoria in the shape of an Act familiarly
known as “27 Eliz.” It made gifts of
land revocable at the will of the giver,
but did not interfere in any way with
gifts of goods and chattels. If Jones gave all
his movable property to ’Arriet, really, but
not provably, to dodge future creditors, ’Arriet
would be quite secure ; but, if he gave his wife
or his son a piece of land in consideration of year-
long weary work on it, no matter how bona-fide.
the gift was, this beautiful Act would step in
and allow him, perhaps 40 years after, to sell
or mortgage the land, even if the purchaser had
clear notice that it belonged to someone else.
In New South Wales, some years ago, a scan-
dalous case procured the emasculation of this
old -rou6 progeny of the virgin-queen. Anoma-
lous laws of this description abound on the
Statute-book, and most of them are of as full
vitality (in the law-courts) as when they were
enacted, or alleged to have been enacted. A
Cobweb-Broom Act is needed in places other
than Maoriland.
A slab of immortal truth from Melbourne
Punch :
What has been the reward of the faithful or scrupulous
auditor in the past ? In nearly every case he has been
treated with insolence and contempt, and in every case
when possible he has been run out of his position, either
by the directors or their friends the shareholders in
meeting' assembled. What thanks did the auditors of the
Australian Natives’ Association receive for drawing atten-
tion to the state of the investment account; the auditors
of the Brunswick Council for finding fault with the books
and accounts of the Council; the auditor of the Wesleyan
Church Building Loan Fund for not signing the balance-
sheet ; the auditors of the Guardian Accident Company
for declining to sign the balance-sheet in the form pre-
sented ; and that able man, Mr. Alex. Young, of Messrs.
Turquand, Youngs, Bishop and Co., accountants, London,
for drawing attention to additional provision being
necessary for losses in connection with the N.Z. Loan
and Mercantile Agency Company, of which co. he was
auditor, and for stating that if re-elected it was his inten-
tion to issue such reports as he thought were necessary
in the interests of the company ? Mr. Young was not
re-elected. The directors stated that they would resign
if he was again appointed, so Mr. Young was gently run
out. We have in our mind’s eye at present three auditors
who are next door to starving. They were too particular,
too hard for directors and managers to deal with, so they
have been shunted. What do the shareholders or credi-
tors care? We could present a long list of auditors who
have been insulted, cast aside, and are now neglected
chiefly on account of their scrupulousness in dealing with
balance-sheets. We maintain, in the interests of fair play
and justice, and especially in the interests of the creditors
and shareholders of our financial institutions, that the
auditor of the future, if he is to have all tha responsibility
of balance-sheets cast upon him, must be placed by
the New Companies Bill in an almost impregnable
position. He must not be subject to insult by
councillors, directors or managers, nor be liable to re-
moval either by packed meetings of shareholders, or
threats of resignation by directors. Auditors in the past
have saved many heavy losses, found out many defalca-
tions, and done much hard, painstaking work ; if, in the
future by law, they are to be under pains and penalties,
and made responsible for the whole financial con-
dition of our banks and companies, their povver and
position must be defined, they should be appointed by
Government, or by some independent board, and no
auditor should act for more than two institutions. The
work to be done, and the time required, would occupy a
year, and would have to be proportionately remun-
erated.
“The Notorious Mrs. Ebbsmith.”
The secret is out. Pinero is a woman — and a
New Woman to boot. None but she could have
written “ Mrs. Ebbsmith,” with its monotonous
recital of the Magnanimity of Woman, the Mean-
ness of Man. Now the Monster may see himself
in his true colors—which are uniformly drab and
dirty. Cleeve —a vain, sensual, nervous weakling ;
the Duke—the dregs of a worthless roue ; Sir
Sandford —a prig and an ass ; the Rev. Winterfield
—with some manliness, but —a Wesleyan parson ;
—there is a gallery of leading characters for the
Brutes to contemplate ! But look at the women !
Agnes—a simple angel (horrid that her “ sex ”
should “ find her out ”); Gertrude—another angel,
brimful of noble notions; Mrs. Cleeve—angel
encore! Now do you begin to comprehend what
women really are ?—for, of course, these are types,
patterns, and the bulk of the Sex is guaranteed
exactly same as sample.
* * * *
And what are the relations of those Brutes to
these Angels ? The husband of Agnes—a Brute
who wallows somewhere off the stage —treated her
“for about twelve months like a woman in a
harem, for the rest of the time like a beast
of burden.” Gertrude’s husband (also wal-
lowing behind the scenes) ditto, ditto but he
only kept her in the harem for “ weeks—weeks,
not months ! ” Mrs. Cleeve’s husband ditto ditto •
with modifications due to his ignoble character.
Two Brutes die (Heaven is weary of them); the
third Brute, Cleeve, separates from his victim and
endeavors to drag Angel Agnes to the pit by way
of her Sex (such a nuisance—Sex !). But Angel
Gertrude flies to the rescue—assisted by her
brother, who, by joining the Third Sex, has dodged
the destiny of Brute which. Nature designed for
him. The three Brutes are baulked of their prey.
But an infernal idea strikes one of them—a con-
ventional bandage is placed over Mrs. Angel
Cleeve’s eyes, and she is induced to tempt her
sister to ruin. In vain ; her angelic nature asserts
itself; she tears off the bandage and denounces
the Brutes, who retire gnashing their horrid teeth.
Whoop ! Virtue triumphant ! Angelic pas de
trois blessed by the parson ! The sky opens and
discloses “God in Her Heaven,” with female
angels sitting round on silver thrones and gazing
contemptuously over the edge at the male devils
all down below. Pure white lights. Certain.
*****
There was never such a gift to the New Woman
as this play. Her -hopes, her trials, her Heaven
of Reform, her Hell of Marriage, her strength of
character, her weakness of Sex (such a bore-*
Sex !), the crusade for a new morality, the in-
famous necessity for the propagation of a new
race to profit by the new morality —all are set
forth or suggested. God bless Mrs. Pinero ! How
gallantly she rears the standard of truth, how
* * * *
It was precisely at this point that I thought of
“The Second Mrs. Tanqueray,” and “brought
up all standing. ” For there the noble, magnani-
mous rebel against cruel conventions is a male,
and his sacrifices, his efforts to give Paula another
chance, are baulked partly by Paula herself—who
is practically incorrigible—and partly by the prim
little Pharisee, “Saint Ellean.” In “Mrs. Tan-
queray ”it is his callous, unsympathetic (first) wife
who made Aubrey miserable- not he her. Ardale is
an average man, whose valor may be set against his
vices; the Orreyeds “pair,” and don’t count in
the division ; Drummle and the other male
characters are decent, sensible fellows. But the
keynote of the play is struck by its central figures
—Aubrey, Paula, and Ellean—and how, O New
Woman ! does the stainless, generous, wife-harried
male compare with the female sinner and the
female Pharisee? The devil take Mr. Pinero !
What a truthless, cowardly contrast ! So like a
man !
* * * *
Are the plays complementary ? Hardly; though
a little alteration would make them so. If
Aubrey’s marriage were merely the first step in
a public agitation for the assistance of fallen
women, and the folly of his agitation were shown
by his own experiment, the analogy of his role
with that of Agnes might seem close enough to be
intended by the author. But his experiment is
meant to begin and end in his own household ; he
does not preach marriage-with-the-fallen from pole
to pole as Agnes would preach free-union. In any
case, it is a mistake to consider Pinero’s chief aim
as the intentional inculcation of a definite moral
lesson. His first object is to write a good play —a
play which will be worth seeing, worth reading,
and worth thinking over. To do that, he must
picture life as it is—“ hold, as ’twere, the mirror
up to nature ; show virtue her own feature, scorn
her own image, and the very age and body of the
time his form and pressure.” If Pinero’s plays
are good plays, and contain moral lessons, then
these are the moral lessons of life, not of Pinero.
His talent but selects from the materials which
are ready to everybody’s hand, refines, dresses,
illuminates, and knits tangled skeins into a cohe-
rent story. But if his selection be bad, if he
heighten lights or blacken shadows to gain a
moral effect which he did not, in essence, find
awaiting him, then his play is unnatural, it is a
bad play, and he has missed his object.
* * * *
The apparent contradiction in the sex-morals of
“ Mrs. Tanqueray ” and “ Mrs. Ebbsmith ” is due
to the little-appreciated fact that the universe is a
big thing, and you can’t get more than one corner
of it on one stage at one time. Both are good
plays; but “Mrs. Tanqueray” is far the better.
It has better incident, better dialogue ; it moves
better, is better constructed; its effect is more
powerful, more continuous, more permanent.
“Mrs. Ebbsmith” has the stronger motive—the
strife between Good and Evil for a human being
is the strongest motive imaginable—and Agnes
might conceivably be made a stronger character
than Paula. She is not —Pinero’s execution fails
to reach the height of his conception. Had it
succeeded, he would be another Goethe, and
“ Mrs. Ebbsmith ” a second “ Faust.” But
Pinero, though a very clever fellow, is not a genius.
* * * *
I like Mrs. Brough, for she doesn’t wear petti-
coats, and her graceful outlines and motions please
the eye and the soul. The other women are
mostly waddling bundles. I don’t like Mrs.
Brough, for she is a phonograph, an automaton,
with the eternal air of saying a part and gesticu-
lating by machinery. Her glare, her tricks with
the handkerchief, her frantic rush, all weary me.
I hate to say this, for she “ toils painfully,” and
she has given me much pleasure in “Niqbe,”
“ The Amazons,” and a dozen other plays. Even
in ‘ ‘ Mrs. Tanqueray ” she succeeded for three-
fourths of the play, because the part suited her,
and the tragedy comes only in spots. But for the
sombre, tragic Mrs. Ebbsmith you want a tall,
gaunt, ferocious she-wolf —the child of Dam-
shicawgo and a female clothes-prop and
Mrs. Brough is everything but that. Even
if she had the originality, the initiative,
which the part requires, she is too good-looking,
too well-kept, has too much flesh on her face,
Her features are meant to smile : when she frowns
she is less impressive than grotesque. For the
rest, Miss Faber has the same misfortune ; her in-
significant features condemn her to insipidity.
Miss Spong is plump (“ Hilda, I charge thee put
away embonpoint ! ”); and does she try to imitate
Mrs. Brough ? Boucicault is good, as always ;
Brough is good, as nearly always. Ward, as
Lucas Cleeve, is fair—no more. The whole pro-
duction is not on a level with the company’s
version of “ Mrs. Tanqueray ” —but it is a lot
better than Australian audiences deserve.—Brig-
ham Brown, M.D., F.R.C.S.
The latest American advertising dodge.
A shopkeeper offered a silk dress to any woman
who would consent to be married in his show-
window. The whites shirked the publicity, but a
colored girl consented, and was duly spliced in full
view of a grinning crowd.
The reference and boycotting system has a
strong hold Out-back, despite the Union agita-
tors’ objections (writes a Bulletin correspondent).
As a rule, those loudest in condemning the
“ stiffs as bushmen call them—carry a “ White-
ly King” reference in one pocket and a Union
ticket in the other. Men are asked to show refer-
ences even when applying for foot-rotting sheep
or burr-cutting.
The dwellers at Warrandyte, one of the
least utterly hopeless of Vic. village settlements,
have struck a new terror, worse than rabbits. It
seems that wild deer invade Warrandyte in the
early morning and browse on young fruit trees,
not to mention cabbages and small salads. They
are protected by a penalty of <£ls a head for shoot-
ing at them, and no ordinary wire-netting is tall
enough to defeat their agility. Wherefore the
settlers crave leave to “ pot ” the deer, and as
Premier Turner can’t think of any reason why
these ruthless destroyers should be held sacred, it
is probable that venison will be cheap presently.
“ What shall he have who killed the deer? ” for-
sooth ! Down at Warrandyte he has been getting
<£ls, or three months, instead of several square
meals gratis.
