Opera House Rickards’ Variety Co.
That rather serious-minded production’
“ The Yeomen of the Guard,” began the operatic
season at Sydney Lyceum on Monday night. It
also served to introduce Mr. Courtice Pounds, the
Firm’s new tenor, and to re-introduce Miss Flora
Graupner after a long and lamentable absence of
nearly three years. Lau»i, Howard Vernon and
Miss Nina Osborne are likewise in the cast, and a
few other people of less importance, together with
one or two who don’t matter much. There are,
to all intents and purposes, only half-a-dozen
characters in the “Yeomen ? ” and a large and
valuable portion of the co. is therefore crowded
out, and held over for future publication.
*****
Courtice Pounds, as Colonel Fairfax,
proved to be a handsome individual, not nearly
so fat as many people expected, and looking in
some attitudes rather like Brownlow’s Henry IV.
Also, he is the possessor of a clear, melodious,
excellently-managed voice, and his first appearance
was an emphatic success all through. Miss Flo.
Graupner is practically unchanged, and her Elsie
Maynard is almost as good a performance as any-
thing she has yet done. But despite Lauri’s
jester, and the lofty and dignified tights of
Courtice Pounds, and the restoration of the long-
lost Flora, the best features of the opera are
Howard Vernon, gaoler and tormentor, and Miss
Nina Osborne in the short frock and tan stock-
ings of Phoebe Meryll. When one considers
this pair carefully, it seems almost a pity
that the opera doesn’t end differently. For if the
giddy Phoebe had married the large and affection-
ate tormentor, then their children might have
themselves polishing the thumbscrew
when they came home from school, and might
have done their home lessons on top of the rack,
and removed the oxide off the scavenger’s daughter
in between times, and otherwise helped the old
man in his declining years. Vernon's tormentor
is a grim, sallow, earnest, and apparently con-
scientious person, and The Bulletin considers it
Erobable that he would have made a good hus-
and. The “ Yeomen ”is a five-night, show only,
and by the time this issue appears it will be nearly
over. Sydney doesn’t enthuse much over it nowa-
days, and the fact that the “ Gondoliers ” goes up
on Saturday, and “Ma Mie Rosette ” in the
middlq of next week, is sufficient reason for drop-
ping the subject right here.
** * *
At Her Majesty’s the curtain no longer
goes up sideways or comes down mid first, and the
condenser has got into working order, and the big
explosion explodes regularly, and a lot of the
mesmerist’s needless loquacity has been cut out,
and “To the West” has settled down into a
reasonably workable melo-shriek which, at its
worst, is much better "than a lot of the imported
article. It is not particularly coherent, and never
will be, but not one melodrama in ten will screw
together at the joints, apd this one screws quite as
well as the average. And, anyhow, there is a
pleasant, more-or-less-Australian atmosphere about
it; it is suggestive of the salt-bush plains, and the
great Dead Sheep Land, and Bill the wanderer,
who pervades the wastes Out Back, and our own
great national drought, and many other things
pertaining to this rising country. And the spec-
tacle of Dampier disguised as a maundering old
shepherd driving a flock of sheep through a dust-
cloud past a public-house arouses feelings of pride
concerning Australia’s staple product—that pro-
duct which infests the interior on the back of the
shiftless merino, and' furnishes employment for
Drover Bill and his dog, and finally drives the
squatter “ into an asylum for insane paupers,”
** * *
The “Work Girl” ends its unhonored
career this week. The partially-naked, flying Sem-
ite will fly for the last time, and Westinacott will
wheel his superfluous fruit barrow across the stage
to its final disappearance on Friday night.’ On
Saturday the Co. will produce “The Enemy’s
Camp,” which has a fine sound about it
suggestive of the breaking down of the commissariat
waggon in the distance, and the bang and clatter
of war, and Sweeny going off at furious speed for
reinforcements, and the large heroic trumpeter
blowing his very vitals into his brassy instrument,
and. the general calling out from the rear to charge
and disembowel everybody and everything. If
“ The Enemy’s Camp ” is as good as it sounds, it
should be a pleasant change from several things
that have happened lately.
*# * *
The Sydney Criterion co. is still living up
to the good report which this paper gave it last
week, and “Mixed” seems good for some time
longer, if required. No news about a change of
programme has so far come to hand.
** * *
A considerable portion of the Rickards co.
having left Sydney for another sphere of useful-
ness, it was considered necessary to pour oil on
the lacerated feelings of the public by turning on
the valuable Coleman once more at the Tivoli.
Unless something totally unexpected intervenes,
therefore, that serious-looking person will have
again laid his astonishing legs before the com-
munity by the time these remarks appear in print.
La Belle Maie is also announced. This lady is
new to Sydney, and that city has gashed itself a
good deal while shaving during the last week or
two, through wondering what she may be like.
** * *
The Opera House, about the most con-
sistently unlucky in Sydney, is to be
re-opened next Saturday by the Cogill Brothers
with another variety co. The Bulletin respects
the pluck of these brothers—but its opinion about
their discretion is simply unpublishable.
** * *
Sydney Music Hall, out of an undue
desire for variety, has lately turned on a Highland
sword-dance person, and a Professor who swings
the Indian clubs. The Indian club business, to
The Bulletin’s eye, has always seemed to be
merely a sad, purposeless agitation of wood ; and
the sword-dance, when done in all its beautiful
entirety, looks like a man with an obsolete weapon
gone raving mad in petticoats. However, this is
only a casual opinion offered without profound
study of the subject; and the rest of the programme
is good enough to atone for these things.
** * *
“ Notorious Mrs. Ebbsmith,” lately holding
forth to sympathetic sisters in Sydney, carried her
troubles to Melb. Princess’s, last Saturday night,
and got a very favorable hearing. It is as the
heroine of a play for ladies that Mrs. Ebbsmith
may prove a sound B. and. B. investment. She
possesses a large intellect, yet she pitches a Bible
into a burning fiery furnace, which is foolish, and
immediately snatches the singed volume out again
and hugs it to her bosom, which is a mere act
of Bible idolatry, inconsistent with deep
thinking. She has been educated up (or
down) to haremic pleasures by a man whose
memory she despises, nevertheless the ardor
of the one she loves doesn’t seem to appeal to
those old-established feelings. Mrs. Ebbsmith
is quite a genuine womanly woman, made to ap-
pear an oddity by unusual circumstances. She
admits, with her bare elbows resting uncon-
cernedly upon a hot stove, that her sex has found
her out. Then she rebels against her natural
impulses, at the bidding of a petty Rev. Amos
and his priggish sister, and flees to a dreary
vicarage, where she devotes her lively intellect to
the subject of Jonah and the whale, and the con-
templation of cheap pictures of bogus saints. Or
perhaps she returns finally to her Lucas. On the
other hand, perhaps she doesn’t.
* * * *
The story of Mrs. Ebbsmith may have
strong human interest for women, but it doesn’t
agitate the other fellows. An ordinary man, un-
happily, thinks very little more about the true in-
wardness of female character than a rooster
worries about a hen’s. The keenest male student
of this fascinating subject can get no further than
the discovery that a woman’s sex is always finding
her out and playing pranks with her. Having
discovered the existence of the mystery, he says
“ Amen ! ” and closes the book for ever, if he be
wise. That cold-blooded, commercial-minded
romancer, Rider Haggard, commenting on the
simple cussedness of women, suggests that men,
unable to weigh female motives in the
scales of their own experience, must con-
sole themselves with the reflection that
the inexplicable is generally not worth under-
standing. Pinero’s play holds up a mirror wherein
the fair sex may catch reflections of its cussedness.
The sex will like “Notorious Mrs. Ebbsmith,”
consequently a good many men will be dragged
thither. To the present writer’s way of thinking,
this gloomy play is admirable, on account of the
dialogues, until religion comes snuffling into the
third apt. Then it grows irritating. But how
like a woman ! A clever Angelina is a delightful
circumstance as long as she keeps Rev. Amos at
bay, not longer.
* * * *
Boucicault’s naughty old Duke promises to
be one of his first-class representations anon,
though his Grace thus far strikes one as being
more amusing than real. Miss Faber is always at
home in a truly Kerristian character, but Cecil
Ward and Miss Spong are neither very real nor
amusing. Mrs. Brough, without a doubt, fails to
make the most, or nearly the most, of her great
part. She plays to the gods, and is plainly acting
when naturalism is most required of her. Never-
theless, Australia doesn’t hold another lady who
could perform Mrs. Brough’s task with equal
effect, also Cecil Ward is about the best man pro-
curable for the part of Lucas Cleeve. So, m a
comparative way of speaking, “Notorious Mrs.
Ebbsmith ” is excellently interpreted.
** * *
B. and B.’s advt. extracts from the “ Mrs.
Ebbsmith ” dialogue are calculated to tickle
womankind, “There’s only one hour in a
woman’s life—a moment when circumstance taps
her upon the shoulder and says ‘ Woman, this
hour is the best that earth can spare to you.’ It
may come to her in calm or in tempest, &c., &c.”
The Bulletin’s idea is that the Hour aforesaid
always comes to her in tempest, if her best boy is
anyway equal to the occasion. For, after all, the
hour depends upon the man—
“ The Sixth Commandment ” gives indi-
cations of running into a third week at Melb.
Alexandra. Dan Barry’s cheerful horror is reminis-
cent of “ Mrs. Ebbsmith ” in two awful particulars.
There’s a parson in it, likewise a furnace, and the
parson commits a ship’s officer to the fire in exactly
the same way as Mrs. Brough starts to destroy her
good book. He opens the iron door and pops
the gentleman upon the glorious coals. But he

Charles Godfrey’s clever treatment of
several not very brilliant music-hall songs is
making him liked at Melb. Opera House. Young
Australia doesn’t shout itself hoarse over the jingo
song, “Balaclava,” with its handsome scenic
trimmings, but the “ actor vocalist, travelling
solely on his own individual merits, impresses the
boys a good deal. Slade Murray’s imitation of God-
frey’s style and voice has discounted the original
man’s novelty to almost the same extent
that Thornton spoilt Edward Terry s effect in
“Sweet Lavender” when he produced it here
years after the mimic had played it out. But in
Godfrey’s case there is the redeeming fact that his
imitator didn’t beat him at any point.

It is customary to mourn the departed
theatrical glories of Melbourne’s boom-time, when
the first Gaiety Co., headed by Leslie, barren,
Letty Lind and Sylvia Grey, brought johnnies to
ruin, and the price of a dress-circle seat was 50
per cent, higher than normal. Yet, as a matter
of fact, seven weeks of “ Djin-Djin ” yielded more
coin to the Princess’s treasury than the first seven
weeks of the first Gaiety Co.

Theatrically, all is chaos in Adelaide.
The performing bears have GONE.
WOMAN'S HOUR.
A sudden clasp of hand in hand,
A cling of mouth to mouth,
And Love makes beautiful the land—
North, east, and west, and south.
The air seems filled with fragrance sweet
Distilled from passion’s flower :
Life is a wondrous fair conceit
In Woman’s fleeting hour:
A poem, whispered to her ear
By Love, in softest tone
Not heard by him her heart holds dear—
The joy is hers alone—
Though Man, a creature gross and mean,
Soon turning sweetness sour,
Remains the painter of the scene—
The author of the Hour.
At Poverty Point.
Holdar M. : “ Isn’t it about time “ THE profession ” re-
laxed its tenacious hold on that synonym for Depression?
I am a patient man, but constant allusion to that weary
term by weather-beaten, egg-battered derelicts of this
order whose toes protrude from their boots as they ex-
citedly extol the shallow pomps and vanities of their
craft—l nearly said graft—chafes even my abject spirit.”
Amj’ Sherwin, the now celebrated Tas. singer, was
reared at Forest Home, in the lovely Huon district. When
the Cagli-Pompei opera - troupe was in Tasmania, Fred
Packer, now in Sydney, told Pompei he knew of a girl
who had a lovely voice, and that if Pompei liked he would
undertake that she would sing in Donizetti’s “ Pasquale.”
Within three weeks from that date, old Pompei gladly
agreed to the proposal, in which he knew there must be
money ; and Backer coached Miss Sherwin, who did sing
in “ Pasquale ” as arranged. Success followed success,
and that was how Amy’s big career had its beginning,
till now the fair Tasmanian holds her own amongst the
first singers of the day.
An interesting dispute went on at Sydney Lyceum
betweeu Mrs. Brough and Dot Boucicault anent
the wayward Mrs. Ebbsmith. The actress holds, and
gives effect to the conviction in playing, that “ Mad
Agnes” is a cold, callous, loveless creature at heart,
whereas manager Boucicault insists that she is intended
to be a woman full of love and passion, with these feelings
temporarily frozen over by her hard struggle against
conventionality and its brutal ways. Author Pinero has
been appealed to as to which conception he intended.

Patrick Campbell at every possible point, will do Fedora
in Melb.—a part that Mrs. Pat. didn’t illuminate over-bril-
liantly in London recently. When shall we see Mrs. B.
as Juliet?

In the critical eye of George Bernard Shaw, there is but
one actress in the world, and her name is Janet Achurch.
Shaw writes all his leading female characters round this
clever woman. When Dot Boucicault was in London a
year ago, G. B. S. was anxious that he should produce a
new play he had written -a marvellously-clever play, says
D. B.—called “ Katrina.” Boucy didn’t see the fun of
risking money in a London venture at that time, but
offered to do the thing well in Australia. “Then you
must take Janet Achurch for the leading part,” insisted
Shaw, “ or leave the. play alone altogether.” With Mrs.
Brough here, Boucicault couldn t very well import
the Ibsenite actress, so Shaw’s play won’t happen in Aus-
tralia.

Dear Bulletin, —Your par. about a theatrical super-
stition that ill-luck followed Nelly Barren after a gift of
lyre-bird’s feathers recalled to me some incidents on board
the Liguria in ’BB, when I was a fellow-passenger of Miss
Barren and Mr. Fred. Leslie. Miss Barren had a great
dread of the number 13, and told us how she had, on
leaving London, invited some friends to a farewell supper.
At the last moment several were unable to attend, and,
after they had sat down, they found they were 13 ! While
she was relating the story I counted the people at the
captain’s table, and found that we were 13 1 This quite
upset Miss Barren, and the captain asked one of the
officers to make the fourteenth ; but several times during
the voyage we were again 13. During the trip Miss
Farren had a bad attack of gout, for which she
blamed the “ fatal 13.” The late Geo. Vining (once
manager of London Princess’s), if a play was mentioned to
him on a Friday would have nothing to do with it; but a
horseshoe was to him the emblem of unfailing good luck.
Once when he was coming down to Kent to the reading of
a new play of my father’s, my brother and myself (two
small children) placed two horseshoes on the path leading
from the station to our house. George Vining arrived
jubilant, with the horseshoes hanging on his walking-
stick, and accepted the drama without hearing it read.
Whether he blessed those horseshoes afterwards I don’t
remember. —Yours, Roland Watts Phillips.
Rudyard Kipling, writing to a Wellington (M.L.) friend
by last mail, dissipates a few current rumors re himself.
He hasn’t gone to India to “ write up ” that country for
the U.S. magazines, but is living at Naulakha, Waite,
Wyndham, County Vermont. “ Another cheerful error ”
he says, “ is the statement that ‘ Smythe’ (who the deuce
is Smythe ?) has got me down as a lecturer ! He may have
me down, but I don’t think he’ll ever get me up on the
platform.”
“ The Trilby Dance,” a big feature in Drury Lane
panto., goes to music written by a Victorian lady—Mrs.

(Melb.). She is now devoting herself to music. Mrs.
Stewart is money-lender David Henry’s daughter.
At one Paris music-hall Mdlle. Alengon, a very hand-
some woman, comes on with nothing upon her but a
chemise and a diamond necklace, and explains her lack of
garments by saying she has lost all her money in the
Transvaal, and has only what she stands up in. Then the
audience cheers as one man.

Talking of Cyril Tyler, and his rapid change from a
freaky soprano into a legitimate tenor. About four years
ago, at a Sunday Masonic Service in Melbourne Town
Hall, the then well-known chorister boy, Horace Stevens
sang a treble solo very charmingly. Brother Truelove’
who conducted the musical service on that occasion, was
lately called upon to arrange a similar entertainment in
aid of some Melbourne lodge. Moreover, he was particu-
larly desired to let them have another solo from the
youngster who sang so sweetly of yore, Masons in general,
it seemed, being under the impression that a boy’s treble
endures for the term of his natural life. Truelove fed the
delusion with a solemn assurance that the same young
artist should certainly appear again, which promise was;
fulfilled. The audience heard a rather tall, slim young
man of 19, or so, roll forth a solo in a deep baritone.
That was the original Horace Stevens, with four years’
growth added to him. He has developed a fine man’s
voice, and is likely to make a name for himself anon.

Sims Reeves will, The Bulletin’s London correspondent
thinks, be another disillusionment to Australians. The
shining star has almost become a cold cinder. Like
another leonine-headed patriarch, Reeves has just married
a young wife, and his vocal energy has not been increased
thereby. He sings to a piano accompaniment and
entirely to his own “ time.” The usually-noisy music-hall
is hushed during his items, which are gasped forth, the
notes being economically restrained. No attempting to
“ bring” the audience with a final C. Just a clear, cor-
rect, almost sweet, slow appeal to Maud, for, I suppose,
the ten-thousandth time, to come out into the snailwalk.
But the “ house ” gives him an ovation every time.

“ Memento Mori,” by Clement Scott, in remembrance
of his friends, Arthur Dacre and Amy Roselle :
Come, crimson Death, with poppy-lidded eyes,
Kiss me with open mouth, perfumed with dreams !
Love’s land at last! Pure Love that never dies,
Our world is what it is, not what it seems !
Creep closer still, give me thy drowsy breath,
Touch me ! For this is Life ! It is not Death !

the pockets of the two Adelaide discoverers,
Durham Doolette, son of G. Boulder Doolette, and
Charley Northmore. Britishers cwn a large slice
of country there, which accounts for so little being

to be one of the best reefing fields (reefs opened
up average 40*.) in that land groaning under the
incubus of the six families.—Aov r.
The Wild- Cut Coiumn.
T he Bank of North Queensland has had only a
.’moderately good time for the half-year ended 3 a Dec.
The net profits are returned at £llB2, against -11736 for
previous half, and 1 1674 for the one ending Dec. 31, ’94.
The profit is only equal to a div. of a little over 2 per
cent, per annum, so the board has wisely added it to the
previous balance, making 3945 carried forward. The
B.N.Q. has released all its reconstructed deposits, and a
large portion of them have been paid in cash. The whole
deposits are now only £191,755, compared with 250,488 a
year ago, and the bank has £79,990 in liquid assets, equal
to nearly 40 per cent, on all liabilities, which is almost
the biggest reserve in Australasia. Expenses are being
steadily cut down ; the profits are being used to gradually
write down the assets to bedrock prices; and the release
of the fixed deposits will make a further great saving in
interest, so things ought to look much better before long.
And, considering that the unpaid capital alone (saying
nothing of the £454,000 of cash and other assets) would
cover all the liabilities to the public and leave a big
margin, the depositors are in a safer position than those
of almost any other bank in Australasia.
* * * *
South Australia, which already has a debt equal
to £62 10s. per inhabitant, is floating another loan of
£839,500—in London, as usual. It is offered at 3 per
cent., with a minimum of £94, and, after paying all
expenses, even if the loan sells at £95 all round, the
interest will probably amount to at least 3.) per cent.—
probably more, considering the loss at redemption.
Just now, there is some £25,000,000 of gold lying in the
Australian banks, about £7,000,000 of which is idle
simply because it can’t find a safe and readily-realisable
investment at any price ; and, under these circumstances,
it is at least 10 chances to 1 that the S.A. loan would
have been taken up as cheaply in this country as in
England. The Australian money-market is quite as much
overstocked, in proportion to its size, as that of England ;
and the saving of the 12s. 6d. per cent, stamp-duty pay-
able to the J. Bull Government, and the cost of remitting
interest each half-year to London, are two big items in
favor of flotation in Australia. Taking all into account,
a loan floated at £92 here would be about as good as one
at £94 in England. Also, local flotation would have kept
£25,000 a year interest in the country without diverting a
single penny from any other enterprise, the money being
now absolutely idle and profitless. Under these conditions,
any Government that was cap >ble of getting out of the
ancient financial rut would at least have made the attempt
to do its financing locally. The idea of borrowing (."800,000
of British money and bringing it into a country which has
already some £7,000,000 in hand which it doesn’t know
what to do with, is more than grotesque.
* * * *
Montgomerie's Brewery Co. (Melbourne) made
a loss for last half-year of £1660. Previous half-year
the profit was only £49; for the half ending 31st
December, 1894, it was £l7B, and for the one ending June,
’94 it was £922. Therefore, in two years it has made a net
loss of £5ll, and it has also written £BOOO off its previous
balance to cover depreciation of assets, so it may be
said to have dropped £B5ll, or very near it, in 24 months.
Also, it has an overdraft, which has been practically
stationary for a long time, of £68,000, and the interest on
this is weighingitdown heavily. Yet the “goodwill” of the
concern is set down as an asset worth £25,000, and if this
alleged asset were taken out, the co. would have a short-
age of over £22,000. Query : what man would pay £25,000
for the privilege of losing £1660 in one half-year? One
M‘Gregor is chairman of this erstwhile great but now
somewhat melancholy beer-concern—-the same M‘G., The
Bulletin believes, who lately declared that no country
ever prospered unless Scotchmen were at the head of
affairs.
* * * •
The practice of setting down “goodwill ” as an
asset is generally unsound. The goodwill is sometimes
valuable if the co. sells its business as a going concern ; if
the co. bursts and goes into liquidation," it is almost
always absolutely worthless. Therefore, goodwill, in per-
haps nine cases out of ten, is an asset which is only valu-
able when it isn’t wanted, and is of no use at all when it
is wanted. And that kind of an asset is always a thing to
be viewed in a depressed spirit.
* * * *
The Vic. revenue fell behind the estimate by
£65,340 in the first half of the financial year, but in
January it suddenly got ahead of the estimate by £57,940,
so that the shortage is now only £7400, and if some rich
man only dies Premier Turner still has hopes that he
may not only make ends meet, but pay off £250,000 of the
old deficit. New South Wales revenue, for the six
months ended 31st December, kept well ahead of the
estimates, but in January it fell £63,000, and, though it is
still ahead of the estimate, this sudden shock makes
Premier Reid wear an anxious expression behind his eye-
glass. If there are no more shocks, however, everything
will be pretty right.
* * * *
When the Commercial Bank of Australia
appeals to its depositors for a reduction of interest, it
will have five strong points in its favor which no other re-
constructed bank has yet possessed. (1) It has called up
every penny of its available capital, and, if the de-
positors squeezed it, they couldn’t thereby get any
more out of the shareholders than is being got out of them
already. (2) Probably they would get less ; the share-
holders are paying up remarkably well while the bank is
going, but, if it stopped, a lot of them would compel the
liquidators to fight at law for every cent of their money.
(3) Liquidation would mean the permanent stoppage of
the present business, which is worth, even now, a good
deal per annum, which mostly goes to the depositors. (4)
It would mean forcing the sale of the assets in a market
which can hardly get worse by waiting, and which may
get better. (5) The worse that can happen to the ordi-
nary shareholders, over and above what is happening
already, is the loss of their shares, and, as these are
wholly valueless and pay no dividends, and seem likely to
continue that way for a very long time, and as a lot of the
ordinary capital may have to be written off in any case,
the fear of losing their shares has no more terrors for
many of them than the fear of losing their mother-in-law.
So, if the depositors turn nasty, a lot of the ordinary
shareholders will probably tell them to go right ahead
and be .

cial Bank, before and since reconstruction, says chairman
Service, is its purity from scandal of any sort. The

Balmain New Ferry Co. had a traffic revenue
last year of £9730 —decidedly the best return up to date—
and seems to be in a highly prosperous state. Neverthe-
less, its balance-sheet is a remarkable affair. It pays alO
per cent, div., yet it has not fully paid off its prelimi-
nary ex.’s ; and such an alleged asset as “ preliminary
expenses, £676,” is a thing to be wiped out with all pos-
sible speed, even if the div. has to be kept down to 8 per
cent, to do it. Another asset is “ articles of association,
£33 18s. 3d,” which looks as if it should have been written
off as expenses. Articles of association are not an espe-
cially saleable asset. The co. has an insurance-fund of
exactly £lOO, which is a miserably small guarantee
upon £ll,OOO worth of floating property, seeing that one
little collision might send £4OOO to the bottom in five
minutes. The reserve for maintenance of steamers (£500)
and reserve for equalisation of dividends (£300) are un-
objectionable, but as these reserves and the div. absorb
£240 more than the year’s net profits, they look a trifle
ridiculous. Moreover, the general reserve-fund, which
was 1332 at the end of ’94, is now set down at only £304,
and the difference has apparently been put among the
receipts and used to swell the profits and the div. A
board which goes fooling with the reserve in this un-
authorised manner evidently knows nothing of its busi-
ness. Last year a div. of £342 was declared, and yet the
amount is charged in the balance-sheet as £4lO, and
what has become of the difference is wholly unexplained.
The boats at December, ’94, were valued at £12,239, and,
after writing off £1032 for depreciation, they appear, in
next balance-sheet, at £11,147, which doesn’t square by a
long way. The reserves, according to the articles of the
association, must be invested in the Govt. Savings Bank
or in Govt, debentures, but no such investment is shown.
The chairman hasn’t signed the balance-sheet as required
by the articles. There is no reserve-fund account at all.
“ Furniture, turnstiles and general plant” in ’94 stood at
£134 4s. 7d., and, after writing off £l9 6s. 6d. for deprecia-
tion, they stand now at £l7O Bs. Id., which needs some
explanation that certainly isn’t afforded. Altogether, the
Balmain New Ferry Co.’s balance-sheet is a marvellous
affair, and the co. seems to be run on pure pick-and-
shovel principles.
* * ♦ »
The Australasian Building and Investment
Society (Melbourne), which, when last heard from, had
£5300 less assets than liabilities, has taken a short cut out
of its difficulties with its depositors. It owed £lO,OOO to
these individuals, most of whom received an exorbitant
rate of interest as interest goes nowadays, so the directors
advanced the needful amount of their own money at 4
per cent, and paid off the lot. Also, they offer to reduce
the interest on this advance if necessary. This wholly
unusual kind of director ought to be encouraged. The
average director hurries to get his money out at a crisis
instead of putting it in.

to roost. The eight-hours clause has been under violation
for some time, and now Premier Turner is going to talk to
the co. and consult his law-officers. As the Tramway Co.
depends on cutting down its wages-sheet and stretching
the time-sheet for dividends, it is silently cursing Gillies’s
memory.
+ * * f
Perth (W.A.) City Council is having still more
experience of what private enterprise means. The
Council lately asked the Govt, to terminate the trouble
with the Water-Supply Co. by buying the latter out.
Meanwhile the co., scenting trouble, sold out to another
co., engineered by the energetic H. M. Deakin, of Sydney
land-boom celebrity. The Govt, is now willing to buy at
the price which would have been offered to the original
co., but the new co. wouldn’t be headed by Deakin unless
it wanted a profit on its bargain, and the result is a price
which Govt, considers prohibitive. There matters stand
at present.
* * * *
As a safeguard the N.S.W. steamship owners
put an “L” and “G” in some obscure part of their
tickets, which is supposed to mean “ Lady” and “Gentle-
man.” Hence, if one of the other sex presents the wrong
ticket, the co. will know the ticket has changed hands,
and will refuse to carry. The only punishment the holder
of the transferred ticket has to bear is the loss of passage.
The co’s could not prosecute, as transferring tickets
is no crime ; and, unlike the Railway Commissioners, the
co.’s have no officially-authorised by-law to rely on.
» * * *
The Yic. Supreme Court deeision that a credi-
tor’s judgment against a married woman cannot be
enforced by imprisonment capsizes a pretty kettle of fish.
There are numbers of married women in business in their
own names in and around Melb., especially in the bung-
trade, the husband keeping dark in some other line.
Knowing the risks they ran, and the natural facility of
married women in quietly disposing of assets, suppliers
have always relied on the Fraudulent Debtors’ Act to
make the debtors part. Judge Holroyd’s decision adds
another industry to the list of “ how to live on the
never ” in Melbourne.
• * * *
A recent English decision (under the Companies
Act) makes “ auditors ” officers of the company. Impor-
tant, especially in view of the hints that it is a step
towards making directors also officers of the company
from which they receive their salary or wages or
honorarium.
* * * *
The worst of the sweaters are large dairy-
farmers, who retail their milk in Melb. They make their
farm-laborers work about 15 hours a day for 10s. per
week, and the food they give them is of the coarsest des-
cription. The men they employ in town are not treated
much better.

from £lO3 to £lO3 10s., expecting a good rise when the
investment strengthens again.

“ Glowing accounts are given by the prospectors
of the new Eldorado lying—good word !—75 miles n. of
Lake Carey. The reefs are said to be ‘ studded with
gold.’ The Hawk’s Nest is described as a second Hannan’s
for lode formation, whilst the well-defined quartz-reefs
are wonderfully rich in both coarse and fine gold.” This
and a lot more rich finds all appear in one Coolgardie
wire. If Eastern people could only be brought to believe
these things the Broken Hill boom would be a fool to the
Westralian one. But in face of so much richness going on
now for three solid years, it is inexpressibly sad to remem-
ber that even poor N.S.W. turns out more gold.
♦ * * *
Particulars of some recent Westralian mine
flotations, taken from Clarke and Co.’s share-list, are in-
teresting. The Black Prince (Coolgardie) is a fair sample
of about 10 such promising ventures. 100,000 shares in
the co. ; 30,000 absorbed by the vendors and promoters,
and 20,000 reserved by the co., leaving 50,000 for the
public. A shilling per share paid up yields £2500, of
which the vendors take £2OOO. The mine starts with
£5OO, which has to support an office and a secretary, in
addition to working the reef.
* * * *
The wires from Westralia announcing that
miners are wanted at £6 per week should be looked on
with the utmost incredulity.
* * * *
A Westralian mine, sold in London for about
£lOO,OOO was (The Bulletin is told by an expert highly
honored in mining and other circles who has personal evi-
dence of some of the facts) floated on specimens obtained
three miles away. The man who reported on it for the
London buyers got 400 guineas. He also got 500 guineas
from the sellers. The two men who salted the alleged
show were carefully exported to their native land. It
would be rather hard to guess the name of that costly
duffer —because there are such a lot like it in similar case.
* * * *
Premier mine, 25-Mile, Coolgardie, first crush-
ing with the new battery, 67 tons, 1540 z.; “ but the
blanketings are yet to be treated.” How much blanket-
ings will it give ? probably not a ton. “ The manager ex-
pects a better average next crushing, and he has 100 tons
on the surface equal to that put through.” How some
bosses must bless the newspaper amateur mining man ! If
the stone is only of equal value, how can they get a better
average. It is always the next crushing which is going to
make the cake.
* * * *
Old John Wallace (Vic.) explains London boom-
mining by saying that mines that would pay well on
a capital of £50,000 or £60,000, are floated for £200,000, to
enable the promoters to grab the difference.
* » * *
Business on Perth Stock Exchange is so utterly
killed by the telegraphic collapse that on two days of last
week not one sale was effected. No one can get up-to-
date news from the mines, for even the hard-driven
camel, though a lot faster than the W.A. telegraph, can’t
keep the city posted on the daily and hourly fluctuations.
No one can get information that is less than a week old as
to prices in Adelaide or Melbourne. And as no one cares
to buy into a mine that may have gone bung last week,
or to give £1 a share when the Adelaide price may have
dropped, for all he knows to the contrary, to 2s. 6d.,
things are dead.
« * * *
One of the clearest and most truthful statements
concerning W.A. mines has been made by a South Aus-
tralian, recently returned. He says, of Hannan’s, that
there are some English mines doing good, quiet work.
The early disastrous purchases made by English buyers
have evoked a spirit of mistrust and caution, and there
will be no more buying pigs-in-a-poke for some time.
Even the good things are crippled by the heavy tolls
taken by the London promoters. The market has been
smothered by holders of free-shares shovelling them on to
it. The idea of development is subservient altogether
to the idea of trading in shares. “ How is the market ? ”
—not “ How is the mine?”—is the question most eagerly
asked, although, for developments, it is worth while to
watch and see if the miners are operating at the “ evening
open-call ” sale at Kalgoorlie.

From Cue : “ The Warden’s Court was crowded
on Tuesday, when a large number of exemption cases were
dealt with. The Golconda North and Fortune of War
mines applied for three months’ exemption, but were only
granted one month. The Francis Reward, Cue Victory,
and Orient were each granted one month’s exemption.
The Lake Austin was refused an exemption.” Ominous.

The biggest liars have got away with the biggest

* * * *
On a sulphide basis nothing is cheaper than
Broken Hill Proprietaries. People have got so used
to looking on the big mine as a phenomenon that when
they find its sulphides not much, if any, better than the
average in the smaller mines they are disappointed. But,
after all, it is the size of the property that deceives. The
Junction has a wonderful body in its western develop-
ment ; but investors and speculators might be startled
if they saw the vast hole that has already been made in it
—the result of practically all work being concentrated on
one spot. It is not possible to concentrate either all work or
all attention upon one patch of the big mine, with its
three blocks traversed from end to end by the lode ; so
that a patch quite as big as the Junction’s appears a
small thing in such a setting. It may not be that the
Proprietary has a body as rich as the Junction’s ; but
underlying its kaolin patches it certainly has many thou-
sands of tons of very rich sulphides quite undrawn upon
so far, not to mention the vast bodies of fair-grade stuff in
other parts. And, meantime, it, and it alone of Broken
Hill mines, has some carbonate ore to speak of. It has
still probably as much as any two others, bar Block 14,
ever had together—which is a considerable thing to say.
At the same time, it must not be forgotten that the B.H.
Proprietary Co. is in 960,000 shares, and that a ss. per
share rise means over a quarter of a million added to the
market-value.
« * * *
During 1895 eighteen men were killed while
working at the Broken Hill mines. Also, 18 mining-
dividends were paid. In 1894 the fatal accidents num-
bered only 9. The South disaster brought up the past
year’s figures.
* * * »
The B. H. South, The Bulletin said the other
week, ought to be paying small divs., as even before the
accident in its workings, when eight men lost their lives
seven months ago, it was understood to have money in
hand. A comparison of the workings then and now gives
some answer to the question why divs. have not been
paid. The mine is now a forest of timber. General
manager Gluyas obviously dreads another collapse.
Perhaps a good deal might be saved if there were better
means of introducing mullock from the surface to fill up
with. As it is, while the management is praiseworthily
protecting the workmen, most of the profits that had
been counted upon are certainly going to the timber-
merchants.
* * * *
The million slump in Broken Hills has made
small meat of Melb. brokers, all of whom were more or
less holders at from 60s. to 655. Also, Adelaide’s million-
aire brewer, Sims, must feel rather sick. He was about
the heaviest Australian buyer at 60s. and 61s. Apart from
the yield-drop, Britishers, who snapped up all the big par-
cels offering at 30s. and thereabouts, have been unloading
for their profit.

B.H. North concentrating mill is at work, run-
ning smoothly. May Bros,, of Gawler, will hardly make

well. B.H. Central (Ashcroft process syndicate’s mine)

Shareholders in Baker’s Creek Extended have
refused an offer from London for the mine, surrounded
with conditions in favor of the promoters. The prospect
is a good one for a co. with a solid working capital ; it is
valueless without.
« * * *
•More jumping at Yalwal, N.S.W.—this time
the Golden Quarry, the present big-patch claim of the
field. It will probably be a long time before Yalwal
places a mine in London. The Homeward Bound mine
was just placed there on a solid basis when the news came
that the mine had been jumped, and all negotiations were
declared off. The time is nearing when London will de-
cline Jo deal with any Australian mine until the law with
respect to jumping has been completely changed. Which
is the one and only good reason for leaving the law as it is.

M.L.C. “Johnny ” Wallace (Vic.) tells a Melb.
interviewer how, disheartened by non-success, he was on
the point of “chucking” prospecting at Beechworth
when a mate s wife persuaded him to have another try,
and got her husband to finance him a fortnight’s rations.
™luck turned, and to-day he is worth close on a
million. W.A. rushers, he says, are leaving a better
country" than they are'goipg to. Ventures that he owns,
or is the big shareholder in, employ 1500 men. “ Johnny”
is plucky, as witness him putting £24,000 into a Rowdy
Flat (Yackandandah) sluicing claim, which didn’t turn
in a penny last year, but is now paying.

The Bulletin asks the Mount Lyell people
to explain these confusing reports re the trend of the
pyrites lode wall in the South drive, No. 3 tunnel : “Nov.
16, wall making a sharp turn to the west ” (right). (No
change reported till after the new year.) “Jan. 6, wall
turning to the left ” (south). “ Jan. 18, pyrites sOM making
away to the right.” “ Feb. 10, wall appears to be making
back again to the left.” Little things like this make
people suspicious.

People tempted to plunge among the countless
Mt. Lyell pups should desist until they have visited the
Mount itself and travelled thence per boot to the particu-
lar pup whispered into their ear by the voice of the
charmer. To do this is calculated to make a man careful.

The large damp hand of British capital is falling
heavily on Reefton (M.L.). A British syndicate is negoti-
ating for the purchase of the Keep-it-Dark, No. 2 Keep-it-
Dark, Welcome, Globe, Wealth of Nations, Energetic and
Progress mines, and if this arrangement is completed—
which seems almost certain—the Big River will be almost
the only important mine at Reefton which hasn’t been
swallowed up by the all-devouring J. Bull.
* * * *
Cyanide is asserting itself at Croydon more than
on any other Australian field. Last year it increased the
gold output by nearly 30%. Last month one co. handled
2200 tons of tailings. Cost’ of treatment, including royalty,
6s. 6d.; profit, 18s. 6d. per ton. At one time Croydon
ore was thought too coppery for cyanide, but practice has
shown it remarkably well adapted.
♦ * » »
Dear Bulletin, —The artificial manufacture of
pearls, i.e., designs in pearl, is an old dodge, centuries
old, and was played on the Catholic missionaries not
many years after their first visit to the East. The'Malays
used to produce crosses and virgins to order, to the
amazement of the Christians, who regarded them as indi-
cations of the Creator's approval of the missions. But
the “man from the Bahamas” overlooks the time re-
quired for the growth. It will take at least three years,
and, as the bivalves frequently shift their grounds, pad-
dogking them is necessary to success.—Shark’s Bay,
W.A.
* * * *
A prospector at Swift’s Creek (Vic.) had a battle
with a mountain cat at the bottom of his shaft recently.
When he let himself down the rope he found the beast
waiting, and after much trouble transfixed it with a pick.
A real “ Wild Cat ” item. The prospector thinks badly of
the omen.

established in N.S.W., any trade-protection society which
can’t pretty accurately size a man up will be much behind
the times. Most other Govt, departments “leak” a lot,
anyhow.

CAMEO
A recent wedding, when a youth of
23 escorted to the canopy a wealthy bride of 55,
has occasioned much speculation as to the manner
in which the terms of the damsel’s late father’s
will are to be carried out. Said will decrees, among
other strange injunctions, that the two surviving
daughters are to share each other’s company
always. The old man thought they were confirmed
old maids, but Cupid is a sad wag sometimes.
* * * *
A Tasmanian paper hears that Lord
Brassey has bestowed an annuity on Mrs. Fancy,
a Tas. widow who lives on the roadside near the
now historic Don Hill. When touring the N.W.
coast of the island one of the Brassey party —Mrs.
Freeman Thomas —fell off her bicycle, damaging
the permanent way and tearing her uniform.
Mrs. Fancy was on hand with needle and thread
and sticking-plaster to mend the lady. Every old
woman who lives near a country road affected by
touring cyclists from Guvmentouse will now forth-
with procure repairing material and ait on a stone-
heap awaiting windfalls in the shape of damaged
pedallers.
* * * *
The Brasseys and suite are a compact
family party, and make few personal friends.
SANS CASH.
The Minister for the Interior ignores a deputation.
There is not likely to be a regular Govt. House
gang this time, as there was under the Hopetoun
regime. The party go round on their bicycles
and H.E. talks in a fatherly way to anybody
and everybody, but their visits and invitations
are mostly official. To aggravate the annoyance
of the “Toorak push,” Lady Brassey has shown a
distinct preference for the daughter of the family
of a wealthy retired draper.
* * * *
The proceedings of the Lormer Magisterial
Investigation Board at Melbourne suggest many
tilings. What would be the effect of a similar
enquiry in Sydney? Many rumors are in every-
one’s mouth as to the conduct of certain magis-
trates.
* * * *
Despite many threats of exposure, Mel-
bourne jury-squarers are still active as ever.
Within the last two months a couple of glaring
instances have been brought under authoritative
notice, but the state of the law is alleged to make
proceedings difficult.
* * * *
Rapiport, J.P., gave the Lormer Board
(as Melb. journals seem to call it) a graphic
description of his interview with detective O’Don-
nell, at the time of Mrs. R.’s arrest on a charge of
stealing a watch and chain. O’Donnell, according
to this, said, “So help me God, and as I know
you’re a Mason, and by the Masonic law, I did
not know she was your wife.” Rapiport, in his
graphic description, afterwards said to O’Donnell,
“ You’re a lying scoundrel; you took a false oath,
a very solemn (Masonic) oath, that you did not
know it was my wife when you arrested her.” The
solemn oath aforesaid was registered in 1888.
O’Donnell gave a decidedly different story of his
conversation with Rapiport, and also stated that
he wasn’t a Mason previous to 1894.
* * * *
The saying used to be that no monetary
case could come before the Melb. bench sans a
Hebrew on it.
* * * *
The Westralian “ electric ” telegraph
again. A Melb. man wired from Albany to Fitz-
Gibbonville, announcing his home-coming per
next mail. He arrived before his wire, completed
his business, and, after a fortnight’s stay, returned
West. When he left Melb. his Westralian mes-
sage had not arrived. On reaching Albany, for
curiosity he dropped in at the telegraph-office to
enquire re the telegram and get his money back if
possible. “ When did you deposit it ? ” He gave
day and date. “Five weeks ago,” said the clerk ;
“ oh, you can’t stop it now —it must be at least half
way there by this time ! ”
* * * *
It is explained that the reason why those
Westralian messages don’t get through is that the
white ants, with which the country absolutely
swarms, eat them as fast as they come along the
wires.

You sometimes see more champagne than
water in Coolgardie. The laying of a foundation-
stone one recent afternoon “fizzled” itself into
.£3OO.
At Purakanui,
near Dunedin (M.L.),
the railway is built
in a spidery fashion
along the face of a
cliff overhanging the
sea. The rock rises
in a wall on one side,
and on the other you
can look out of the
carriage-window and
see the deep blue
water 150 or 200 feet
below. The line was
carried round this
way in the old days
instead of by an easier
and shorter road in-
land, because sundry
influential persons
held ground in the
vicinity. The other
day, a train was nego-
tiating this weird
jumping-off place
when engine No. 2
got off the rails and
jolted along the ex-
treme edge of the pre-
cipice. The other
engine and carriages
somehow remained
on the line, so the
whole affair didn’t go
overboard, as might
reasonably have been
expected. Then the
passengers got out,
and found that a bit
of the cliff had fallen

The billiard-room more than pays the rent
of a big Coolgardie pub., said pub. frequently re-
turning £2OO of bar gold a-day.
Chalmers Church, Melbourne, is in a
dilemma with <£60,000 of property, but no revenue
to pay the meenister’s salary. The kirk dates
back to the fifties, when Dr. Cairns refused to let
in a kist o’ whustles. At that time there was
plenty of revenue, though the property was worth
nothing in particular. R.C. Archbishop Carr
(the situation is indeed humorous) casts covetous
eyes on the property now, and is willing to go as
far as <£60,000. The Cathedral also wants to
£ather in the little bit of land that belongs to the
lutherans, and it keeps a patient eye on the other
handy block with the somnolent Unitarian church
on it.

Little is said of the many unfortunate
swagmen who have lately met awful deaths in the
N.S.W. bush. News of bodies or swags found
comes into the townships almost daily, and many
old identities on the Western tracks are missing,
In a station store on the Paroo there lie seven
swags found ownerless on the run.

THE FATE OF FATE.
Melb.
The rise and the fate of man,
Of universe, love and hate,
Innumerous seers relate;
But where is the pundit can
Inform us when fate began,
And what is the fate of Fate !

AT PER DOZEN.
The most important point for the consideration
of all persons who desire to possess photographs
of those nearest and dearest to them is with regard
to permanence rather than price. No photograph
that will turn yellow, break out in spots, or vanish
into nothingness within a few weeks can have its
value expressed in figures. When delivered, it
may shine like the enamelled mystery on a soap-
box, but the seeds of disease are there, and must
come to the surface. This may be regarded as
one of the certainties of cheap photography at per
dozen. _ _ _

* * * *
Melbourne “sassiety” is stumped for
sensations. A local chronicle of small beer
devotes a “stick,” or so, to Mrs. So-and-so’s
“merry-go-round party,” at St. Kilda. The see-
saw party comes next. And, by the way, here is
a suggestive little merry-go-round rhyme :
A. miserably futile person made three
attempts at suicide in one day recently at
Waverley (Sydney). First of all he set himself
on fire, but he didn’t burn well, possibly owing to
the accumulation of ash, or the dampness of the
fuel, or want of ventilation, or his generally-
incombustible nature —anyhow, he speedily went
out. Then he stabbed himself with a knife, but
not knowing the locality of his own vitals
nothing in particular came of it. Finally, he
threw himself into the sea, and at this stage the
police grew tired of his feeble proceedings and re-
moved him to tho hospital in custody. A man who
couldn’t kill himself once in three tries deserved
nothing better.
* * * *
He always retained a certain sense of the
decencies of life, did Jones. That, however, was
not his real name (he was an educated Englishman
“of exceedingly-good family ”), but it was the
name, under, over, by or through which he was
hanged. On the morning of the butchery,
three of them stood on the scaffold con-
demned to death for a particularly
brutal murder in company. The second
man was a Chinese, and the third an
ordinary average murderer. In his last
hour Jones sent for sheriff Ellis. “I
want you,” he said, “to grant me one
last favor, will you, sheriff ? ” “I will
if I can, Jones, what is it?’ “Don’t
hang me next to that —— Chow ; ” and Ellis,
when telling this tale, always adds as the
point of the story, “ And, by gad, I kept my
promise.”

When a husband gets damages from a
financial company, as happened the other day,
for loss of conjugal comforts through partial
destruction of his wife by the negligence of the
company aforesaid, what ought he to do with the
money? Just think hard over it and reply at
leisure.

• * * *
When the great flood came down the
Fitzroy River (Q.), the other day, the <£54,000
bridge, the pride and joy of Rockhampton, began
to groan horribly in the middle with the pressure
of the waters, and to totter in a most alarming
fashion. Then the Mayor and Corporation, or the
Divisional Board, or some other local authority,
rose to the occasion as great minds always do at a
crisis. It came down with a long rope, and tied
one end to the bridge and the other to a stump on
the bank. To all intents and purposes it might
as well have tied a runaway ironclad to an
envelope, or moored a hurricane to twopence, but
anyhow it had demonstrated the advantages of
Local Government and it seemed quite contented.

Apropos the terrible ferry accident at
Brisbane, The Bulletin wonders how many
“ disappearances ” there will be from that city.
There are at least two people in Sydney who were
reported missing in London after the Princess
Alice disaster.
Beyond a few life-belts, what means of pre-

Three bridges—each in its time the only bridge
connection between North and South Brisbane —
have been rendered unsafe for traffic by floods ;
two have been swept away altogether. Had the
Victoria Bridge not shown signs of collapse, the
Pearl ferry-boat would not have been daring that
flood. At all cost, at all trouble of overcoming
engineering difficulties, there must be a high-level
bridge.
* * * #
Many of the Westralian postal officials
deal in stamps, and strange whispers float about
Perth from time to time re alleged mysterious
losses from old-time stocks in the P.O. vaultr.
According to the Fremantle Messenger, on Nov.
4 last the Perth post-office suddenly discovered
that it was short of halfpenny stamps. As the
total sale of these stamps at Perth is only about
£2 worth per week, a few might easily have
been borrowed from another office, pending arrival
of a daily-expected supply from England. The
postal officials, however, ordered an issue of £5O
worth of old brown threepenny stamps, surcharged
as worth a halfpenny. The surcharge was to be
printed in red ink across the stamp, but, when 90
had been printed, it was found that red didn’t
show well on brown, so the remainder were printed
in green. This made the first 90 stamps unique ;
they were nearly all bought up by one P.O. official,
and are now worth about 30s. each. The remainder
were also snapped up by dealers, and, the new
supply of proper halfpennies coming to hand, the
makeshifts were withdrawn. One very big official-
dealer didn’t notice the value of the unique 90 at
first, and when he subsequently tried to get the
purchaser to let him into the spec, that heartless
underling refused.
*****
Theorised by Prof. Ray Lankester, in
Melb. Argus, that snakes are immunised against
snake-poison by absorption of poison from their
own glands. At first sight, this seems likely,
until it is remembered that only some snakes are
venomous. But the fact remains that non-venomous
snakes are also immune, as are also said to be
some lizards. This knocks Lankester’s notion
kite-high.
* « * »
The Adelaide Hospital Board, which has
been giving the Kingston Ministry so much
trouble, ceases to exist on Saturday, Feb. 29, and
few of its members will be re-appointed. Dr.
Way, the Premier’s chief antagonist, has already
been re-elected by the University, which he repre-
sents on the Board, but nearly all the other mem-
bers are nominated by the Cabinet.
*****
Bishop G-oe has brought three-fourths of
his clergy down on him by appointing a chubby
collegian to the snug little Goshen of Balwyn,
about the sweetest outlying Melb. suburb Kew
way. The fact is that devotion to useful duty is
the surest bar to preferment. Bush toil renders
the clergyman ‘ 1 unpresentable,” as the courtly
word is. Failing an importation from England,
the young ladies of the congregation prefer a hot
loaf from the seminary.
* * * *
Dr. Clubbe reports that, by the anti-toxin
serum treatment, the mortality from diphtheria at
Sydney Children’s Hospital was last year reduced
by about 50 per cent. Anti-toxin the doctor
regards as one of the greatest of all medical
triumphs.
* * * *
Eight hundred Sydney unemployed were
last week supplied gratuitously, by a local manu-
facturer, with a large bottle of ink apiece.
Each man will now be able to fully state, in
writing, all his reasons for committing suicide.

The N.S.W. Society of (professional)
Artists will evidently prevail, in the long run,
over its deadly enemy, the (old and more-or-less
amateur) N.S.W. Art Society. Minister Garrard,
when the additions to the National Gallery are
completed, “ will consider the question of re-
organising the State-subsidised classes for the
teaching of art.” In a word, the professional
artists’ scheme for the withdrawal from the Art
Society classes of the =£soo State subvention and
the institution of classes at the National Gallery
itself (cl la Melbourne) will probably come off.
THE LAY OF THE ROUNDABOUT.
The merry-go-round, or roundabout,
Was an innocent thing of yore,
When it did not dip like a frisky ship
Approaching a shingled shore ;
But tne merry-go-round of modern time
Is a wicked, seductive whirl,
Which many suspect of a strange effect
On the merry Australian girl.
Up and down the “ roundy ” goes,
And ladies muster thick
To take their turns. One might suppose
’Twould turn the darlings sick ;
But the far-off look in their dreamy eyes
Admits of never a doubt—
’Tis the look of the damsel, not too wise,
Expressing the love she can’t disguise
From a wooer who wins her heart with liej—
As they ride on the roundabout —
bout, bout—
The giddy old roundabout.
It comes along to a country town,
And when it has passed away
The father of sins stands chafing his fins—
The devil there is to pay.
For Faust has fished for his Marguerite
As an angler fishes for trout,
And the bait which caught that maid distraught
Was a fly on the roundabout.
Up and down the “ roundy ” goes,
Nor seems to threaten harm ;
Yet virtue’s broken signs disclose
The danger of its charm ;
And the heartless Phyllis, so cold and vain,
Who formerly used to flout
The passionate prayers of her faithful swain
Shows sudden regret for her past disdain,
And weeps on his bosom, like tailing rain,
After trying the roundabout —
bout, bout—
The giddy old roundabout.
Wherever it works its secret spell
The wonderful tale is told—
Of Tottie, once shy, now winking her eye
In a manner extremely bold ;
And many a pale young Christian man,
As pure as an oyster pearl,
Gets morally wrecked through this strange effect
On his Sunday-school teacher girl.
Up and down the “ roundy ” goes,
It drives a roaring trade :
An amorous breeze it wildly blows
For matron, as for maid.
The monarch who reigns in a sultry clime
Is luring the ladies out;
O, this merry-go-wrong of modern time
Is a sort of a blissful social crime,
Which is why we sing, in a rorty rhyme,
Beware of the roundabout—
bout, bout—
The giddy old roundabout.

returned unless stamps (of any province) accompany.

would gain. People who can scrape up the money for

other casual ex’s. Cheap-excursion trains are crowded
because they mostly run on holidays; run them every
day and they would be little better than the rest.

£1,200,000 a year, and your “proximity tax” would yield
about £BO,OOO at the very outside. Why don’t you learn
your subject ?

Sydney, February 18, 1896.
My Dear Moorabinda—
The maiden reception given by the brand-new
Mayor and Lady Mayoress of Sydney (Mr. and
Mrs. I. E. Ives) eventuated at Sydney Town Hall
on 12th inst., and attracted the usual job-lot of
people in their Sunday clothes, performing the
game greedy antics round the tea-tables. The
hall was handsomely decorated with flags and
palms, and a short classic programme on the organ
lent a subdued air of culchaw to the proceedings.
There is a great deal of comfort to be derived from
merely reading the names of Haydn (not Heydon),
Mozart and Handel on your programme ; it gives
an especially gratifying sensation to persons whose
musical education is just abreast of ‘ ‘ Salute my
bicycle ” to find, on getting home, that they have
aotually lived through eight classical bits without
turning a hair.
The Mayoralties just arrived—including two
mature daughters and a son—presented a more
grown-up appearance than their predecessors in
office. The new Mayoress is a lady of medium
height with an apparently excellent temper and a
somewhat rococo taste in dress. tier reoeption-
gear was a weirdly-cut black silk trimmed
with white silk covered with black net. A
steel-grey and pink bonnet with silver fix-
ings, a cameo brooch the size of an ice-plate,
white gloves which had burst in the pumphandling
process, a small bouquet and a black fan completed
an ensemble which was enough to get Madame s
dressmaker the unqualified sack. By the way, no
neophyte at receiving folks should juggle with a
bouquet and a fan if she wants to look quite
equal to the physical pressure of the occasion.
Besides, many a visitor to the reception under dis-
cussion, who is under the impression she shook the
new City Mother, really played the game of grab
with a couple of fan-sticks. I am told that the
new Mayoress is not a speech-maker, and so pre-
sents the usual spectacle at women’s oommittee-
meetings of a president who doesn’t like the sound
of her own Voioe. Mayor Ives reminds me of
John See gone to seed. He is an unaccustomed-
looking potentate who yawned drearily more than
once during the ceremonies and sported his
Mayoral robes as if they had been hung on him
to dry.
A large attendance toed the civic scratch, and
the dressing on the whole was Februaryish—the
weary last robes of summer. An English visitor
out the most notioable figure. She hadn’t “pil-
lows sewn into her arm-holes,” but wore plain,
close-fitting sleeves, with a cape expressing the
natural outline of her shoulders. Also, her Skirt
wasn’t five yards round about, and altogether her
outline was diametrically opposed to what we have
been let to consider the snip—yet she looked
modish. An ex-mayoress (whose smile at her suc-
cessor was a work of art) was fittingly attired in
green ; but I never saw such an ill-gowned crowd,
taking it all in all. Its component parts mostly
achieved the high sartorial level of the back-row
of guests in grand opera. But the mayoral recep-
tions are a great social mystery. In the papers
next day you see the names of all the. world and
his wife as being present, and yet few of these big
funs are visible in portly flesh during the show.
don’t know how you explain it. Perhaps Mrs.
de Darling Point lets her bonnet and mantle
descend on her housemaid, whom she sends mas-
querading along with her card-ease ?It would be
about on a par with the approved fashionable
trick of sending your empty carriage after corpses
of no particular consei uence.
I thought it rather a reflection on the real signi-
ficance of civic functions that an enterprising tea
firm should have waylaid the guests on the steps
of the Town Hall and given them printed invita-
tions to a cup of tannin in an adjacent shop. It
looked as if they had taken the measure of the
majority, anyhow. Also, it had a weird appear-
ance of one free drink deserving another.
The latest story g©mg the rounds evolves from
a recent fashionable sale of sticks in the home of a
well-known golden-haired belle, which sale has
harrowed the most sacred feelings of more than
one man about town who happened to run his eye
over the catalogue. For down on the list of
household effects were a number of love-gifts the
chappies aforesaid had given to the damsel herein-
before alluded to, who had heartlessly thrown them
all under the hammer to go for whatever they’d
fetch—souvenirs from johnnies near at hand and
mementoes from youths at a distance all sharing
the same fate. Charming nineteenth-century
tableau ! But how a girl like that must gnash her
pearly teeth when Strephon weighs in with a
lovely but perishable bouquet, which has no after-
value ! This is a good tip for the johnnies who
want to win a society grrl—always make your
enslaver presents which will appeal to a pawn-
broker by and by.
Apropos Johnniedom, this is the latest smartism
which bears the imprimatur of the gone,
but not forgotten Gee-Gees, though I don’t
remember hearing it. Says Gussie, “Are you
dining anywhere on Thursday, old chap ? ” And
when the old chap—scenting an invitation in the
breeze—replied “No,” Gussie chips in with “By
Jove, won’t you be hungry on Friday, then ? ”
The short B. and B. season at Sydney Lyceum
ended last Thursday, with the “Notorious Mrs.
Ebbsmith,” who was a dowdy and vociferous
female while she lasted, and who never would be
missed if she was never billed again. Consequently,
while people hated to lose sight of the popular
players, they were glad to see the last of the dreary
cataloguing of matrimonial misfits which made up
the play. Mrs. Brough (whose voice showed signs
of the wear-and-tear of this ranting r&le) and Miss
Spong shared the floral honors of the occasion, and
the audience had the last curtain up three times in
farewell.
By the way, they clipped some of the redundan-
cies of dialogue in this wordy show and cut out
parts of the first business, one grateful elimina-
tion being the resting of Cecil Ward’s close-
cropped head on the very bare shoulder —to put it
very mildly—of Mrs. Brough. I never thought
that pose suggested dramatic passion—it was only
cheaply spoony d> la Sydney Hyde Park o’ nights,
where the lady usually does all the courting and
nestles the genleman’s manly head on her weak but
willing collar-bone. But Mrs. Ebbsmith abridged
was just as bad as that eccentric person in toto. The
only thing I liked about the play itself was the
cruel rise it took out of the she-wolves of the
Women’s Rights brigade. Here you had an
advanced woman trying to regard man as a worm to
be kerrushed under foot and never led to the altar,
yet falling heels over head in love with a paltry he-
person whose brazen faults of character were
vividly apparent to the meanest intelligence in
the back row of the gallery. And the best of it
was that the pusillanimous Mrs. E. drew the
female she-suffragists and New Women of Sydney
like a mustard-plaster—very much like one m the
ways she scored off their tenderest feelings. On
the last night a peculiarly hard case of would-be
female-voter turned up with an equally enthusias-
tic pal and both he-women sat and snorted scorn-
fully when Mrs. E. fell in love in “ the common,
weak way of women. ”
In one thing at least men can never hope to hold
a candle to women, and that is jumping to con-
clusions. There was an exposition of woman’s
ability in such mental gymnastics on a North
Sydney tram, the other day. A picnic-party got
out leaving two passengers, a man and a woman,
in the oar. Scarcely had the picknickers got
away and the tram started than the woman
frantically exclaimed to the man : “ O they’ve left
a basket! O poor things ! Do throw it out after
them ! ” So he took the basket and billy-can and
flung it after the party. But it wasn’t their bag-
gage, after all. The shrill shriek of the tram-
guard’s whistle as the billy clove the air disclosed
the fact that it was the long-suffering official’s
dinner which had been hurled at a party of total
strangers. The conductor got some of his food
back after stopping the tram, but the funniest
part of it was that the real culprit—the woman-
glared like Svengali for the rest of the journey.
How a woman does hate a man for merely obeying
her orders and making her look ridiculous !
The recent big ferry fatality in Bananaland
somehow reminds me of the stories told of the in-
gratitude of people who are saved from drowning
—in Sydney Harbor, anyhow. The cynical water-
police who cruise about in search of spilt crews,
and so on, have a poor opinion of humanity when
it is pioked up with a boat-hook out of the raging
main by its clothes or its back-hair. It’s not
always that the rescuers get decently thanked by
the men, and when the ex-drowned is a woman,
and thankful to the verge of coming round to re-
hearse her emotions three times a week—that
frightens the male Grace Darlings worse than any-
thing, for they know by bitter experience that she
has made up her mind to marry some of them.
From publisher H. J. Samuell comes “ Drifting
Away ” —song, words by Di Samuel, music by T.
A. Packer. The song is dedicated to Gov. Brassey
and a picture of the Sunbeam adorns the cover.
“ Drifting Away ” is a pretty, silvery melody, for
which the words are an admirable fit; the whole
thing would be oharming sung in a boat by moon-
light. A violin obligato is arranged with the song,
which is prettily written throughout, and in easy
compass—D to F.
Talking of music that most excellent con-
ductor, Mr. David Cope, jun., has everything in
train for the blossoming forth of a Sydney
Amateur Operatic cO., with the inimitable Harry
Leston as stage-providence. Great quantities of
amateur vocalists have joined in enthusiastically,
and, if their ankles perform half as well as their
voices promise, the new venture ought to boom,
and also prove a happy hunting-ground for W.
and M. Light and heavy opera is to be exploited,
but I hope the amateurs will give good old “ Mari-
tana ” a rest, and never allow their Serpolettes, in
“ Les Cloches de Comeville,” to weigh more than
9st. But, above all, I hope they don’t harbor fell
designs on “Mari tana.” Even professional
gipsies are a weariness and a burden, and, when it
comes to amateur Maritanas, with Lazariilo
supremely conscious in cotton tights, and the
King of Spain in a misfit wig knocking his shins
against the modern furniture in Mary s boudoir,
the situation becomes unbearable for every bo< y
but the relations of the performers. I never
knew why amateur operaticists don t mg up ne\.
stuff, and let the old chestnuts roast. I hope that
is what Mr. David Cope intends to do.
By the way, I am pleased to see that Sydney
Amateur Orchestral Society has yarded U P lts
recalcitrant subscribers and collected their tees,
and means to start on a fresh career of melody
with Hazon still at , the win d. For which relief
much thanks. The S.A.O.S. is too promising o
be nipped in the bud.
Extremely apropos to the near season of fish and
fasting which a large section of the religious com-
munity indulges in comes Mrs. Wickens s little
book of clear-cut recipes for savory Lenten dishes.
One’s mouth waters while devouring the printed
description of dainty dishes, which are all de-
scribed without those mysterious “ quantities
which make the old cook-book as eas .7 Y, UT ’ c T er "
stand as an apothecary’s measure. With Airs.
Wickens a spoonful of salt appears as such, and
not as “one cubic foot of saline particles, or
something of the sort. The only thing I object to
in “ Brillat-Savarin ”--I mean Mrs. Wickens s
charming volume —is her advice to the, cook
to color a lobster’s innards with “ rosalme, if the
crustacean isn’t blushing enough to make a pretty
dish. Why should we dye our poor unfortunate
stomachs to foster the vanity of a naturally anaemic
crayfish ? Nobody carries his love of color to
the extent of wanting to have his victuals sent
to a dyer’s.
Talking of meat and drink reminds me that
Sydney has a little Bohemian French cafe with
sand on the floor and paint on a fair percentage of
the lady patrons, and a very good menu to boot—
but, mind you, I don’t want to advertise it.,
Here’s an instance: “Voolez voo du poissong,
says the waiter in his best Woollooinooloo French
to the customer, who, if he is new to the establish-
ment, at once breaks out into a mental perspira-
tion sorting out “Wee” from among his lingual
superstitions. And then the good old order goes
clear and sharp from the same waiter down to the
kitchen, “ Fish, wan.’’
If you want to see what the “ society columns
of the daily papers have come to, just read Sydney
D.T. entertainment-list of last Saturday, and ask
some experienced man about-town who’s who.
The Comic Opera Co. made a welcome re-
appearance at Sydney Lyceum on 17th inst., and
set up one of the Firm’s gloomiest operas, “ Yeo-
men of the Guard,” as the vehicle to introduce
tenor Courtice Pounds. C.P. aforesaid is a bikist
of many laurels, so his legs may be warranted to
wash without shrinking, though if I hadn’t heard
on good authority that the “ full-fashioned ” bulge
was all his very own I might have been sceptical
as to the genuineness of his flowing ankles in
“Yeomen.” He is a plump and amusing youth
whose private pet name is the Heathen Chinee, on
account of his bland smile and half-open eyes, and
who is cheerfully unlike the tenor of commerce in
that he doesn’t leave a high note and a set of white
teeth to drag him through a show without further
effort on his part. Mr. Pounds’ own description
of himself is best: “ a comedian who can sing ”
his acting is as full of artistic detail as. his voice is
round, mellow, and sympathetic. I wish, though,
he wouldn’t occasionally give a wriggle when a
high note blocks the passage. I hate to see people
squeezing melody out of themselves as if they were
working vocal lemon-squash.
Flo Graupner was Elsie, in a frock beribboned
like a bazaar tambourine, and about two-thirds of
her old voice ; and another new member was the
large, fair Mr. Percy Bathurst, who usedn’t to
know what to do with his hands and feet in
“ Morocco Bound,” and who blossomed out as Sir
Riehard Chumley on a pair of thin black legs—
the whole structure weirdly suggesting a two-
legged spider running away with a barrel of tar.
But, mind you, those baggy, beef-eater robes make
both hands and feet look small by contrast —that
is why modern women wear big sleeves, to appar-
ently reduce their waist-measure. But I don’t
much care for Yeomen—it’s a bit of a problem
play in a small way, judging from the remarks in
act 2 that a “ Maid must marry. She must marry,
though the altar turns a tomb.” Really, it must
be most embarrassing to be a man nowadays !
I don’t think it’s quite fair, all this glorify-
ing of women as martyrs and it’s quite
superfluous, too, for every female under the sun
takes her little turn at wearing a resigned, bear-
me-to-the-stake expression (especially strong when
she knows she has got cold mutton for dinner), and
it’s sheer waste of time encouraging her in the
game she plays so well. There’s no “must” about
a “ maid ” marrying, exeept in comic opera—she
can say “ no ” everywhere else, though she doesn’t,
apparently. And, as for the “ altar turning to a
tomb,” why, men can stare at the stage with a
mournful expression just as well as women, while
the singers deal out such interesting statistics. I
have just heard of a bridegroom who doubled up
and died from peritonitis after bravely eating his
way through a pile of bride-made scones.
A quiet wedding was that of Mr. Herbert
Darley, son of Acting-Gov. Darley, who was
married to Miss Georgina D’Arcy Irvme, daughter
of the late Canon D’Arcy Irvine, of Goulburn, at
Bowral, on 13th inst. The bride wore the usual
albescent get-up with wide hat and feathers, her
three maids also sporting white frocks and hats, so
that the uninitiated looker-on sorted out the bride
with difficulty. The blonde Mr. Harry' Darley
was best man. I hear that the new bridegroom
and his brother are running a flourishing station
at Salisbury Plains, Bowen, N.Q., and that the
recent Benedict is spoken of as an aspirant for a
seat in next Bananaland Assembly.
SAPPHO SMITH.

During the last session of Q. Parliament
Tozer talked 62yds. s.Vin. in Hansard minion
type Powers 40yds. 2ft. 6in., Drake 36yds. 2ft.
gin ’Glassey 37yds. 2ft. llin., Petrie Ift., Thomas
lft. 1 in., Midson 3ft., Crombie 2ft., Cribb Ift. sin.,
