Melb. Age was virtuously indignant with
Hancock, M.L.A., for “puffing” Speight in the
Assembly whilst his libel-action against the news-
paper is still subjudicc. The effrontery of this
indignation lies in the fact that David Syme has-
tened to glorify himself at a public dinner a while
ago when everybody knew that Speight’s appeal
to the Privy Council was being prepared.
One of the pleasantest, coolest, most
central and best-appointed restaurants in Sydney
is the Manhattan Dining Room, situated in the
basement of the great Equitable Building in
George-street, Sydney. It is in the middle of the
busiest portion of Sydney, and is in every respeot
a high-class establishment with good cooking, good
attendance, a lavish bill of fare, and luxurious
fittings and furnishings.*
The interminable Adelaide hospital dis-
pute, which nobody seems really to know anything
about, save that it is something concerning a
woman, has partially disrupted the Kingston
Ministry. Chief Secretary Gordon, one of the
strong men of the Cabinet, the promotion of whose
sister (a nurse) over the heads of her seniors at
Adelaide hospital was the casus belli, resigns, and,
with a general election close at hand, his depar-
ture may prove a shock to the Ministry of “All
the Talents. ” For a Cabinet of such a calibre to
disrupt over one hospital nurse is pitiable, anyhow.
Sometimes Northern agriculturists smile
at the Q. Agricultural Department—more often
they curse. Its Ministerial head is a bank-
manager, its office head an unsuccessful farmer.
The other day specimens of sugar-cane were
wanted, and an entomologist was sent to New
Guinea to collect them —all entomologists being
naturally cane-experts. He brought back, it is
alleged, a variety of samples which were full of
“borers,’’and had to be promptly destroyed, one
“ borer ” being calculated to ruin more cane in a
week than an entomologist could suck in a twelve-
month. The Department is now on the look-out
for an astronomer to go to Ceylon and collect
coffee-grubs.
The defeat of the Government candidate
at Christchurch (M.L.) was brought about through
the splitting of the vote on the Cold Tea question.
The Tory party put up Lewis as a single candi-
date. Two men, both bearing the lamentable
name of Taylor, insisted on standing more or less
in the Government interest, and when the Govern-
ment decided to support one Taylor the other
Taylor went in “on his own ” as a Cold Tea candi-
date pure and simple. As the Government’s
Taylor was only partially Cold Tea, and the loose,
independent Taylor was wholly Cold Tea, the
pump vote went solid for the latter. Result:
Oppositionist Lewis, 4714 votes; Cold Tea Taylor,
4302 votes; Government and partially-Cold-Tea
Taylor, 3196 votes.
Yic. Fat Man, disconsolate at the success
of the Turner Ministry and recognising its own
feebleness and incapacity to produce a leader —harm-
less Jock MTntyre, and the always-in-a-minority
Murray Smith, both being impossible—has written
to its old pet, Duncan Gillies, asking him to return
and save his bleeding country ; the idea being that
the author of the boom budget could upset the
present Government and then lead the Free-
Traitors’ campaign at next general election. But
letters received from the little man indicate his
unwillingness to return. The indolent Duncan is
very comfortable where he is. The Melb. Tories
sent him to London because he was in the way
while Patterson was forming a Ministry, and he is
not disposed to come back.
Yic. Legislative Council, under the leader-
ship of old Idoftgoods Sargood, made an exhibition
of itself as usual over the Federal Bill. In the
debate more than half the members declared that
it would be ruinous to trust the work of constitu-
tion-making to delegates chosen by the “ unedu-
cated masses.” Then they proposed to send ten
reliable, high-toned delegates appointed by Par-
liament, regardless of the fact that these would be
voted down by the thirty representatives of the
ignorant masses from N.S.W., Tasmania, and
S.A., so that their culchaw would go for nothing,
after all. And, finally, the Bill was passed on the
old basis —the appointments to be made by the
ignorant masses as originally proposed, so either
the Council doesn’t believe its own tale about
the ruin that will come upon the country or else
it doesn’t care whether the country is ruined or not.
The Yie. Council proposal to abolish the
ballot for the Federal delegates, and to substitute
the Post Office, is a specious attempt to revert to
the old condition of things when employes, tenants,
debtors, and defendants generally voted one way
and believed another. The' value of the ballot is
not that a man can vote secretly, but that he must
vote secretly. The voter at present cannot prove
whom he voted for, and the inquisitive parish
priest, and employer, and mortgagee can at best
but get the voter’s statement on the subject—the
consequence is that coercion of the old sort is a
thing of the past. The postal voting paper would
be marked under supervision of the boss, and
would record the opinions of the boss. If Vic.
wants to abolish anything, let it commence with
its Leg. Council.
Lewis, the Tory member for Christchurch
(M.L.), denounces the Seddon Ministry in a weak,
small way because it has, he alleges, “attracted
to our shores the poor and destitute of Australia.”
Any Government which makes its province worth
living in thereby attracts the poor and destitute of
other provinces ; the only way not to attract the
poor and destitute is to make the country unfit to
live in, and the Lewis kind of man objects equally
if the destitute come and also if they have reason
to stay away. And when the Seddon Govern-
ment tried to combine two sets of advantages by
making the province fit to live in on one side, and
shutting out the extraneous poor and destitute by
an Undesirable Immigrants Bill on the other, the
Lewis party objected in so loud a voice that it
nearly blew the roof off the Universe.

POLITICAL
POINTS
N.S.W. Govt, battle-cry : “Go on the
Sand, young man.”
The (S.A.) National League hankers for a
new name. Why not “ The National Leech ? ”
“ By Australian Independence, a career
would be opened for honorable ambition in every
form.”— Lang.
A year or two back, Parkes said he was
“of the stuff of which martyrs are made.” Now
he is a survivor of the “ giants.”
Parkes haspires to represent Waverley
because he wants “settled Government.” Ho
yus ! ’E wants the Reid Government settled.
When honbst men fall out, rogues
prosper. The Govt, gang will scoop the pool in
Bananalaml now that Powers has given Glassey the
retort courteous.
A current piece of cant up North:—“l
don’t really see any difference between the Oppo-
sition and the Government.” But they vote the
Ass ticket every time.
Before elector.; can vote in S.A., their
names must be on the roll for six months. The
general elections are being delayed to allow as
many women as possible to become qualified.
Q. Labor-party doesn’t like lawyers. It
looks askance even at Powers and Drake. But it
will put up Summerville, an Ipswich solicitor,

Vic. railways announced railway works,
and in 10 days 1000 men applied for employment—
not all Victorians. Married men with two years
residence take precedence in r.ccordance with
neighbor Reid’s Freetrade policy.
Labor politicians are keen in the North.
The other day the Revision Court adjourned at
Cloncurry to allow a well-known station overseer
time to put in an electoral claim. The Labor men
immediately got horses; scoured the country for
some 250 miles, and turned up next court day
with 17 electoral claims to balance the other side’s
one.
Q. Government will put up “ Bob ” Fraser,
ex-Mayor of Brisbane, to run with Kingsbury.
Fraser has achieved a boisterous popularity by a
simple recipe. Always greet a man by his front
name, and shake hands with everyone you don’t
know.
Drake is to be opposed for Enoggera by
Woolcock, once private secretary to Griffith, and
the strongest supporter Drake had. After sitting
on a rail for years to see which way the cat would
jump, he has decided that she is once more about
to claw Nelson’s back hair.
Sydney D.T. gives Saturday portraits of
the two chief candidates in Waverley election. It
prints thfe Freetrader with full, smiling lips that,
at first sight, seem to show all his teeth ; it pre-
sents the Protectionist with a downward boomer-
ang curve that savors of ill-temper, if not of
hardened cruelty.
Boodlewraith is now cordially inviting the
Opposition to come inside and be assimilaterl.
Tozer is quite a Radical now ; the Kanaka trade
will soon be dead (the niggers will be, anyway),
and land-grant railway schemes are a thing of the
dim and distant past. But while those Acts dis-
grace the statute-book Tozer will protest in vain.
The other day an enthusiastic and con-
scientious Single-tax organ up North advertised
for a young fellow for the office, and all the appli-
cants were asked to give samples of their penman-
ship. The text set for them was : “ Protection
does not increase wages. ” After that the chosen
applicant was not startled to death when offered
ss. per week.
Dear Bulletin, —“ Bananaland, ”as ap-
plied to Queensland, is singularly inappropriate.
Portions (extensive ones, too, in the South and
S.W.) will not grow bananas at all, and others
grow them badly. The true banana-land ex-
tends from Mackay to Cairns —so that, in the
event of Separation, the real banana country
would lose its proper appellation, inasmuch as the
name Bananaland would probably adhere to the
Southernmost division or Q. proper. Why not
re-christen the country with that quaint, descrip-
tive vocable — “Mongrelia” ?— P. Luftig.
Maoriland finds a splendid opening this
year for its surplus grain in N.S.W., and a Christ-
church paper remarks that this sufficiently proves
the advantages of Freetrade. Quite true. Also
the German victories over France in ’7O showed
the advantages of having a rotten army, and in-
capable commanders, and a military system war-
ranted to go to pieces in the shortest time on
record. That is to say, they showed the advan-
tage to Germany of France having these things,
and in the same way the present case shows that
Freetrade in N.S. Wales is very good—for Maori-
land.

N.S.W. Chamber of Manufactures reports
that one Sydney boot-factory has cut down wages
15 per cent, since the new tariff came into opera-
tion ; a large joinery work has made a reduction
of 20 per cent, and reduced its staff ; the Waratah
soap and candle factory has reduced wages 10 per
cent., and one cement factory has closed altogether.
This is what the S.M. Herald calls “ cheapening
production.” Also, Sydney Benevolent Asylum
reports that the number of destitute parsons re-
lieved in January, ’96, was the largest l’ecord for
one month since the institution started.
Perth (W.A.) Morning Herald has been
making a fuss over the ruinous departments,
and winds up by calling on Premier Forrest to
resign and appeal to the country. Now, one of
the principals of the Herald proprietary is Alex,
Forrest, M.L.A., brother of the Premier, and
people are wondering whether the Herald, having
just started, is only pursuing a circulation, and
will soon lapse into the straight path of Govt,
support.

Editor Bulletin, —Ee the proposed

The Single-tax League, Sydney, is to be
“ revived ” —“ revive ”is good. The secretary, in
his next annual report, should be at some pains to
explain away the defection from the “ Cause ” of
men, who, like Fitzpatrick, of Windsor, and other
candidates for Parliament at last election, under
pressure, repudiated the Single-tax after years of
advocacy of it, and contented themselves with
avowing that they were simply Land-value taxers.
Sleath, M.L.A., whose quarrel with the
Labor-in-politics officials over a £25 cheque which
he got for the purpose of assisting him to contest
the Wilcannia seat is still going on, appears
to have scored so far since the correspondence
was published. First he was elected treasurer
of the N.S. Wales provincial council of the
Labor Federation ; after that president of the
A.W.A., which is the chief power in Bourke dis-
trict Labor circles. But it detracts from these
certificates that the A.W.A. poll closed before the
correspondence was published.
The N.S.W. Freetrade Government, in its
public works contracts, now fixes ss. a-day as the
minimum wage for unskilled labour. But it also
fixes it as the maximum wage, for all extra work,
which was not included in the specification, is
paid for on the ss. a-day basis, and not one cent
more. Therefore the contractor must stick to the
ss. so far as any extra work is concerned, and he
naturally sticks to it all through. Before the
Government’s minimum—or rather maximum—
wage came into force, the rates paid for unskilled
labor, to really good men, ran as high as 7s. a-day,
therefore the principal result so far has been a big
reduction in their earnings. This is what is called
“ reducing the cost of production.”
“As to why I wish to enter Parliament
again,” Parkes is reported as saying, “ I can only
say that I have sat in every Parliament that has
existed in this country, and I would like to sit in
this simply because I have been in the others.” It
is the horrible unsymmetry of the thing which
touches the old man, apparently. Parliament
doesn’t seem complete without him—there’s a void,
a sense of something missing—he has visions of
the Leg. Assembly waking suddenly in the night
and stretching out its hands to feel if he’s in the
bed, and crying all over the pillow because it
doesn’t know where on earth he’s got to. So the
poor old man proposes to fill that void at once.
It’s a bit of a sacrifice, but he likes to see things
harmonious and shipshape, and he’ll do it. “It is
my dooty.”

W.A. Opposition is a bigger sham than
even some W.A. mines. Attorney-General Burt
lately hinted at resignation, and though Parlia-
ment contains a fair proportion of lawyers, the
two principally tipped for the office were Opposi-
tion-leader Leake and Oppositionist James. Each
would snap at the portfolio if the chance offered.
Premier Forrest has only to hold up a portfolio,
and the Oppositionists would fall over each other to
get at the prize.
Staggering revelations as to the state of
Victorian railways rolling-stock can’t wait many
more months. They must come. The carriage
fittings are dropping to pieces, the engines have
never before been in such a bad way within
the memory of the e ldest employes. Goods
trucks are laid aside by the hundred for repairs
that, will not be attempted until the ne' c railway
policy is decided upon. The failure of this year’s
wheat-crop spared the department a confession of
its truck shortcomings, inasmuch as means of
transit for a bountiful harvest are not available.
The commissioner who fin ’s hin elf appointed to
the job of making the \ . Jways pay, will
have to start off with an e._ _. mous new expendi-
ture on rolling-stock.
The S.M. Herald is right: Old man
Parkes hasn’t “ Buckley’s chance ” for the Waver-
ley seat. He has no show of being returned, and,
even were he elected, he would have no following,
and could do nothing. The Samson of old has
been shorn. The hairy hypnotist has lost his
ancient grip. The Sydney-Smiths, and Goulds,
and Youngs, and Garrards, the political starvelings
whom he once contemptuously employed as supers
in his show, and who, at his behest, were wont to
fill themselves up daily and nightly with meta-
phorical kerosene and candles, and mustard-and-
water, and other horrors, respond no more to the
glance of his erstwhile eagle eye. Once, they
would have quailed before him, like the slaves in
a pantomime. Once, at a word or a look from the
Hairy Bismarck, their knees knocked together
and the swe:.t of fear poured from their finger-
tips. But old man Parkes is played out and done
f or now.

The name of a Chinese witness called to
substantiate the charge of corruption against
a Melb. honorary magistrate vtas Ah Tip.
It is said that, as the result of a visit to
Chamberlain’s town residence, S. A. Agent-General
Playford has been induced to promise to buy and
wear a Court suit.
Ex-Chief Justice Stephen, on retiring, be-
stowed his judicial wig and robes on Justice
Windeyer. On the latter’s retirement who will
inherit ? The Bulletin suggests barrister Heydon.
It is the ambition of Premier Kingston’s
wife to be the first woman to vote at the coming
S.A. Assembly election. South Australia is the
first province on the continent to give the suffrage
to the other sex.
When will someone tell the truth, the
whole truth, and nothing but the truth, as to the
reason why Col. Johnson was not shot for his
part in the Bligli rebellion, instead of being
simply cashiered ?
Theo. Hansen, the Victorian artist, has a
studio at Chelsea, London, and is doing real hard
work. He is much in request for local concerts,
and is enabled to keep the pot boiling by his
vocal abilities alone.
Regret will be felt in Sydney at the
death of a lady who was somewhat prominently
associated, in an indirect way, with a notorious
military inquiry, and whose position excited the
keenest sympathy for her at the time.
It is about time that old man Parkes’s pet
hangers-on abandoned their policy of working-up
outsiders to induce the veteran to make additional
humiliating public appearances. The issues of
the pitiable farce, if repeated, will assuredly react
upon themselves.
Patchett Martin, the literary Melbourne
ex-telegraph official, takes life very easily and
indolently in London. He is one of that rare,
lucky, much-envied type—the fireside, slippered
journalist. He has a little money, lives in quiet
style, and is fat and happy.
Sydney Jephcott has just been married to
Miss Dickson, an East Richmond (Melb.) lady.
Australians should know more than they do of the
singer of the “Southern Swan Song,” “’Twixt
Snow and Sleet,” and “A Hymn for the Dis-
possessed. ”
Vic. Education Minister and Chief Secre-
tary Peacock is getting himself disliked by the
Cold Tea and W.C.T.LT. brigades. Whenever
they deputationise him with thunderous allega-
tions re Sunday trading, or age of consent, or
half-witted Messalinas, &e., he listens blandly
for half-an-hour, only jerking in occasionally
“ Fancy that now ! ” Then he remarks, in a
quaintly quiet way, “Ladies and gentlemen, I
have listened carefully to what you have to say,
and will give your remarks exactly the considera-
tion they are entitled to. It is quite possible on
your own showing that you have more and better
opportunities for observation than governmental
officials and police could possibly have. There-
fore, if you will kindly place the facts, dates, and
proofs before me, I will carefully go into the
matter. Good morning.” Then the deputation
meanders out silently and wonderingly.

Stead is described as an ordinary-looking
man, about sft. 2in., shabbily dressed in a badly -
cut tweed suit of assorted patterns. Strong,
broad, heavy face, seamed with wrinkles ; pene-
trating eyes, ugly mouth, with discolored, sepa-
rated teeth. When he smiles which is more
frequently than you may expect —the lower lip
drags down on one side. His forehead flows up
on each side of his scalp, divided by a jutting pro-
montory of loose, fluffy hair. His full-bearded,
benevolent, matter-of-fact face is suggestive of
the foreman-mechanic who has risen by worthy
merit.
The friends of Hon. S. E. Lees, M.L.C.,
ex-Mayor of Sydney and ex-superintendent of
William-street Wesleyan Sunday-school, got a
“ perfect staggerer ” last week, when they saw his
name appearing as that of one of the trustees
for a lottery-distribution of properties. Mr. Lees
has since repudiated all knowledge of the matter.

position in English black-and-white art.
The late Richmond Thatcher was always
very timid in crossing a street. A friend who had
often remarked this once asked him the reason-
“ Well, old man,” replied Dick, “ I am always in
dread lest I should be run over and killed by a cab
containing a woman of the town— which I considex
the most dishonorable death an honorable man can
die. ”

N.S.W. Premier Reid, speaking at Waver-
ley the other night on behalf of the widow and
children of the late Angus Cameron (into whose
seat he, the Premier, seeks to assist the .yapping
little Alderman Jessep), quite broke down with
emotion. Mr. Reid is in by no means good health,
and anxiously awaits the return from Japan of the
head of the Ministry, Attorney-General Want,
who cannot possibly be back for six weeks at
least. The Premier often finds it hard to stay
awake, and, as he is a man who can’t possibly take
any exercise to speak of, his condition is most un-
satisfactory.
CARRIE MOORE and IVY SCOTT, in “ Djin-Djin.”
Archbishop Murphy (R.C.), of Hobart,
now in his 83rd year, who is an enthusiastic
astronomer and an excellent showman, the other
day invited a select party to visit his observatory,
where he has a very fine telescope. Lady Gor-
manston, who was star-gazing, suddenly exclaimed,
“ Oh, archbishop, it is wonderful, really wonder-
ful, most wonderful!” “Yes,” said the arch-
bishop, apologetically, “ but will your ladyship
please excuse me one moment, just while I remove
the cap of the telescope ? ”
A correspondent writes to Melb. Herald,
strongly disapproving of Premier Turner’s clothes.
The positively truculent shabbiness of the Hon.
George will precipitate a crisis one of these days.
It is in strong contrast to the spick-and-span neat-
ness of the ennobbled M‘lntyre, leader of the
Opposition, who, in black frock-coat and black
tie, looks like an undertaker. The Turner Govern-
ment are an ill-dressed lot altogether. Attorney-
General Isaacs and Lands Minister Best are the
only mitigating items.

A monument is being erected in MePb.
cemetery over the grave of “Father Finn, of
Heidelberg,” who has been dead 15 years. F.F.
was the first priest educated and ordained in Vic-
toria, but he fell out with Archbishop Goold and
wasn’t interred in the Mortuary Chapel with the
other priests. But F.F.’s brother-in-law, an in-
fluential squatter, went to Rome a few years ago
and made “proper representations,” and now
Father Finn’s name has been inscribed on the
tablet in the Mortuary Chapel with the others.

counsel was putting in cross-examination but

cases > 1 shudder for the accused.”

MR. DUANE, OF AUCKLAND.
We shall print the whole of Mr. Isaac Duane’s
letter, only regretting that it is not longer. He
might have mentioned that June, the month in
which he wrote, is the coldest month in the year
in his country, and January the hottest. He might
also have reminded us that New Zealand is almost
as large as Great Britain, with a much more
desirable climate. He might further have said
that it is a wholesome place to live in, as it con-
tains few physical sources of disease, and possesses
no insect so noxious as the English wasp. Yet on
reading his letter we shall find that those attractive
islands in the far Southern Sea are not free from
an enemy we are called upon to fight here in Eng-
land, as elsewhere throughout the world.
“ I have much pleasure in writing you,” says
Mr. Duane, “as to me it is a privilege as well as
a duty to describe an experience in which I am
confident you will be interested.
“ Ever since I was a boy I have not only suffered
from indigestion in its worst form, but 1 have been
a martyr to it. Such success in life as I have
achieved has been in the face of the constant
opposition set up by this miserable complaint.
All its symptoms are familiar to me as the smoke
of London is to a dweller in that rather grim old
city. The bad taste in the mouth, the fitful
appetite, the distress in the stomach after eating,
the pains in the chest and back, the dull headache,
the sense of weariness and fatigue, the depression
of spirits, the want of ambition to take hold of
any labor, the weakness resulting from lack of
sufficient nourishment, &c.—all these were part
and parcel of my life from my youth to a time I
am going to speak of in a moment.
“ I can only account for it by assuming that I
must have inherited a tendency to this disease.
At all events it cast a gloom over my whole his-
tory up to the date of my recent happy deliverance.
The record of the sleepless, wretched nights I
passed would make almost a volume by itself.
Times beyond counting I have arisen from my
bed in the morning, glad the night was gone, and
yet in no frame of mind to welcome the day. To
ithe chronic dyspeptic rest does not bring strength
as it does to others.
“You will hardly need to be told that I made
every effort to obtain a cure. I tried medicine
after medicine —now something I thought of my-
self and then something advertised in the news-r
papers. And as to doctors (against whom I desire
to say not a word), I tried one after another, and
faithfully used the prescriptions they gave me;
but nothing more than temporary relief came of it.
‘ ‘ About four years ago a friend spoke to me of
the great reputation of Mother Seigel’s Syrup in
curing all ailments of the digestion, and urged me
to make a trial of it. I might as well say frankly
that I had little faith that it would do me any
good; but I was in such pain that I was in a
mood to try anything that offered the remotest
chance of a cure. So I bought a bottle, and the
very first dose made me feel better. This was so
cheering and hopeful that I continued taking the
Syrup, and to my surprise I grew better and
better until I was cured. All the symptoms which
made my life a burden for so many years are now
gone, and lam a different man. If ever, from
any cause, I have a temporary recurrence of
indigestion, a few doses of Mother Seigel’s Syrup
Eroduee immediate relief, and leave me in good
ealth. You have my full consent to publish this
letter. lam well known in Auckland, and always
glad to tell anyone by word of mouth what a
wonderful cure your remedy worked in my case.
(Signed) Isaac Duane, coach builder, Karangahape
Road, Auckland, New Zealand, June 25th, 1895.
Our home readers will perceive that not in
England alone but in far distant quarters of the
globe this celebrated medicine is known, and is
successful where others do not avail. It is the
most commonplace of truths that its praises are
sounded wherever civilisation extends, and almost
in every written language the name of Mother
Seigel’s Syrup finds an acknowledged place.*
One legal gentleman, anyhow, is bitterly
disappointed over the Maoriland Midland Rail-
way arbitration. When he came to Wellington
with his brief he took it for granted that the case
and his refreshers would go on in the orthodox,
interminable legal fashion, and he hired a fur-
nished house for six months. Then Umpire
Blake put the case through in three weeks, and
the feelings of that lawyer are too painful for
expression.

The most effectual Melb. debt-collector is
a coal-black darkey champion heavy-weight fighter
and wrestler. He sits in the Govt, office-passages
and then, questioned as to his business, explains
that Mr. Shentpershent has sent him to collect an
instalment from Mr. Algernon Sealingwax. It is
impossible to move him until the unfortunate
civil servant “ stumps up.”
A newspaper without paper, La Tela
Cortada, “The Out Cloth,” is the title of a
Spanish satirical weekly, published at 2h<i. It is
printed on a handkerchief, which, after reading
the contained matter, the buyer can send to the
wash. It would be a good idea to print some of
the Australian evening papers on cambric; then
one could wash them before reading them. They
badly want washing.
Dear Bulletin,— The exact note of the
morepoke need not be a matter of controversy.
At a short distance, it is distinctly “more-poke.”
Approaching the .limit of audibleness—which,
under favorable conditions, is about a mile—it is
just as distinctly “ pope-pope ;” certain sound-
vibrations being exhausted in transit. ‘ ‘Mo-poke ”
is intermediate; andno further rendering by means
of our alphabet can be oorrect. At a short dis-
tance, the double note impresses one as strong
and striking; softened by distance, it becomes
weird, sad and forlorn.— Tom Collins (Vic.).
In Maoriland, if a prisoner escapes from
the lockup, the policeman who was in charge at
the time is given three davs in which to find him.
If he doesn t get him within that time, the M.L.
Govt, has no further use for that policeman.
Consequently, the most fearsome example of fast
sprinting in the province is a lockup-keeper going
after an escaped prisoner, and in 99 cases out of
100 he is back with him, or with one who looks
sufficiently like him to pass muster, inside the
specified time. In the hundredth case he simply
sends back his uniform by parcel post and goes
gum-digging.
Dear Bulletin, — Re your remarks on
Premier Braddon and the Alabama. The Alabama
didn’t go to the bottom in her first fight, as her
first engagement was with the U.S. war-steamer
Hatteras off Galveston, when the Southern ship
sank her enemy and captured her crew in 13min.
The defeat of Semmes and the destruction of the
Alabama by Winslow, of the Kearsage, was no
disgrace to the former, as the Kearsage was practi-
cally an ironclad ; and U.S. Captain Winslow’s
report of the engagement states that early in the
fight the Kearsage was wounded in the stern-post
on the water-line by a 3-inch shell, and that a
catastrophe was only averted by the shell failing
to explode. The shell was cut out and is still on
view in the Naval Museum, New York.— Hermes.
The Melb. police-courts have a way of
rubbing things in. The other day a young man
who had just escaped after a hard run with a
stolen brown-paper parcel, took refuge on a St.
Kilda tram, but a constable who was on the
vehicle arrested him on spec. He got three
months. Then the officer applied for travelling
expenses, and 4s. 5d. being found on the prisoner,
2s. of it were handed over to the intelligent bobby.
Here the prisoner, who had taken the three
months philosophically enough, proceeded to lift
up his voice in protest. “ I don’t mind the three
months’ quod,” he said, “ but they didn’t ought to
have copped my two bob. It’s the meanest thing
I ever heard of. Here I’ve got to pay a —; —
policeman for runnin’ me in. I’m actshally payin’
for the wear an’ tear of the blessed officer.”


Some definitions. Morality : A woman’s
love ; and brotherhood. Immorality : Sublimated
morality. The Devil : Two or three old women.
A Sydney house-agent now puts in a
Chinese bailiff at 4s. a day on distressed tenants.
The regular rate for the Aryan bailiff is Bs. a day,
so the Chow has a distinct advantage in point of
cheapness. Think of the degradation of a country
where a white woman whose rent is in arrear is
bailiffed by a Chinaman !
RABBITS IN VICTORIA.
The Rodney, Waranga and Deakin Shire Councils,
in conference on 22nd January, 1890, unanimously
resolved to purchase TOXA, the celebrated rabbit
exterminator, in wholesale quantities, in order
that they might be able to supply it to their rate-
payers at cost price.
The shattered skeleton of a man was found
lying under a bush at Yancowinna (Broken Hill),
the other day, and the nearest constable was sent
to investigate the matter. The constable, having
gazed intently at the bones, some of which had
been pulled around by hungry quadrupeds or
birds, found a verdict to the effect that deceased
had expired about three years ago from natural
causes. After which he gathered up the fragments
and gave them Christian burial. It takes a police-
man to discover whether a clean-picked bone died
from natural causes or a dose of poison.
Dear Bulletin,—The correspondent who
writes about mutton-birds’ eggs being disposed of
to Vic. housewives as farm-reared duck-eggs,
“ which they much resemble,” has evidently never
seen, or smelt a mutton-bird’s egg. It does not
resemble a duck-egg, being distinctly ovate (with
a broad lower extremity), of a rough chalky-white
color, while the anas backyardii lays an oval egg,
polished and bluish-white. Besides, even a blind
housewife would not be taken in unless she had
influenza —the strong musky smell is detectable
at 20yds. I submit, as proof, a blown specimen
that has been in my cabinet seven years, and is
still strong.—Van Demon.
S.S. : A recent “ Sappho” paragraph has
determined me never again to commiserate the
female in distress. Some time back I gallantly,
and even apologetically, drew the attention of a
fashionably-dressed young lady to the fact that
her watch was hanging out, and she rewarded me
with a look of utter loathing. The other day, in a
railway-carriage, I discreetly intimated to a sweet
creature in pink that her hair had broken bonds,
and her contempt for my ignorance found expres-
sion in a sneer from which I have not yet re-
covered. Now, when I see a lady with her golden
hair a-hanging down her back, or with her store-
teeth attached to her ear, or her rationals on
upside-down, I shall maintain a masterly reticence,
and assume that the apparently dismantled
female is a pioneer of fashion.

Report on. tHe Valuation ets at 31st December, 1895.
Gentlemen,—
I have now to report that, in accordance with the usage of the Company, the Annual Valuation
has been made of its liabilities in both Branches, as at 31st December last.
The rate of interest earned in the two Branches during the period, 1893 to 1895 inclusive, calcu-
lated on the mean of the funds in each year, has been as follows:
The decline in interest in 1895 in the Ordinary Branch will, in all probability, be largely recovered
In 1896. It will be seen, however, that the rates now earned are fully satisfactory, when viewed in
connection with those employed in the calculations.
During the past year the mortality in both Branches has been very favorable in respect of the
number of deaths, but in the Ordinary Branch a portion of the claims having occurred under policies
of more than average amount, the profit for the year from the light death-rate is not, relatively, so
great as in 1894.
The brief particulars of policies dealt with in the Valuation were as follows :
Before referring to the details, it may be mentioned that the two Branches of the business,
possessing, as they do, radical differences, and being managed on dissimilar lines, have been valued by
separate methods, as in 1894. In dealing actuarially with the affairs of a comparatively young Office,
the fact cannot be lost sight of that while the first nine or ten years of an Ordinary Life Assurance
Company may be fairly profitable, in the case of an Industrial Office, the hea,vy cost of obtaining the
new business, and the heavy lapse rate invariably experienced, make it impossible to obtain more than
very small returns in the corresponding period.
The work involved has been very laborious, requiring the protracted labor of the actuarial and
statistical staffs, assisted by a number of temporary clerks, and latterly supplemented with clerical aid
from other departments of the Office. I have pleasure in recording the zeal that has been shown in
carrying out the undertaking.
Industrial Branch.
The pla.» of keeping up a carefully elaborated system of schedules for valuation purposes, referred
to in my last Report, has worked very satisfactorily during the year. Month by month the particulars
of the policies in force are obtained under each Table in the precise form requisite for the calculations,
and enabling the liability in respect of the various classes of contracts to be calculated with equal
accuracy, and much less work than if the particulars had to be obtained afresh on each occasion from
the Office books. It is contemplated adapting the system to the Ordinary Branch during the present
year.
The valuation has been made on the lines adopted in 1894, viz., the English Life Table No. 3,
Males, combined with 4 per cent, interest, the premiums valued being the gross premiums, less 35 per
cent., for all Whole-of-Life Assurances, and less 35 to 37£ per cent, for Endowment Assurances, varying
according to the future duration of the risks. It may be remarked that while the basis is the same as
twelve months previously, it has been somewhat stronger in its effects on this occasion owing to the
higher average durations of the policies, bringing a number into liability which had none at the in-
vestigation of 1894.
throu°hout U ° US and Annuities were used, and negative values were carefully excluded
The basis of the Industrial Valuation is, I believe, fully as severe as has ever been submitted to by
any .British industrial Office of equal age. *
The largest class of transactions are policies for the whole term of life, regarding which thn
remarks made in my last report, apply with equal force now. There has been, dunn» 1895 an
increased issue of limited-payment policies, in consequence, no doubt, of the special benefits con-
ferred in connection with this class, of fixed paid-up policies on a liberal scale. Endowment
Assurances have decreased m number and premiums, the amount assured showing an increase due
to the future average duration of the risks having been considerably extended during the Tear bv

Sydney, 11th February, 1896.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
There is likely to be trouble between the Cricket
Council of Australasia and the ’96 Australian Eleven. The
Council undertakes no financial responsibility in connec-
tion with the tour, does not even offer to secure the
necessary bank overdraft to pay preliminary ex’s and
passage-money to England, but wants to have complete
control of the team. It has gone the length of appoint-
ing the executive committee of the team, which, if the
choice of captain falls on Harry Trott, will mean that
the committee will consist of three Victorians. Sydney
and Adelaide representatives are not likely to stand that.
Further, the Council has decided that the team shall have
minutes of all meetings kept to be produced to the cricket
legislators if necessary. What has the Council got to do
with the internal workings of the team, anyway? It
interfered when the last eleven came back, washed a deal
of dirty linen, and left matters practically “ as you were.”
* * * *
Joe Darling reached the summit of his ambition
In being- included in the Australian team. Joe was a
great college cricketer, but his father, the rich Adelaide
miller, sent him to the country and intended him for a
fanner. Twelve months of tilling the soil satisfied
Joseph, who returned to Adelaide and opened a cricket-
ware business, and vowed he would yet play for Australia.
Old John Darling, who was much incensed at first, gave
Joe a big cheque when he banged Richardson and co.
about last year, and has now fulfilled a promise to give
him ,£2OO if he were included in the Australian team.
* . * * *
Dear Bulletin,—lt is frequently asserted that
Giffen insisted on his brother Walter going to England last
trip, and now insists on Lyons. This is an error, as it is
very apparent that Giffen, although desirous of Lyons’
inclusion, did not take the initiative of getting a 14th
man tacked on to the team. Moreover, despite the con-
tinual “barracking” claim for the captaincy, I feel sure
(after H. Trott’s public assurance that he had no desire
for the unthankful billet) that the champion of living
cricketers, George Giffen, will be found leading the ’96
team to victory and—perks.— Hiawatha, Adelaide.
* * * *
“ Blondin ” Lyons showed what was left in
him the other day by driving up 195 in 85min. Blondin
swished everything—made his first 100 in 42min.
* * * *
Worrall came along with another sensation last
Saturday, bagging six St. Kilda wickets for 17 runs.
Carlton, who was emergency man with the last Vic. team
that visited Sydney, got 10 wickets for 40.
* * * *
Iredale, the N.S.W. batsman, is to be married
before going to England, and Donnan’s wife—sister to
Syd. Gregory—will go with them to England.
The Bulletin would like to see Eady save some
of his big scores until he reaches England. For instance,
182, not out (put up in Tas. last Saturday), _ would be
happily placed in Aus. v. All England.
The selection of that fourteenth man will be the
most trying and thankless job that ever selectors had.
Albert Trott, Turner, Lyons, Howell, C. M‘Leod, Worrall
—whichever of the first four is chosen a howl will be
raised by the supporters of the rest.
As soon as he was picked to represent Australia,
a certain member of the team was waited on by a lady,
who made him take the pledge.
* * » *
The Australian Cricket Council could not do
otherwise than approve of the sub-committee’s team, for
the said sub-committee was appointed by the Council ex-
pressly to pick the players. Therefore the Council could
not object to the team without stultifying its own pre-
vious act.
* * « *
Syd. Gregory, during the English tour, will be
correspondent to a Sydney daily.
* * * *
“R.E.M.” (G. 1.): (1) He is out. (2) If the
batsmen don’t cross, A is out. (3) Yes, if the ball is
pitched on the line of the wickets and keeps on the
wicket. Hasn’t your umpire a book of rules ?
* * * *
A member of the last Australian Eleven relates
that at one of the numerous banquets in their honor,
when there was much cheering in response to the senti-
ments of an orator, an eccentric member of the team
grabbed a hot joint and waved it around his head three
times, scattering the gravy on the assembled guests.
(How came that hot joint on the table at dessert? But it
is a good enough story.)
* * * *
Adelaide cricketers say that as a batsman Clem.
Hill is far superior to Joe Darling this season.
* * * »
Blackham has accompanied eight Australian
teams ’Ome as wicket-keeper.
* * * *
Vic. Leg. Ass., on the motion of Dunolly
Duggan, amended the Council Streets Betting Bill to
exempt racecourses registered by the V.R.C. from the
definition of a “ street.” This would legalise betting on
all metropolitan courses excepting Ascot and Richmond.
The Council did not agree to the amendment, and at time
of writing did not seem likely to. The V.R.C. already
has alarming powers, and to hand over the legislation
of betting to its control would be an astonishing instance
of trust in an irresponsible body already powerful and
careless enough ; and would probably postpone the advent
of the totalisator for a thousand years. The V.R.C. wants
the tote for revenue purposes—but the Duggan proviso
would do just as well. It is a singular commentary on
the Vic. Assembly that it overwhelmed Murray’s Tote Bill,
but accepted Duggan’s apotheosis of the V.R.C. as the
keeper of the country’s conscience on the gambling
question. But the Tote Bill was opposed by Ikey Mo
and Holy Joe in conjunction—a triumphant federation.
George Davis, member for Gippsland and Tim Swiveller,
opposed the Assembly proposal and made the V.R.C. very
* * * * *
The Suppression of Street-Betting Bill, intro-
duced into Vic. Assembly, was fathered by the t.R.C.,
and is really a knock at the proprietary clubs. How the
unregistered courses, which the V.R.C. hates like poison,
can be construed to mean “ streets ” it would be interest-
ing- to learn. The metropolitan club only wants betting
legalised on its own course and those of clubs under its
wing. The difference between gambling on a registered
and unregistered allotment isn’t apparent, but the object
of the bill is very much so.
* * * *
The old moss-grown objection to making turf-
inquiries public, on the ground that publicity would scare
evidence away, might be applied, with equal force, or
want of force, to every enquiry into every variety of'
scandal. No revelations would be brought, to light out-
side of the court-room if such delicate consideration were
shown for the feelings of witnesses. It is the rottenness
of racing which accounts for stewards sitting with closed
doors. Interested witnesses would give all the evidence
they now give under any circumstances, but the public
mustn’t he ir any part of it through the newspapers. The
public, of course, wouldn’t hear the worst of it, even if
reporters were present. Turf-reporters are, in a great
measure, suppressors of the awful truth, otherwise their
occupation would pretty soon be gone.
* * * *
A Sydney civil-servant has been engaged on a
“ system ” for two years, and still hopes to be able to beat
Ikey Mo, when he starts on the warpath. The only man
the writer ever knew whose system was guaranteed (by
himself) to knock the books every time, died in an
asylum. His declining days were spent in endeavouring
to discover the microbe of prickly-heat in the second
finger of his left hand.
* * * * •
The Vic. turf reform meeting was a miserable
fizzle. J. R. Cowell said reporters were admitted to a
turf enquiry at Oakleigh once, and they cross-examined
the jockeys who came forward as witnesses. Then T. C.
O’Brien told Gaunson, who proposed the abolition of
assumed names, that he could never have had a mother-
in-law. _ *
* * *
The old sixpenny-sealed-envelope game is again
in evidence. A person who’d expect a good tip for 6d.
would reckon on making a first-class win for a shilling
“ on the nod.”
* * *
Tattersall has offered Tas. Govt. £5OOO a yeai
for a local monopoly of the sweep business—consultation
crooks having already begun to infest Hobart. By the
way, it was not the pietists but the publicans, &c., who
drove Tatt. from Brisbane. They reckoned if he was out
of the wav more would be spent in beer and skittles.
* * * •
“ I live by honor.” —Bookmaker.
* * * *
Father Thos. Keating, whose death was caused
by a railway accident at Granville, Sydney, recently, was a
breeder of blood stock. His stallion, “ Lord of the Lake,”
two brood mares with foals at foot, a yearling colt and a
chestnut mare, have been announced for auction by the
Curator of Intestate Estates.
* * * *
Mr. S. Miller says paid stewards aren’t needed
at Flemington or Caulfield, the present stewards being
selected from a large racing body. Then he qualifies his
remarks by saying they can’t afford it. Precisely. Mr.
Miller said nothing about stewards who bet; who are
“ in” with jockevs, or who are afflicted with convenient
blindness ; nor did he compare paid men, who don’t bet,
with honorary men—some of whom do all sorts of things.
Flemington and Caulfield need stipendiaries as much as
the smaller clubs do, if not more.
* * * *
Many V.R.C. members are allegedly in favor of
paid stewards, but, “out of pure cussedness,” they
wouldn’t vote for Gaunson’s proposal. Old members
won’t be led by new ones.
v * * •
The tote will come along some dreary day when
people aren’t looking, and provide money for paid officials.
Also it will raise stakes and give clubs more money than
thev know what to do with.
• * * * *
The recent form of Lena, the Hobart Cup
winner, quite justified the price of 12 to 1 against her in a
field of seven, although the opposition was weak enough.
Mischief, who finished second, can’t stay a mile and a half
course, especially when the tail-end of it inclines upwards.

The Golden West.
That Westralia will soon be the leading colony
ot the group is the opinion that is gradually being
xorced upon the world. History cannot furnish
such an example of growth as this colony has
shown during the last two years. A little while
ago the West was practically unknown : now the
country s praises are being sounded throughout
the universe. Capital and labor are pouring in
and the other colonies are being drained of their
youth, rhe finest productions of the day are now
procurable there, just as in the leading shops
ot George-street, Sydney. All stores in the colony
are stocked with Frossard’s Cavour Cigars, the
best cheapest, and most delicious smoke that the
world has ever seen. These cigars are medically
recommended, as, having the nicotine extracted in
vacuo, they are absolutely uninjurious, whilst re-
taining all the fragrance and aroma of the excellent
leaf employed in their manufacture. Frossard’s
Cavour Cigars are obtainable everywhere in
packets of 8 for Is. *
One day the week before last there
arrived in Sydney from foreign countries 42,800
sacks of wheat, 5800 sacks of corn, and 31,700 sacks
of flour.. Also, a Chinese steamer arrived the same
week with 5000 sacks of flour from the cheap leper
of the Flowery Land. Plainly there are good times
coming for the farmers and millers of N.S. Wales.
The leper, by the way, is a new competitor in the
flour business, and it will be interesting to watch
his progress. In the first 39 days of ’9G N.S.
Wales imported =£220,000 worth of farm produce,
against <£44,000 worth in the corresponding period
of ’95. As the province got through with the
smaller amount last year, this vast increase can’t
all be really wanted, and somebody must be
crushed out by the rush of competition. Also,
there is <£4500 per day more to be paid to the foreign
producer that there was last year, and as none of
the province’s exports are looking up to that ex-
tent it would be interesting to know where the
money is to come from.
No better soda-water is to be bought than
that produced by the Volcanic Soda-Water Co.,’of
Sydney. This co. imports natural carbonic acid,
compressed in cylinders, from the Eifel mountains
in Germany, where the gas bubbles up in vast
quantities out of extinct craters, and is purified by
passing through 300 ft. of water before it reaches
the surface. Consequently the trace of sulphurous
acid, which exists in almost all artificial carbonic
acid, is wholly absent. The water used in the
manufacturing process is carefully purified by the
use of the Chamberland-Pasteur filter, and the
result is a beautifully clear, brisk, sparkling fluid
which effervesces for a full half-hour after being
drawn, and is one of the most refreshing drinks
imaginable.*
The firm of J. C. Ludowici and Son, Ltd.,
has, since ’5B, been one of the best known of suc-
cessful manufacturing industries. Both at the
factory, Lane Cove River, and at the warehouse,
162 Clarence-st., Sydney, the employees receive a
share in the profits—a bonus—which secures their
interest and contributes to the co. ’s' success. The
co.’s leather being carefully prepared on modern
principles—only oak extract and wattle-bark are
used in tanning—comes out quite odorless, equal
to or better! than the best imported leather, and
much cheaper. Hence it is greatly prized by lead-
ing boot and shoe manufacturers. A special line
is the famous leather bolting ; 90 per cent, of the
leather belting used in N.S.W. comes from this
firm : it is the finest and steadiest running belting
in the market, and is exclusively used by the
N.S. W. Go vt., by al llarge industries, and by electric-
light stations. Other specialities are raw hide
belting, chiefly for iced-food machines ; patent
double-edged leather belting for driving dynamos ;
patent leather link belting, as used on the electric-
light at the harbor fortifications ; on the North
Shore electric trams ; in the Broken Hill mines.
Raw hide laces, better than other laces ; leather
hose, so popular on steamboats and railways ; sole
leather, engineers’ leathers, pump and hydraulic
leathers; special leathers for refrigerating
machinery; also best imported English and
American belting ; also every other kind of belt-
ing and every kind of leather ; in short, all and
every leather which can reflect credit on the
firm’s high reputation.*
The Equitable Life-Assurance Society of
the United States has been under examination by
the Insurance Department of the State of New
York, and the result of the investigation is
summed up by the superintendent, in his report to
the Legislature, in the following words “ The
examination has been of the most searching and
exhaustive character. The officers and directors
have given every facility to the department during
the progress of the examination. The securities
owned and held as collateral were examined and
counted. The cash was balanced and verified.
The same diligence and exacting care was applied
to all the categories of assets. The valuation of
the liabilities has been made in the most careful
way, nothing being taken for granted, and abso-
lutely independent computations being made in
every case. It affords me profound satisfaction to
find that all the affairs of this vast insurance cor-
poration are in a sound and prosperous condition.
The policy-holders of the Equitable may be satis-
fied that their interests are guarded by conscien-
tious and skilled officers and directors, whose
integrity is a guarantee of fidelity, and whose long
experience qualifies them to conduct the business
in a manner best calculated to conserve the true
interests of the policy-holders.” Mr. C. Carlisle
Taylor, for some years a member of the head-office
staff in New York, has recently been appointed
general manager in Australasia for the society. *

blocked, ran in a winner. Newhaven, who is reckoned

* * * *
The N.S.W. gelding Paris has been entered for
all the big English handicaps at the opening of the season.
He will never get 7.8 again. Enthusiastic Australians
have killed his prospects as a racer.
* * * *
The puffs that Carbine, Carnage, and Trenton
got in London sporting papers were written by Aus-
tralians. The Sportsman’s Camage-Trenton article was
inked by Nat. Gould. Australians are patriots in sporting
matters only.
* * * »
Seven men bowled out a Sydney tote-proprietor
beautifully the other day. Each put ss. on a pony, and
when the printed card appeared the horse in question
was shown to be backed by one only. The bookie had a
bad time when the seven turned up, and an erst prize-
fighter was knocked into limbo during explanations.
* * » *
Horses weighted at under 7st. have only been
successful four times in Aus. Cup. Malua and Highborn
are the only pair that have won with over 9st.
'* * * *
Disparity, a most likely-looking Newmarket
outsider, is in great form, and has been picking up pre-
liminary oats under big weights. The only Fryingpan
(her sire) that ever won the event was Fortunatus.
* * * *
The Laundress party has hopes of annexing
the V.R.C. sprint a second time. Eight-ten should prove
a trifle too much. Her paper chance doesn’t look nearly
so attractive as that of at least half-a-dozen lower weighted
dashers. Aspen, the only dual winner of the New-
market, was favored by the weight each time—6st. 81b.
and 7st. 81b.
* * * *
The always highly-thought-of Dreamland may
come into favor ere the day for Aus. Cup, but at present
backers are chary. A horse with a record is preferred to
a going-to-be.
* * * i|e
Writes “ Scrip ” :
The Newmarket Handicap list will be much re-
duced by the 29th, but a great field is expceted to start.
The writer believes the unmentioned Idolator, who ran
third last year, will play a big part in the finish. So will
Disparity. The three-year-old, Thunderer, is not fit ;
Foliage has been backed right and left by Adelaide people,
but his owner hasn’t got a cent on him. He’s perhaps
the best horse extant over six furlongs, but he may not
start. As he isn’t entered for any other events at the
meeting the suspicion is justified. Apart from the fact
that Foliage may not run through forestalling, the writer
believes that there are only three in the race anyhow—
Idolator, Whakawatea and Disparity. If Foliage starts
the race is practically all over. The only outsider with
'any pretensions is Monard.
In a slow go any horse might win the Aus-
tralian Cup. At present, the writer believes that Elswick,
a seasoned horse, will take the stake. Of the three-year-
olds, he holds a most pronounced opinion about Waterfall.
Wallace could never give him 51b. Of the light weights,
Lord Richmond is undoubtedly the best. His brother,
Broken Hill, won two years ago with 7st. The really un-
known horses in the race are Dreamland and Chesterman.
* * * *
Martin, the Yankee cyclist, is having it all his
own way just now. Last Monday he won the Mile and
Five Miles scratch races at Auckland, the Mile Open Han-
dicap, and came second in the Half-mile Handicap. At
every distance he downed Pither, the M.L. champion, and
Harris, the English champion. If pace, stamina, and
cleverness constitute a champion, Martin fills the bill.
* * * »
Martin and Porta came to Australia together as
partners. But an accident, which broke Porta’s arm,
enabled Martin to beat Porta always, and when Porta got
well Martin still wanted to always start and always win.
So the partners dissolved. Just after which Porta, with
fierce gesticulations, announced to some friends, “ Mar-
tin ! Martin ! always Martin ! Martin always win ! Mar-
tin first! Porta second ! Martin first! Porta last! Always
Martin ! never Porta ! Why not Porta ! We shall see !”
.And they did see immediately—Porta downed Martin and
Pither in the International mile.
* * * *
Martin, say Australian cyclists, is the most
cunning rider on the track. He has won just about £lOO
in cash prizes in Australia, and is the man to back in any
scratch event where he meets the in-and-out scratch men.
* * * *
Wally Kerr, though only 25 years old, will
not cycle-race any more. His recent M.L. trip, he
says, was only undertaken to keep him in form for the
hoped-for trip to Greece. Now he has abandoned that
project and will stick strictly to biz.
The first interprovincial four-oar race, organised
by M.L. Association, takes place in May, on the Parra-
matta. M.L. has turned out no first-class men lately,
amateur rowing there being at present very amateur
indeed.
* * * *
It appears, on most trustworthy evidence, that
there is a hitch in the Harding-Stanbury arrangements
owing to Harding or Stanbury or Sullivan or Stanbury’s
backer or somebody else having done or not done some-
thing which ought not or ought to have been done, and,
as Stanbury himself lucidly explains, there is just now a
little confusion.
* * * *
That club-swinging-record affair coming on
shortly at Foley’s Athletic Hall will be about as sprightly
a game to watch as a mule-race of 500 miles with only one
mule racing. Fancy watching a man swinging clubs for
24 hours! It is only the brain of an athlete that could
conceive so drearily-monotonous a contract. Mrs. Brough,
with even her lovely calves on view, never swung the
clubs in “The Amazons” more than a few minutes.
* * * *
A scheme is afoot for a really big cycle factory
in Melbourne.
* * * *
Melb. cyclists, in view of recent collisions, are
agitating in favor of equestrians being compelled to carry
lights when riding in the suburbs at night-time !
# * * *
The Maharajah of Patiala has been snapping
up every available horse capable of galloping, and now
owns a queer lot of 600 good, bad, very bad, broken-down,
useless neddies, most of them Australian-bred.
* * * *
England, America, Italy, M.L., and every Aus-
tralian province will be represented at the forthcoming
March carnival of Sydney wheelmen —on the new cinder-
track at Sydney Agricultural Ground. The wheelmen, by
the way, have invested all their profits in the new grand-
stand to be erected on F. Webster’s estate.
• » * *
It came out in a Sydney police-court, the other
day, that a girl, 13 years old, owned and controlled the
movements of a racing-pony.

of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 660 ft.,

literary and artistic : 1, New Books ; 2, Second-

“Sir” John M‘lntyre also responded for the Legislative |
Assembly. He could not, he said, claim to be an Austra-

Exhibition Building.
There are no “ women” in Barcaldine (Q.).
T’other day a “women’s meeting” was to have
been held in the local church. No one attended,
the fair ones loudly protesting that they were
“ ladies,” not “ women.” Of such is the Kingdom
of Outback !
In Stipendiary Giles’s opinion, bearded
prisoners should not be permitted to shave until
they emerge from the police-court process—then,
of course, many are shaved under compulsion. No
doubt identification may sometimes be eluded by
judicious use of the razor, but Mr. Giles’s preven-
tive theory could only apply to unbailed prisoners,
and those able to procure bail could still be
shaved, trimmed, or otherwise barbered beyond
identity. That is just the old appearance-nine-
points-of-the-law difficulty dished tip anew. .To
carry the P.M.’s theory to its illogical conclusion,
beardless prisoners should be compulsorily shaved
every day, and those with the Parkes brand of
hair should have their heads imprisoned in
specially-designed stocks to prevent their locks
from ruffling. And any person proved, on expert
examination, to have refrained from washing for a
period of, say, six months should be denied soap
and water until his case concludes.
A fine instance of the great art of identifi-
cation recently disturbed the peaceful town of
Parramatta. A year or two back a lady left
Parramatta for Perth (W. A.), and while there a
strange man tried to assassinate her husband with
a revolver. The police never captured him ; pro-
bably by walking fast he kept ahead of the tele-
fram containing his description, and so got away.
lately the lady came back to Parramatta on a
visit, and in the street one evening she caught
sight of a familiar face which she recognised imme-
diately. She rushed to the police-station with the
tale of the attempted murder at Perth, and the
police were started out in search of the criminal.
Presently one of them, a plain-clothes officer,
learned, to his amazement, that he was the man
wanted, so he gave up looking for himself and
returned to the office to report, and there the
wildly-excited lady identified him in the most
positive terms. Then an inspector told her that
the man in question had been looking for the
humble drunk and running in the disrespectful
small boy in Parramatta at the time of the
attempted crime in Perth, and talked to
her like a father generally. She had known
the policeman slightly during: her previous
residence at Parramatta, and simply fitted her
recollection of him to the event that was upper-
most in her mind, and if he had been an unknown
wanderer without a reliable alibi, her hard swearing
might easily have got him 14 years. A whole lot of
identifications have no doubt been built on the same
system. A man wants to remember the shadowy
figure that came suddenly round a corner and
“stoushed” him one dark night. He comes
across the dimly-remembered face of the man he
once saw going down a street on a runaway horse,
or shinning up a ladder at a fire, or eating peas
with a knife, or doing something else of an unusual
character —and he promptly identifies him because
there is a hazy recollection attached to his face,
and he assumes that it is the recollection that is
uppermost in his own confused intellect. If the
average man only ponders long enough over the
small, red-headed man with bow legs, who
snatched his watch in Valparaiso years ago, he is
capable of positively identifying G. R. Dibbs as
the criminal, simply because he once saw Dibbs on
a platform in a badly-lighted hall, or caught a
glimpse of him when he was in full blast in the
Savings Bank.

THE PART PRODUCER.
Critics wondered for a season
What on earth could be the reason
Of the “ honor ” paid to Jocky M‘lntyre,
What service was requited
When that gentleman got knighted !
But further it were needless to enquire.
Though not himself a native
He’s a genius creative,
And an honor to the name his offspring bear,
For, in aiding and abetting
Young Australians begetting,
He has handsomely contributed his share.
As an enterprising father
Mac. has done his duty, rather !
He was long ago a hero of renown,
And his patriotic labors
Were admitted by the neighbors
Every time a little stranger came to town.
His fame waxed more resplendent
With the birth of each descendant,
And Her Gracious kept his services in view,
Till at last friend Mac., delighted,
For his children’s sake was knighted, „
To encourage free “ production of a few.”

plaint. May you long be spared under God to

"One looks for the daily case of suioide as
naturally as for the leader!” — Daily paper,
The world just now is teeming
With folk, who, fondly dreaming
The after life
Is free from strife,
By poison, bullet, rope, or knife.
Anticipate it.
For long they deeply ponder
On what is over yonder,
Till, sorely tried,
They suicide,
And chance what’s on the other sido
Or under-rate it.
Some stay the last proceeding
For fear of not succeeding,
Because the law
Will bind them o’er
To keep the peace for evermore,
Or make them rue it 1
From those still contemplating,
Or who for means are waiting,
We’ll obviate
So sad a fate,
By briefly stating, while they wait.
The way to do it.
Now, everybody knows if
You fool with an explosive
There is a jar,
And then afar,
Upon some little twinkling star
You fall in pieces,
There’s never any warning—
No need to order mourning—
Ere you can wink,
Or even think,
You’ve reached, are on, and o’er the brink
At which life ceases.
It saves the cost of burial,
In fact, the job’s done very ill,
If folk can find
Two bits in kind,
To let your people keep in minu
You once were mortal ;
It’s better than cremating,
Or casing in lead plating,
You miss the squirms
Of sated worms,
And go direct to make your term
At heaven’s portal.
The flesh is weak—why vex it
By painful mode of exit ?
Take one good bite
Of dynamite,
And, when your things are settled quite,
Just set her going.
St. Peter’ll keep his eye on
The bits that get to Zion,
And what is “ Mowed,”
The other road
Will find, on reaching Nick’s abode,
Most careful stowing!
PONTE.
Since Melb. Argus started a “funny”
column on Saturdays, to the glory of little local
identities, its ordinary news matter is degenerat-
ing into puerile excuses for coming verses. This
sort of thing appears as intelligence from Ben-
digo: “Mr. D. B. Lazarus, M.L.A., is one of the
latest victims of cyclomania, and apparently he
has contracted the disease in its most virulent
form. In five weeks he mastered the art of
balancing, rode a few perspiring miles, and having
been laid by with plasters for several days through
a sharp encounter with some of surveyor Minto’s
road-metal, he emerged an accomplished cyclist.”
Then follows two inches of machine-made humor,
dealing with a bike journey in which Lazarus
aforesaid, “ George H. Hobson, the well-known
auctioneer,” and other historical personages dis-
tinguished themselves. Which paves the way for
a set of verses on Saturday, the burst of poetry
being based on the assumption that ‘ ‘ everybody
is talking ” about the Bendigo heroes, and expect-
ing them to be “ taken off ” in the weekly comic
column. The result can be imagined. Lazarus,
auctioneer Hobson, surveyor Minto and co.,
together with their envious personal friends, think
they have never read anything better in their
lives, and the general public says “ Damn ! ”
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr ftassmussen’s advt. on page
23 of this issue.
Prof. Halford, of Melb. University, has
carefully tested the effects of music upon Aus-
tralian snakes. He bought a mouth-organ, and
his first victim was a tiger-snake, which resisted
the attack of the deadly instrument for half-an-
hour and was ultimately abandoned as a hopeless
case. Another reptile of the same species proved
equally intractable. Then the scientist got an
unfortunate “copper-head” into his power and
blew his organ untd the snake pleaded mutely for
mercy by coiling itself upon the table and pre-
tending to be dead. This ghastly experiment was
repeated next day, with a like result.


Dear Bulletin, — Re your two recent
parsr. about the South Sea Islands Nui and
Manhiki. Your correspondent is quite right
about the praying capacities of the people
of the former island; also, in the matter of
or ncon^nence * But they don’t drink kava
there—the tree doesn’t grow in the Ellice Group.
It is an awful thing now-a-days to live on an
island such as Nui, where the native teacher bosses
the community, although Kerisome, the Samoan
teacher at Nui, is a very decent old fellow, with
some fine daughters. When the missionaries first
planted the Seed in the Ellices, there were some
curious laws made to prevent unchastity. It must
be borne in mind that what is looked upon as a
serious offence in a married woman is regarded as
mere courtesy in a spinster. Indeed, a girl is just
as proud of her performances in Cupid’s arena
before marriage as a white society belle is fond of
telling how many dances she has attended. In
Funafuti, many years ago, the following scale of
fines was drawn up by the local Samoan teacher,
and solemnly passed by the kaupule, or town
council:—
Stealing- food
Non-attendance at church
Calling out “ May your father’s throat be baked,
and dogs eat your mother’s liver ! ”
Adultery with a married woman
~ „ single „
Incontinence on the Lord’s Day
Calling out “ Go to Hell ”
Incontinence by married man with a young girl
~ by single man
Same act by boy and girl—so cocoanuts
Fishing on Sunday
Laughing on Sunday
Smoking ~ in public

This teacher waxed fat, though some of his
daughters contributed largely to the church
revenues by their disregard of their father’s moral
ordinances. One of these damsels one day was
held up to public derision through the unkindly
act of a white trader who didn’t like his reverence,
the old man. The beauteous maid was listening
to love-vows under the trader’s whaleboat, which
was turned bottom-up—the bow and stern being
placed upon two thick logs. Old Paddy T. came
quietly out and pulled the logs away. Then he
sent for the town-crier and told him to call the
people together to see the wickedness of their
pastor’s daughter. They came, the teacher lead-
ing, with a stick in his hand. Old Paddy grinned
—but felt mad when the male culprit was found
to be his own half-caste son.— Mata Pa.
The other day at Casterton, Vic., an old-
man kangaroo attacked one O’Sullivan in his own
yard, and fought several rounds with him before
it could be hauled off. When taken to the hospital
O’Sullivan needed as much stitching as a patch-
work quilt. The writer once saw a kangaroo
attack a man fishing within the enclosure in
Ballarat Botanical Gardens, and, after a brief
tussle, the man escaped with a fragment of a
singlet about his neck, wearing one sleeve of his
shirt, and dragging the mangled remains of his
trousers after him by one leg. As a stripper the
kangaroo, in his excitable moments, has no living
equal.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems. #
Dear Bulletin, —Re snakes swallowing
their young to protect them from impending
danger, it doesn’t matter a rap what Professor
This or Dr. That thinks or believes on this sub-
ject, as the simple fact remains that I, in Peb.,
’BB, at Coogee, saw a female black snake (Pseu-
dechis porphyriacus) swallow her young, killed
her, and actually took the young from her. This
little tinpot affectation of unbelief of facts proved
by an amateur naturalist to whom the great
majority of professors are indebted for corrections
and hints seems to me wofully thin and flat. I say
that certain genera of Australian snakes do swallow
their young as a means of temporary protection,
and my mere ipse dixit on this subject will be
accepted from one end of Australia to the other,
notwithstanding the irresponsible and unqualified
doubts of 10,000 professors.—l am, &c., H. J.
M'Cooey.
