The Anatomy of Starvation.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There are three stages of starvation.
They must be endured to "be appreciated; they
could not be described. See ! if a man had no
eyes he could not appreciate vision. There is first
the great physiological stage : some scientist wrote
an octavo on the cat, he could write a library on
this. Those digestive juices (the saliva and the
pancreatic) crying out for work—something to act
on ; and the hard, hollow stomach. Mere physical
agony : the sustenanceless muscles and the starved
brain-cells.
I breakfasted on bread and water this morning
—I used to have warm boiled milk, but the milk-
man has taken his cue from the butcher and
stopped supplies. N.S.F.
God bless the little baker —he always stops last !
His name shall not be on the black-list at the
doom. It is coming.
As I walked home I endured that first stage :
the smell of the cheap eating-house against which
my nose once so vigorously used to revolt, was
now like the aroma of the ambrosial food of the
gods—aye, like the faint, sweet scent of hasheesh
(not hash-house). But the stale-cigar smell of the
gin-palace disgusted me. Four well-dressed, flash
snobs, half-tipsy, rolled 'out of one, and in his
drunken stupidity the drunkest bumped me. I
cursed him with a feeble curse, but I was weak.
* * * »
Then comes the awful psychological stage—
the broken spirit, the desperate mood. As I neared
home (ah ! it was one once ) a hungry phantom
preceded me. No, ’tis my weak retina —uncaused
by material objects. One gaunt spectre and three
little ones. I shall see them after I pass one, two,
three streets. Ah ! they shall have bread to-night
—but no milk. I shall be an anarchist. Never
in the history of the world was bomb thrown yet
that concessions did not follow ! That is the great
central truth. What daring genius with despera-
tion screwed to martyr-pitch first devised the great
idea? I have suffered in dumb, uncomplaining
stupidity—like the brainless cattle in a snow-
storm. If I take my life in my hand with the
bomb hundreds will be benefited. A man in some
old-world-part offered himself for vivisection if
the vivisectors would settle an annuity on his
wife and three children. He was a poor logician
—not an altruist either —a selfish hound.
Now the climax —the last street is passed. Not
a word from Annie or the elder children, but the
eyes of the youngest ask in an unmistakeable way
if there is the long-awaited news —“work”?
“ warm food ” ? Oh, the brutal No ! A real
downright good cry would do me good —but I am
too weak.
* * » *
And now the transcendental stage—everything
is shadow. My wife, the three little ones, this
foodlessness, are but dreams. Through my glisten-
ing eyeballs I see visions like that view of the
“ Eternal City ” which the old-time dreamer had
from the crest of the “Delectable Mountains.”
There is no real; everything is the puppet of the
senses. That is the greatest Truth. This stage
shall have a climax. “ Perchance to dream.”
Aye, and there shall be an awakening to which
the drunkard’s is but as an atom to the universe.
The haggard gaunt spectre and the three little
ones will become real again. Let your legislators
beware !
Sydney, 15/11/95.
O. HYLLIER.
D. T.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. Murphy, licensed victualler, had drawn a
minor prize in a Tatt. sweep, and he had been
“on a fair bend ” for a week. The grog had got
on to his nerves, and there was a hand of iron
gripping the back of his neck. The doctor said
that it was “ cerebral congestion,” and that if Mr.
Murphy didn’t put the peg in there would be
trouble.
The publican, who had lain awake for nights,
went upstairs for an afternoon nap. He slept and
dreamed that a little red-bearded man entered the
apartment. He was attired in blue, and nodded
his head threateningly at the prone form of the
dreamer. “By Heavens ! ” said the azure appari-
tion, “ I’m going to give you a of a time !”
The sleeping publican went off into a cold sweat,
but he asked as truculently as he could, “ Who
the are you, anyhow ? ” “ Oh,” said the man
in blue, with a demoniac grin, “I’m one of the
blue devils, whe-e-e-e-e ! ”
The derisive laugh of the demon merged into
the 5 o’clock whistle at the foundry. The publican
awoke, looked fearfully round, and remarked with
fervent emphasis, “Thank God!” Then he
descended to the bar to scoop in the threepenny
pieces of the homeward-bound working-man.
He polished up a few glasses, yawned, gave a
bottle or two a * perfunctory push ; then a cus-
tomer entered, “ Pint o’ beer, please.”
The publican’s hand was on the beer-engine
when he glanced sharply at the customer. He
was red-bearded, and wore a blue serge suit and a
blue apron.
The customer’s lips moved—possibly uttering
some commonplace—but the publican could have
sworn his solemnest oath that he said, “ I’m going
to give you a h of a time !”
“The identical beggar who came into my
room,” mused the licensed victualler, as with
insane cunning he sneaked towards the free lunch
where a much-ground bread-knife lay temptingly.
He grasped the knife with a splendidly-acted
manifestation of unconcern ; then, fixing his
eye on a clear portion of the bar, he yelled to the
astonished customer, “ Give me a h of a time,
will you ? ” and vaulted the counter with the mur-
derous weapon in his hand.
The customer bolted up the street, Murphy
after him.
The customer was fleet of foot, otherwise it
would have been a case of murder and suicide ; as
it was, it was only suicide —“ suicide while under
the influence of temporary insanity.”
P.
Two trappers in the Bright district, Vic.,
have in two years sent by rail to Melb. market
54,000 rabbits, to Euroa Preserving Factory 16,000.
and skinned 5,000. That is, they have caught in
all 75,000 rabbits in twenty-four months.
The membrane of a hen’s egg has been
found the best medium for skin-grafting purposes
yet devised —far better than either human or
beast skin. The egg should be very fresh, and
the thin lining membrane of the shell carefully
extracted without yolk matter. Strips of mem-
brane should then be laid on the wound or granu-
lating surface with the inner or albuminous side
down. Egg-membrane is especially useful for
large and otherwise slow-healing burns on the
arm, foot, leg and body.
Two Men and a Woman.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The second-mate of a small craft isn’t the
most highly-paid man afloat, but Ted, of the
Spray, was a steady fellow, and most of his wages
went straight to the hands of his adored and
adoring wife. Jennie was a grand housekeeper
and fond of Ted; so that the little cottage in
Yarradale was always a bright port and a snug
home-nest.
It was a bitter blow to Jennie when the Spray
was lost off the Chilian coast and Ted’s name was
not among the saved. It was hard, too, to sell up
the little home, take to one rented room, and earn
a pittance by needlework from the “ sweaters.”
No wonder that she presently listened to Flash
Jim, who drove a ’bus, and became his wife.
* * * *
In that part of Melbourne the sound of a
woman’s screams, one evening, caused no excite-
ment.
“Only Flash Jim hammering his old woman ! ”
said the casual hearer.
Inside, the sobs were translatable—
“ Don’t, Jim. You’ll kill me ! ”
“Then less mag, carn’t yer. I’ll teach yer to
have no tucker ready when I come home, blarst
yer! ”
A knock.
“ Come in, you, whoever y’are.”
A seafaring man entered and looked excitedly
around.
“ Well, wot d’yer want ? ”
But Jennie only screamed—“ Ted ! ”
“Why, Jennie,” said the new-comer, “what’s
this? ”
“ You, Ted ? You — alive!”
“ Yes ; didn’t you get my letter ? Why did you
leave the cottage ? ”
“ I—l thought you were dead, and I’m—
I’m ”
“ I understand, and so you—married again? ”
“Yes, wuss luck ! ” chipped in Jim ; “ blarst ’er
for a ! ”
Ted t x>k no notice of the interruption.
“ And this brute has ill-used you, Jennie ? ”
The woman looked from one man to the other,
while her face gave varying expression to emotions
that could not be identified.
A pause.
“ Come home with me, Jennie, and forget this
“What, Ted? You’ll take me back? . . .
Do you hear, Jim ? ”
“ Oh, go to , if you want to,” said Jim,
with a coarse laugh.
Another pause.
“I’m waiting, Jennie,” said Ted.
“ Jim ! ” fairly screamed Jennie, “ ain’t you
master here ? ”
“ My oath ! ”
“ Then put that fellow out! ”
ISHMAEL.
A Maoriland preacher, the Rev. Yorke,
of Masterton, on a recent Sunday : “ Suppose
you were to go home to-day and find the Lord in
your house sitting by the fire, how would you
receive him ? ” Then the parishioners tittered. It
was 85° in the shade that day, and the idea of
going home and finding anybody sitting by the
fire made them suspect the rev. gentleman’s
sanity.
A Kodak.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Women generally are deficient in the sense of
humor. But there are three old ladies who are
perfectly full of it—humor of the primmest and
most sardonic kind. These three particular ladies
arc the trio that, with spindle, distaff, and shears,
weave, trim, and shape for us the garment of
Destiny. And whether we come in out of the
wet and secure a cosy place by the fire, or whether
we are left to shiver miserably on the kerbstone,
depends altogether on the texture, shape, and fit
of that garment.
It was in the men’s baths, and I was looking at
two young fellows who had just come out of the
water, and were drying themselves in the sun.
One, a weak-limbed, spavined, and altogether
“ in-bred ” looking youth, with thin, wispy hair,
and no chest, chin, or forehead worth speaking of.
But the other —well, as he stood there m the sun-
light, erect, and with feet slightly apart, he might
have posed as the model for the statue of Apollo
that stood upon Mount Actium. Clean-cut and
muscular as a racehorse in training, his crisp-
haired, well-shaped head was set on a pair of
shoulders fit for a fighting-man.
When, after retiring to their bunks, they came
forth clothed again, my little friend looked quite
presentable in his fashionably-cut flannels and
bright straw hat. He is young Scourings—son of old
Scourings, of Slop-pot Plains. He will come into
several thousands a year, and may have his pick
of our marriageable virgins. Apollo, in his work-
a-day clothes, proves to be the first lieutenant of
a tramcar. He probably won’t think about mar-
riage for some years yet.
Old Slunkey’s daughter is a girl that all our
young men are most' attentive to. She is a flat-
chested, narrow-brained little thing, with bony
arms and shoulders, and just a suspicion of frosti-
ness about the nose that no amount of pearl-
powder seems to permanently overlay. But old
Slunkey is said to be very solid on the question of
settlements, and I am told that she has refused
(among others) the inheritor of one of England’s
oldest historic titles.
I was passing Slunkey’s mansion, the other
morning, and saw a vision of loveliness on the
doorstep. It was “ the slavey ” —ye gods ! —what
a woman ! A pair of frank blue eyes and a sweet
face set in a halo of pure golden hair, the outlines
of a noble figure traceable beneath the thin print
gown. Here was a woman that, in old barbaric
days, men would have fought to win a smile from.
A woman to be clothed in costliest silks, lapped in
soft luxury, and hung with rarest jewels. A
woman fit to be the mother of heroes
and she was bashing the dust out of a doormat !!
Certainly the Fates are three queer old ladies.
Melb.
J. H. WAGNER.
At Gopeoi, an island not recruited for years
(says Cairns Argus), four natives swam off to the
Fearless, and asked to be recruited. They
wanted to go to Cairns “to good fellow master,
Mr. Swallow.” According to the Argus, they
were remarkably handsome boys, light chocolate
in color, with beautiful eyes and teeth.” Note
the Cairns ideal of beauty : “light chocolate in
color.” The Bulletin quite believes the Poly-
nesians are anxious to go to Cairns—they feel
quite at home there. It’s only the European who
feels out of place and like a foreigner in that sultry
nigger-and-Chow-ridden locality.

The Elopement of Mrs. Peters.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Simon Peters, irreverently called “The Apostle,”
returned to the railway camp late on Sunday
night, and found his tent topsy-turvy, and “his
missus ” gone. On the paling table, weighted
with a piece of cheese, was a scrap of sugar-paper
on which was written in Fan’s dog-leg hand
“ I’m sik. I’m goin’to cleer.”
Sim. swore a muffled oath under his abundant
moustache, and looked around upon the unwonted
disorder. The blue blanket and the rug had been
stripped from their bunk,; the spare, rough furni-
ture of the big tent lay about in confusion; and
amongst the grey ashes in the wide sod fireplace
was a bunch of reddish hair. Peters fished this
out, and examined it with as much astonishment
as the slow-going, even-tempered navvy was
capable of feeling. It was his wife’s hair, and had
evidently been hacked off in a hurry, regardless of
effect. Piled on the bush stool against the wall
were Mrs. Peters’s clothes. Nothing of hers that
Peters could recall was missing; even the big
quondong earrings, of which she was so proud,
were thrown upon the floor. Her hat was on the
bed, and her boots were under the table.
Still clutching the mop of hair in his hand, Sim.
backed solemnly and soberly on to a seat, and sat
for a few minutes gravely weighing the evidence.
Obviously, Fanny had gone off clad only in a blue
blanket or a ’possum rug. This was most extra-
ordinary, even for Fanny, but there was some
satisfaction in it, since it should not be difficult to
trace a white woman so attired.
Presently Peters arose and went forth to prose-
cute enquiries. On Saturday, before departing
for Dunolly, he had asked Rolley’s wife to keep
an eye on the missus. As he approached the
gaffer’s tent, however, he heard a woman’s voice
raised in shrill vituperation, and recognised Mrs.
Rolley’s strident contralto.
“ My poor mother that’s in Heaven knew you,
you . She always said you was a
And poor Rolley was inundated with a torrent
of his own choice blasphemies. Simon Peters
knew by experience that when Mrs. Rolley dragged
her sainted mother into little domestic dif-
ferences, she was at least two days gone in drink,
and quite incapable of recollecting anything
beyond Rolley’s shortcomings ; so he turned away
with a sigh, and carried his quest into the camp.
Half-an-hour later he returned to his tent, and
resumed his thoughtful attitude on the stool. He
had secured one piece of evidence that seemed to
throw a good deal of light on the situation. Late
on Saturday night someone had broken into Curly
Hunter’s tent, and stolen therefrom a grey tweed
suit, a black felt hat, and a pair of light blucher
boots. Peters, putting this and that together
slowly, and with great mental effort, concluded
that Curly Hunter and Fanny were about the
same height. He recollected, too, the explanation
his wife offered when he discovered her back to be
seamed and lined with scars.
“Dad done it,” said Fanny. “Poor old dad,
he was always lickin’ me.”
“ But,” gasped Peters, filled with a sudden itch
to be at the throat of his deceased father-in-law,
“ you don't mean to say the cowardly brute lashed
you like that! ”
“Didn’t he?” replied she, laughing lightly.
“He used to rope me up on the cow-bail, an’
hammer me with a horsewhip. Once when I set
the grass on fire, an’ burned the stable an’ the
dairy ; another time when I broke Grasshopper’s
neck, ridin’ him over Coleman’s chock-an’-log
fence; an’ agen when I dressed up in Tom’s
clothes, took a swag, and got a job pickin’-up in
M'Kinley’s shed.”
Early on Monday morning Peters had an inter-
view with Curly Hunter. Hunter was sympathe-
tic, and readily sold Sim. the stolen things at a
modest valuation, promising at the same time to
observe a friendly reticence in the matter ; but, for
all that, two hours later everybody in the camp
knew that Mrs. Peters had run off, and that
“The Apostle” was away hunting for her. The
general opinion, freely and profanely expressed,
was that Simon Peters was a superlative idiot. It
was agreed that Peters would have exhibited
common sense by sitting still under the bereave-
ment and casually thanking Providence for the
“let off.” Since Mrs. Peters started a couple of
ramshackle waggons down the gradient, and nearly
smashed up Ryan’s gang, the camp had suddenly
grown weary of her “ monkey tricks.”
Mrs. Simon Peters was a woman of twenty-
six, ten or twelve years younger than her
husband, more comely, more decent, and
more presentable in every way than the
other wives of the camp. She did not
get drunk in the bedroom end of Wingy Lee’s
shanty on pay nights, did not use the picturesque
idiom of the gangers in ordinary conversation,
and in some respects had been a good mate to
Peters. But it must be admitted that the camp
had further justification in doubting the complete
sanity of Simon Peters’ wife. She had an eerie
expression that was quaintly accented by keen,
twinkling, black eyes in combination with light
red hair and rather pale brows; and she was
possessed of a spirit of mischief that led her into
the wildest extravagances. Her devilment was
that of an ungovernable school-boy, without his
preposterous sense of humor. An uncontrollable
yearningforexcitementimpelled her to the strangest
actions. She had another peculiar characteristic,
one unknown to the camp, in her apparent insensibi-
lity to physical pain. Peters had been astounded
by the fact that a burn, a cut, a scald, or a blow pro-
voked no complainings from his wife, and scarcely
any regard. This indifference extended to the
sufferings of others. After the blasting accident in
North cutting, Fanny, of all the women in the
camp, was the only one who had the nerve to
approach the mangled body of poor MTntyre, and
she placidly worked over the shocking mass, still
instinct with life, whilst the strongest men turned
sick at the sight of it.
Sim. made no effort to understand his wife,
which was well, as he was only an average man,
and she was past finding out He concluded that
her extraordinary conduct was just the natural
unreasonableness and contrariness of women
“coming out strong,” and made the best of the
situation in which he found himself. Being an
average man, Sim. was a superior navvy ; he only
got drunk on big occasions, and, drunk or sober,
treated his wife with indulgent fondness, and
occasionally Fanny seemed fond of him in return ;
but then she had been very warmly attached to
that father who used to bail her up in the cowshed
and lash her with a horsewhip in the hope of con-
verting her to sweet reasonableness.
On the Monday morning Peters first went up
the road, seeking his wife, but no one at White’s
had seen a slim young fellow in a grey suit pass
that way, so he tried down the road, with better
success. Clark, at the Travellers’ Rest, had seen
“just sich a feller ” as Sim. described.
“ They had a drink here Sunday, an’ left,
making for Molagul, it seemed t’me,” said Clark.
“ They ? ” queried Peters.
“Yes. There was two of ’em. The big feller
shouted. A brown-faced chap, with a black
moustache, an’ a deep cut in his chin, here.”
Simon’s grip made a dent in the pewter he held,
and a grey hue crept over his cheeks and into his
lip»s. Never before had he doubted his wife in
this way ; never through all her mad escapades
had he had reason to question her fealty as a wife
till now. Peters remembered the man distinctly ;
he had seen him about the camp, looking for
work. The peculiar cleft chin would serve to
identify him amongst ten thousand. Striding
along the road the fugitives had taken, the navvy
recollected hearing Fanny speaking enthusiasti-
cally of the tall, brown stranger as a fine man,
and the grey in his cheek deepened to the color of
ashes, and his jaw hardened meaningly. His
quest had suddenly assumed a terrible significance,
and that fierce pallor and grim rigidity of the
jaw never left him until its end.
Peters heard of them again in the afternoon,
but got off the trail towards evening, and it was
not till late on the following day that he picked
up the scent. Then he talked with a farmer who
had seen them.
“ They slep’ in an old hut up in my grass
paddock las’ night,” said the man, “ an’went up
the road at about seven this mornin’.”
“ Did both men sleep in the hut ? ” asked Peters.
“ To be sure ! ”
Sim. continued his journey, steadily, and with
apparent unconcern, but cherishing an immovable
determination to kill the brown-faced man the
moment they met.
Early on the Wednesday morning Peters came
up with the runaway. An old man watering a
horse at a small creek told him in answer to his
inquiries :
“ A tall chap, with a divided chin—name of
Sandler, ain’t it? He’s here. I let him a bit
of ringin’. That’s his axe you hear up the
paddock.”
Following the ring of the axe, Peters soon came
upon his man. Sandler stopped working as he
approached, and turned towards him, resting on
the handle of his axe. Sim. walked to withm a
couple of yards of the stranger, and threw off the
light swag he carried.
“You infernal hound ! ” he said ; “ where is my
wife ? ”
Sandler started up in extreme amazement.
“Keep off!” he cried. “What the devil do I
know about your wife ? ”
Peters rushed at him with the fury of a brute, and
the two men exchanged heavy blows. Then they
closed, and wrestled for a moment, but Simon’s
rage lent him a strength that was irresistible, and
presently the other man was sent down with
stunning force. As he attempted to rise, shaken
and almost breathless, Peters, who had seized the
axe, struck him once with the head of it, and
Sandler fell back again and lay perfectly still,
with a long, gaping wound over his left eye from
which the blood poured through his hair upon the
new chips and the yellow grass.
When Peters looked up his wife stood facing
him. She wore blucher boots, a pair of grey
trousers, and a man’s shirt, and carried an axe.
She gazed composedly at the fallen man.
“What have you done, Sim. ?” she asked.
“You ran away with that man ! ” He pointed
at Sandler.
She nodded her head.
“ He didn’t know I was a woman,” she said.
EDWARD DYSON.
Two Devils.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Of all predisposing causes, undue indulgence in in-
toxicating liquor is the most common and the most
dangerous.”— Sunstroke instructions of N.S.W. Board of
Health.
It’s dreadful news that everywhere is flash’d along
the line—
The devil’s in the whisky, and the devil s m the
wine,
And who so fain would drink again, must now
the cup decline !
Lo ! man and beast drop down, drop down, in
field and farm and street;
The mercury’s nigh boiling, and the records all
are beat;
It’s plain to see, you must agree, the devil’s in the
heat!
Here’s a dilemma (like “ To be, or not to be ”) to
vex ’ee :
You either drink, as Christians should, and die of
apoplexy,
Or, if you’re dry, you must not die a parch’d and
thirsty wreck. See ?
And east and west and north and south, through-
out the land accurst,
Are some who die of sunstroke, and some who
die of thirst.
I’d like to know, before I go, which death would
be the worst.
It’s easy talking temperance —you sitting there on
ice;
But, if the sun’s not temperate, it’s mighty cool
advice.
You ought to own the frigid zone —or preach in
Paradise.
Old Sol has had a proper spree, and made the
liquor fly—
Popp’d half the champagne corks in town, and
drunk the country dry ;
And what the sun has gone and done —why
shouldn’t you and I ?
But tho’ the sun exceeds himself all day—the
thirsty brute—-
Drinking the sap from grain and grass, the juice
from vine and fruit—
The festive cuss must punish us because we follow
suit!
The devil’s in the bottle, and the devil’s in the
drought,
And which the worser devil is, is very much in
doubt;
So let us drain the glass again, and drown both
devils out!

Suits? Well, order one. Send your size along,
